The 24 Productive Pairings: Variations and Examples
Author presents two example pairings from the 24 possible ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the factors that produce the 24 pairings and generate the conclusion for a given pairing using the author's examples.

Core Idea
The 24 pairings arise from variations in quantity, quality, and antecedent affirmation/negation of the premises.

In the previous lesson, we learned the structure and conditions of the compound syllogism. Now we explore the three factors that generate 24 valid pairings: the quantity and quality of the conditional and disjunctive premises, and whether the antecedent is affirmed or negated. The author provides two concrete examples to illustrate how the conclusion is formed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three factors
The text lists three variables: (1) universality/particularity of the two premises (kulliyya/juz'iyya), (2) affirmation/negation of the premises (ijab/salb), (3) affirmation/negation of the antecedent (ijab al-muqaddam wa salbuhu). These combine to give 24 productive pairings.
The calculation: For each premise, 2 (quantity) × 2 (quality) = 4 combinations; for two premises, 4×4=16, times 2 for antecedent affirmation/negation gives 32 total, but only 24 are productive (some combinations yield no conclusion).
2
Examine the first example pairing
The author gives: 'Everything that is every B is C, then D is R' (universal affirmative conditional) and 'Always every C is either H or A' (universal affirmative disjunctive). The conclusion is 'It may be that when D is R, then every B is always either H or A.' Note the result is particular ('may be') despite universal premises.
This is pairing 1: conditional (universal affirmative) + disjunctive (universal affirmative) + antecedent affirmed.
3
Examine the second example pairing
The second pairing keeps the same conditional but changes the disjunctive to universal negative: 'Always every C is not either H or A.' The conclusion becomes 'It may be that when D is R, then absolutely not every R is either H or A.' The consequent changes to negate the disjunctive.
This is pairing 2: universal affirmative conditional + universal negative disjunctive + antecedent affirmed.
4
Generalize to other pairings
The remaining 22 pairings can be derived by varying the factors: making premises particular, negating the conditional, or negating the antecedent. Each yields a distinct conclusion form. The author leaves the rest to deduction.
As an exercise, try one variation: universal negative conditional + universal affirmative disjunctive + antecedent negated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاشتراك بجزء المقدم فإما بمحموله أو بموضوعه، فإن كان بمحموله فإما أن يكون موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال أو محمولاً فيها، فإن كان موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال فهو على هيئة الشكل الأول، فشرطه إيجاب المقدم من المتصلة إن كانت موجبة وسلبه إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون المتصلة كلية إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كلية - أي الحكم بها على كل الموضوع - وقرائنه المنتجة باعتبار كليّة المقدمتين وجزئيتهما، وسلبهما وإيجابهما، وإيجاب المقدم وسالبه أربعة وعشرون ،اقتراناً، وخاصته أن النتيجة فيه تكون متصلة مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران المنفصلة بالمقدم ، وأنها قد تكون جزئية، وإن كانت المقدمات كليّة
الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة متصلة مقدمها كلي موجب، وكلية موجبة منفصلة كليّة الموضوع كقولنا: (كل) ما كان كل "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") و(دائماً كل "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ")
الاقتران الثاني: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كلية سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً كل "ج" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فليس البتة إما كل "ر" "هـ" وإما "أ").
And if the commonality is in a part of the antecedent, then it is either in its predicate or its subject. If it is in its predicate, then it is either a subject for the parts of the disjunction or a predicate in them. If it is a subject for the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the first figure. Its condition is the affirmation of the antecedent of the conditional if it is affirmative, and its negation if it is negative; and that the conditional be universal if it is negative; and that the parts of the disjunction be universal—that is, the judgment about them applies to every subject. Its productive couplings, considering the universality and particularity of the two premises, their negation and affirmation, and the affirmation and negation of the antecedent, are twenty-four pairings. Its characteristic is that the result in it is a conditional whose antecedent is the consequent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of coupling the disjunctive with the antecedent; and that it may be particular even if the premises are universal. The first pairing from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal affirmative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive with universal subject, like our saying: 'Everything that is every B is C, then D is R' and 'Always every C is either H or A', and its result: 'It may be that when D is R, then every B is always either H or A.' The second pairing: the conditional remains as it is, but the disjunctive is universal negative, like our saying: 'And always every C is not either H or A', then '(it may be that when D is R, then absolutely not every R is either H or A).'
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where the commonality (al-ishtirāk) between the conditional and disjunctive premises lies in a part of the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional. The common part can be the predicate or subject of the antecedent. If it is the predicate, it can be subject or predicate in the disjunctive parts. When it is subject in the disjunctive, the syllogism takes the form of the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal). Conditions are given: the antecedent of the conditional must be affirmed if the conditional is affirmative, and negated if it is negative; if the conditional is negative, it must be universal; and the parts of the disjunction must be universal (the judgment applies to every subject). Considering all variations of quantity and quality of the premises, there are 24 productive pairings. The result is always a conditional whose antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional, and whose consequent is the result of applying the disjunctive to the antecedent. Notably, the result may be particular even if all premises are universal. The author provides two example pairings: the first with universal affirmative premises, the second with a universal negative disjunctive.

Apply the rules to produce the conclusion for a pairing where the conditional is universal negative and the disjunctive is universal affirmative, with the antecedent negated.

First Two Mixed Conditional-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Defines first two conjunctions of conditional and disjunctive premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first two forms of mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogisms and validate their conclusions using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogisms yield conditional conclusions, not disjunctive ones.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq introduces the first two forms (iqtiranat) of compound syllogisms combining a conditional premise (muttasila) with a disjunctive premise (munfasila). Understanding these forms is crucial for constructing valid compound arguments in Islamic logic, especially in theological proofs where multiple premises are combined. We will parse the structure of each form and see how the conclusion follows from the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises of the first iqtiran
The first form uses a universal affirmative conditional (e.g., 'If B is C then D is R') and a universal affirmative disjunctive (e.g., 'Always either B is C or H is A'). Recognize that both premises are universal (kulliyya) and affirmative (mujiba).
The conditional is the major premise; the disjunctive is the minor.
2
Derive the conclusion of the first iqtiran
The conclusion is a conditional: 'It may be that if D is R, then H is not A'. This follows because from the disjunction, if B is C then H is not A (since the disjunction is exclusive in the sense of opposition), and the conditional links B is C to D is R.
The conclusion is not certain but possible (قد يكون).
3
Identify the premises of the second iqtiran
The same conditional as before, but the disjunctive is now universal negative: 'It is absolutely not the case that either B is C or H is A'. This means the disjuncts are never both true and never both false? Actually it denies any opposition.
Universal negative disjunctive negates the disjunction itself.
4
Derive the conclusion of the second iqtiran
The conclusion is a negative conditional: 'Not always either D is R or H is A'. The reasoning: if D is R, then B may be C (from the conditional), and if H were opposite to D, it would be opposite to B, contradicting the disjunctive denial of opposition.
The conclusion is also conditional, but negated.
5
Generalize to particular conditional versions
If the conditional is particular affirmative instead of universal, two analogous forms arise, following the same pattern. The author leaves these as an exercise for the reader.
Particularity changes the quantifier but not the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، كقولنا : كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر" و(دائماً وإما "ب" "ج" وإما "هـ" "أ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فلا يكون "هـ" "أ").
الاقتران الثاني: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كلية سالبة، كقولنا: (وليس البتة إما "ب" "ج" وإما "هـ" "أ") ونتيجته ليس دائماً إما "د" "ر" وإما "هـ" "أ") لأنه إذا كان ("د" "ر") فـ (قد يكون "ب" "ج") فلو كان ( "هـ" "أ") معانداً لـ ("د" "ر") لكان معانداً لـ "ب" "ج") وقد قيل لا عناد.
وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانان آخران على نسق هذين الاقترانين فاعتبرها
Completion of the first art: on defining forms of conjunctive syllogisms.
The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives, as we say: 'Everything that is B is C, then D is R' and 'Always and either B is C or H is A' then 'It may be that if D is R, then H is not A'.
The second conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is universal negative, as we say: 'It is absolutely not the case that either B is C or H is A', and its conclusion is 'Not always either D is R or H is A' because if D is R, then it may be that B is C; so if H is A were opposite to D is R, it would be opposite to B is C, and it has been said there is no opposition.
And if the conditional is particular affirmative, then two other conjunctions can be composed from it with the disjunctive, following the pattern of these two conjunctions, so consider them.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage defines the first two forms (iqtiranat) of mixed syllogisms that combine a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) premise. The author begins by stating that this is a continuation of the first art on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The first iqtiran uses two universal affirmative premises: a conditional 'Whenever B is C, D is R' and a disjunctive 'Always either B is C or H is A'. The conclusion is a conditional: 'It may be that if D is R, then H is not A'. This means that from the disjunction and the conditional link, we infer that when D holds, the opposite of H holds. The second iqtiran keeps the same conditional but uses a universal negative disjunctive 'It is absolutely not the case that either B is C or H is A'. Its conclusion is also a conditional but negative: 'Not always either D is R or H is A'. The author explains the reasoning: if D is R, then B might be C (from the conditional), and if H were opposite to D, it would also be opposite to B, but the disjunctive denies any opposition, so the conclusion is not always a disjunction. Finally, the author notes that if the conditional is particular affirmative instead of universal, two analogous iqtiranat can be derived, and leaves it to the reader to infer them.

Checkpoint
In the first iqtiran, if the conditional is 'Every intoxicant (khamr) is forbidden (haram)' and the disjunctive is 'Always either a drink is intoxicant or it is not intoxicant', what is the conclusion?
A) It may be that if something is forbidden, then it is not an intoxicant.
B) It may be that if something is intoxicant, then it is forbidden.
C) It may be that if something is forbidden, then it is not a drink.
D) It may be that if something is a drink, then it is forbidden.

Key Takeaway
Mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogisms produce conditional conclusions, and the quantifiers (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) of the premises determine the form of the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the first iqtiran form, construct a valid mixed syllogism on the topic of khamr (intoxicants): Conditional: 'If a drink is intoxicating (B=C), then it is forbidden (D=R).' Disjunctive: 'Always either a drink is intoxicating (B=C) or it is permissible (H=A).' What is the conclusion? Write it as a conditional in English.
?
For the second iqtiran, change the disjunctive to 'Absolutely never is a drink intoxicating or permissible.' What conclusion follows? Explain why the conclusion is a negative conditional.

Structure of Mixed Conditional-Disjunctive Syllogisms with Shared Antecedent Part
Defines conditions and enumerates eight conjunctions when the shared element is part of the antecedent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogism when the shared part is the subject of the disjunctive's parts, and construct the general form of the conclusion.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms with a shared antecedent part mirror the first figure—when the shared term is the subject of the disjunctive.

In our study of mixed syllogisms, we now examine cases where the conditional and disjunctive share a part of the antecedent. The author lays out strict conditions and shows that the conclusion is always a conditional with a specific structure. Understanding these conditions is essential for evaluating complex arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh, where conditional reasoning abounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared part
The author first distinguishes whether the shared part is the subject or predicate of the antecedent. Here we focus on when it is the subject of the disjunctive's parts.
The shared part is the middle term linking the two premises.
2
Recognize the first-figure analogy
When the shared term is the subject of the disjunctive's parts, the syllogism's structure parallels the first figure of categorical syllogisms.
3
List the conditions
The antecedent of the conditional must have the same quality (affirmative/negative) as the conditional itself. If the conditional is negative, it must be universal. The parts of the disjunction must be universal (i.e., the judgment applies to all subjects).
These conditions ensure validity.
4
Describe the conclusion form
The conclusion is a conditional proposition. Its antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional (e.g., 'if D is R'), and its consequent is the result of combining the disjunctive with the antecedent of the conditional.
The conclusion may be particular even if the premises are universal.
5
Note the number of conjunctions
There are twenty-four possible conjunctions based on quantity and quality variations, but only a subset is detailed in the text.
This establishes the systematic nature of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاشتراك بجزء المقدم فإما بمحموله أو بموضوعه، فإن كان بمحموله فإما أن يكون موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال أو محمولاً فيها، فإن كان موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال فهو على هيئة الشكل الأول، فشرطه إيجاب المقدم من المتصلة إن كانت موجبة وسلبه إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون المتصلة كلية إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كلية - أي الحكم بها على كل الموضوع - وقرائنه المنتجة باعتبار كليّة المقدمتين وجزئيتهما، وسلبهما وإيجابهما، وإيجاب المقدم وسالبه أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً، وخاصته أن النتيجة فيه تكون متصلة مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران المنفصلة بالمقدم ، وأنها قد تكون جزئية، وإن كانت المقدمات كليّة
الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة متصلة مقدمها كلي موجب، وكلية موجبة منفصلة كليّة الموضوع كقولنا: (كل) ما كان كل "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") و(دائماً كل "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ" ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ")
الاقتران الثاني: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كلية سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً كل "ج" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فليس البتة إما كل "ر" "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران الثالث المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية موجبة كقولنا: (وقد يكون كل "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران الرابع: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: (وليس دائماً كل "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته كالثاني.
الاقتران الخامس من كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها جزئي موجب، وكلية موجبة منفصلة كقولنا (كل ما كان بعض "ب" "ج" "ر") و(دائماً إما كل "ج" "أ" وإما كل "ج" "هـ") ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران السادس : المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كلية سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً ليس إما كل "ج" "أ" وإما "هـ" ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" دائماً ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران السابع المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته كالخامس.
الاقتران الثامن: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، ونتيجته كالسادس.
الاقتران التاسع من كلية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها جزئي سالب، وكلية موجبة منفصلة، كقولنا: ليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ب" ليس "ج" فـ "د" "ر") وإما كل "ج" "أ" وإما "هـ") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، وهي كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ") لأنك إذا عكست المتصلة كان عكسها ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" ف (بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وهي تصدق عند القلب إلى الإيجاب، كما عرف كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" "ج" وكان (كل "ج" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فبين أنه كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران العاشر : المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كليّة سالبة، وهي كقولنا (ودائماً كل "ج" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" دائماً ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
And if the sharing is by a part of the antecedent, then either by its predicate or its subject. If by its predicate, then either it is subject of the parts of the disjunction or predicate in them. If it is subject of the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the first figure. Its condition is that the antecedent of the conditional be affirmative if it is affirmative and negative if it is negative, and that the conditional be universal if it is negative, and that the parts of the disjunction be universal – i.e., the judgment about them applies to all subjects. Its productive conjunctions, considering the universality and particularity, and negation and affirmation of the two premises, and the affirmation and negation of the antecedent, are twenty-four conjunctions. Its property is that the conclusion in it is a conditional, its antecedent is the consequent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of the conjunction of the disjunctive with the antecedent, and that it may be particular even if the premises are universal.
The first conjunction from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal affirmative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive universal in subject, as we say: 'Everything that is all B is C, then D is R' and 'Always all C is either H or A', and its conclusion: 'It may be that if D is R, then all B is always either H or A'.
The second conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is universal negative, as we say: 'And always all C is not either H or A', then 'It may be that if D is R, then absolutely not either all R is H or A'.
The third conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is particular affirmative, as we say: 'And it may be that all C is either H or A', and its conclusion: 'It may be that if D is R, then all B is either H or A'.
The fourth conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is particular negative, as we say: 'And not always all C is either H or A', and its conclusion like the second.
The fifth conjunction from a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is particular affirmative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive, as we say: 'Everything that is some B is C is R' and 'Always either all C is A or all C is H', and its conclusion: 'It may be that if D is R, then some B is always either H or A'.
The sixth conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is universal negative, as we say: 'And always it is not either all C is A or H', and its conclusion: 'It may be that if D is R, then some B is always not either H or A'.
The seventh conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is particular affirmative, and its conclusion like the fifth.
The eighth conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is particular negative, and its conclusion like the sixth.
The ninth conjunction from a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is particular negative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive, as we say: 'It is absolutely not the case that if some B is not C, then D is R' and 'Either all C is A or H', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Everything that is D is R, then all B is always either H or A' because if you convert the conditional, its converse is 'It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some B is not C', and it is true when converted to affirmation, as it is known that 'Everything that is D is R' then all B is C, and 'All C is always either H or A', so it is clear that 'Everything that is D is R' then all B is always either H or A'.
The tenth conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is universal negative, as we say: 'And always all C is not either H or A', then 'Everything that is D is R' then all B is always not either H or A'.
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive classification of mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogisms where the shared term (the middle) is part of the antecedent of the conditional. The author first outlines the general conditions: the shared part can be either the predicate or the subject of the antecedent. If it is the subject of the disjunctive's parts, the syllogism follows the first figure. Specific conditions are listed: the antecedent of the conditional must have the same quality (affirmative/negative) as the conditional itself; if the conditional is negative, it must be universal; the disjunctive's parts must be universal (i.e., the judgment applies to all individuals). Then the author states that there are twenty-four productive combinations based on quantity and quality, but only the conclusion's form is described: a conditional whose antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional, and whose consequent is derived from combining the disjunctive with the antecedent. Eight specific conjunctions (iqtiranat) are enumerated (third to tenth, with two more from the earlier note making ten total? Actually the text lists first and second again? Parsing the Eight Conjunctions for the Subject-Shared Case
Defines conditions and enumerates eight conjunctions when the shared element is part of the antecedent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to parse each of the eight conjunctions for the subject-shared case, identifying the premises and the form of the conclusion.

Core Idea
Each conjunction varies the quantity and quality of the disjunctive to produce a correspondingly qualified conditional conclusion.

The author enumerates ten conjunctions (though we focus on the eight new ones) that systematically vary the disjunctive's quality and quantity. By tracing the pattern, you will learn to predict the conclusion's form from the premises alone. This skill is crucial for evaluating compound arguments in Islamic legal theory and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
For the first eight conjunctions, the conditional is universal affirmative. Its antecedent is universal affirmative in conjunctions 1-4, and particular affirmative in conjunctions 5-8.
The antecedent's quantity affects the conclusion's quantity.
2
Identify the disjunctive premise
Note whether the disjunctive is affirmative or negative, and universal or particular. This directly influences the conclusion's quality and quantity.
The disjunctive's parts must be universal as per the general condition.
3
Determine the conclusion's antecedent
The conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is always the consequent of the original conditional (e.g., 'if D is R').
This is constant across all conjunctions.
4
Determine the conclusion's consequent
The consequent is about the subject of the original antecedent (B) and the disjuncts (H and A). Its quality matches the disjunctive's quality, and its quantity matches the antecedent's quantity.
Example: Conjunctions 1-4 have universal antecedent → universal consequent; 5-8 have particular antecedent → particular consequent.
5
Apply to conjunctions 9 and 10
Here the conditional is universal negative. The conclusion becomes universal affirmative after conversion of the conditional. The disjunctive's quality still determines the conclusion's quality (affirmative/negative).
This shows a special case where conversion is used.

وأما إن كان الاشتراك بجزء المقدم فإما بمحموله أو بموضوعه، فإن كان بمحموله فإما أن يكون موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال أو محمولاً فيها، فإن كان موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال فهو على هيئة الشكل الأول، فشرطه إيجاب المقدم من المتصلة إن كانت موجبة وسلبه إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون المتصلة كلية إن كانت سالبة، وأن تكون أجزاء الانفصال كلية - أي الحكم بها على كل الموضوع - وقرائنه المنتجة باعتبار كليّة المقدمتين وجزئيتهما، وسلبهما وإيجابهما، وإيجاب المقدم وسالبه أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً، وخاصته أن النتيجة فيه تكون متصلة مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران المنفصلة بالمقدم ، وأنها قد تكون جزئية، وإن كانت المقدمات كليّة
الاقتران الأول من كليّة موجبة متصلة مقدمها كلي موجب، وكلية موجبة منفصلة كليّة الموضوع كقولنا: (كل) ما كان كل "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") و(دائماً كل "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ" ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ")
الاقتران الثاني: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كلية سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً كل "ج" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فليس البتة إما كل "ر" "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران الثالث المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية موجبة كقولنا: (وقد يكون كل "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران الرابع: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: (وليس دائماً كل "ج" إما "هـ" وإما "أ") ونتيجته كالثاني.
الاقتران الخامس من كلية موجبة متصلة، مقدمها جزئي موجب، وكلية موجبة منفصلة كقولنا (كل ما كان بعض "ب" "ج" "ر") و(دائماً إما كل "ج" "أ" وإما كل "ج" "هـ") ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران السادس : المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كلية سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً ليس إما كل "ج" "أ" وإما "هـ" ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ب" دائماً ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران السابع المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، ونتيجته كالخامس.
الاقتران الثامن: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، ونتيجته كالسادس.
الاقتران التاسع من كلية سالبة متصلة، مقدمها جزئي سالب، وكلية موجبة منفصلة، كقولنا: ليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ب" ليس "ج" فـ "د" "ر") وإما كل "ج" "أ" وإما "هـ") ونتيجته كليّة موجبة متصلة، وهي كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ") لأنك إذا عكست المتصلة كان عكسها ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "ر" ف (بعض "ب" ليس "ج") وهي تصدق عند القلب إلى الإيجاب، كما عرف كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" "ج" وكان (كل "ج" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فبين أنه كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" دائماً إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
الاقتران العاشر : المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن المنفصلة كليّة سالبة، وهي كقولنا (ودائماً كل "ج" ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ") فـ كل ما كان "د" "ر" فكل "ب" دائماً ليس إما "هـ" وإما "أ").
And if the sharing is by a part of the antecedent, then either by its predicate or its subject. If by its predicate, then either it is subject of the parts of the disjunction or predicate in them. If it is subject of the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the first figure. Its condition is that the antecedent of the conditional be affirmative if it is affirmative and negative if it is negative, and that the conditional be universal if it is negative, and that the parts of the disjunction be universal – i.e., the judgment about them applies to all subjects. Its productive conjunctions, considering the universality and particularity, and negation and affirmation of the two premises, and the affirmation and negation of the antecedent, are twenty-four conjunctions. Its property is that the conclusion in it is a conditional, its antecedent is the consequent of the conditional, and its consequent is the result of the conjunction of the disjunctive with the antecedent, and that it may be particular even if the premises are universal.
The first conjunction from a universal affirmative conditional whose antecedent is universal affirmative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive universal in subject, as we say: 'Everything that is all B is C, then D is R' and 'Always all C is either H or A', and its conclusion: 'It may be that if D is R, then all B is always either H or A'.
The second conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is universal negative, as we say: 'And always all C is not either H or A', then 'It may be that if D is R, then absolutely not either all R is H or A'.
The third conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is particular affirmative, as we say: 'And it may be that all C is either H or A', and its conclusion: 'It may be that if D is R, then all B is either H or A'.
The fourth conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is particular negative, as we say: 'And not always all C is either H or A', and its conclusion like the second.
The fifth conjunction from a universal affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is particular affirmative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive, as we say: 'Everything that is some B is C is R' and 'Always either all C is A or all C is H', and its conclusion: 'It may be that if D is R, then some B is always either H or A'.
The sixth conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is universal negative, as we say: 'And always it is not either all C is A or H', and its conclusion: 'It may be that if D is R, then some B is always not either H or A'.
The seventh conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is particular affirmative, and its conclusion like the fifth.
The eighth conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is particular negative, and its conclusion like the sixth.
The ninth conjunction from a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is particular negative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive, as we say: 'It is absolutely not the case that if some B is not C, then D is R' and 'Either all C is A or H', and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional: 'Everything that is D is R, then all B is always either H or A' because if you convert the conditional, its converse is 'It is absolutely not the case that if D is R, then some B is not C', and it is true when converted to affirmation, as it is known that 'Everything that is D is R' then all B is C, and 'All C is always either H or A', so it is clear that 'Everything that is D is R' then all B is always either H or A'.
The tenth conjunction: the conditional as before, except that the disjunctive is universal negative, as we say: 'And always all C is not either H or A', then 'Everything that is D is R' then all B is always not either H or A'.

Commentary
This passage presents a comprehensive classification of mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogisms where the shared term (the middle) is part of the antecedent of the conditional. The author first outlines the general conditions: the shared part can be either the predicate or the subject of the antecedent. If it is the subject of the disjunctive's parts, the syllogism follows the first figure. Specific conditions are listed: the antecedent of the conditional must have the same quality (affirmative/negative) as the conditional itself; if the conditional is negative, it must be universal; the disjunctive's parts must be universal (i.e., the judgment applies to all individuals). Then the author states that there are twenty-four productive combinations based on quantity and quality, but only the conclusion's form is described: a conditional whose antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional, and whose consequent is derived from combining the disjunctive with the antecedent. Parsing the 14 Mixed Conditional-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates 14 types of mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogisms with their conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the type of any mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogism from the enumeration by reading its description, including the quantification and quality of premises and the conclusion.

Core Idea
Each iqtiran is defined by the quantity and quality of its conditional and disjunctive premises.

The author systematically lists 14 types of mixed syllogisms. Each entry follows a consistent pattern: it names the conditional type, the antecedent type, the disjunctive type, gives an example formula, and states the conclusion. By learning to read this pattern, you can quickly identify which iqtiran is being described.

Guided Path
1
1. Read the opening phrase
Each iqtiran begins with 'الاقتران [number] من [type] متصلة'. Note the number and the type of the conditional (e.g., كلية موجبة, كلية سالبة). This tells you the main classification.
2
2. Identify the antecedent description
After 'مقدمها', you'll see a description like 'جزئي موجب' or 'كلي سالب'. This is the quantity and quality of the antecedent term in the conditional.
The antecedent is usually about some 'ب' or all 'ب'.
3
3. Identify the disjunctive type
The disjunctive is described after 'و' and before 'منفصلة', e.g., 'كلية موجبة منفصلة' or 'جزئية سالبة منفصلة'. Note its quantity and quality.
Disjunctive always involves 'إما... وإما...'.
4
4. See the example formula
After 'كقولنا:', the author gives an abstract proposition using variables (ب, ج, د, ر, أ, هـ). Match the structure to the types you identified.
Pay attention to the quantification words like 'كل', 'بعض', 'دائماً', 'قد يكون'.
5
5. Read the conclusion
After 'ونتيجته', the conclusion is stated. Note how the quantity of the subject in the conclusion relates to the premises. In many cases it is 'كل ما كان د ر فكل ب...' or similar.
For iqtiranat 9 and 13, the author includes a conversion explanation; note the extra steps.

The eighth conjunction: the conditional remaining as it is, except that the disjunctive is particular negative, and its conclusion is like the sixth. The ninth conjunction: from a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is particular negative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive, as we say: 'It is not at all that if some 'B' is not 'J' then 'D' is 'R'' and 'Either every 'J' is 'A' or 'H'' and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, which is 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then every 'B' is always either 'H' or 'A'' because if you convert the conditional, its converse is 'It is not at all that if 'D' is 'R' then some 'B' is not 'J'' and it is true upon conversion to affirmation, as known, 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then every 'B' is 'J'' and it was 'Every 'J' is always either 'H' or 'A'' so it is clear that 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then every 'B' is always either 'H' or 'A'.' The tenth conjunction: the conditional remaining as it is, except that the disjunctive is universal negative, and it is as we say: 'And always every 'J' is not either 'H' or 'A'' so 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then every 'B' is always not either 'H' or 'A'.' The eleventh conjunction: the conditional remaining as it is, except that the disjunctive is particular affirmative, as we say: 'And it may be that every 'J' is either 'H' or 'A'' so 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then every 'B' may be either 'H' or 'A'.' The twelfth conjunction: the conditional remaining as it is, except that the disjunctive is particular negative, as we say: 'And it is not always that either every 'J' is 'A' or 'H'' so 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then it is not always that every 'B' is either 'H' or 'A'.' The thirteenth conjunction: from a universal negative conditional, whose antecedent is universal negative, and a universal affirmative disjunctive, as we say: 'It is not at all that if no 'B' is 'J' then 'R'' and 'Always either every 'J' is 'A' or 'H'' so 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then some 'B' is always either 'A' or 'H'' because if you convert the conditional, its converse is 'It is not at all that if 'D' is 'R' then no 'B' is 'J'' and it is upon conversion to affirmation, it is true that 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then some 'B' is 'J'' and it was 'Every 'J' is always either 'A' or 'H'' so it becomes clear that if 'D' is 'R' then 'Some 'B' is always either 'A' or 'H'.' The fourteenth conjunction: the conditional remaining as it is, except that the disjunctive is universal negative, as we say: 'And it is always not that either every 'J' is 'A' or 'H'' and its conclusion is 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then some 'B' is always not either 'A' or 'H'.' The fifteenth conjunction: the conditional remaining as it is, except that the disjunctive is particular affirmative, as we say: 'And every 'J' may be either 'A' or 'H'' so 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then some 'B' may be either 'A' or 'H'.' The sixteenth conjunction: the conditional remaining as it is, except that the disjunctive is particular negative, as we say: 'And it is not always that either every 'J' is 'A' or 'H'' so 'Every that which is 'D' is 'R' then some 'B' is not always either 'A' or 'H'.' And if the conditional is particular affirmative, then other conjunctions may be composed from it with the disjunctive.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of fourteen types of mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogisms, known as iqtiranat. They are part of a larger classification of conjunctive syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) where the premises are not categorical propositions but conditionals (muttasila) and disjunctives (munfasila). The author builds upon earlier iqtiranat (the first and second are not included) and lists from the third to the sixteenth. Each iqtiran specifies the quantity and quality of the conditional and disjunctive premises and then states the resulting conclusion. The author uses logical variables (ب, ج, د, ر, أ, هـ) to represent terms, and the conclusions often involve the same conditional or disjunctive form with quantified statements. The ninth and thirteenth iqtiranat include a brief explanation involving conversion (aks) to justify the conclusion. For example, in the ninth iqtiran, the author shows how converting the negative conditional to an affirmative one yields the required conclusion. This passage is technical and requires careful parsing of the logical forms. It is likely intended for advanced students who have already mastered basic syllogistic forms.

Compound Syllogisms with Conditional and Disjunctive Premises
Enumerates six compound syllogisms with varying disjunctive premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct compound syllogisms of forms 11-16 by fixing a conditional premise and varying the disjunctive premise in quantity and quality.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms blend conditional and disjunctive premises to yield new conditionals — a key tool for multi-step reasoning in kalam.

In this passage, the author continues his enumeration of compound syllogisms (اقترانات) that combine a conditional premise (متصل) with a disjunctive premise (منفصل). These are not full categorical syllogisms but rather a pattern of inference that takes two premises — one conditional, one disjunctive — and produces a conditional conclusion. The pattern is to keep the conditional fixed and vary the disjunctive in quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). Understanding this pattern is essential for complex arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh where both conditional and disjunctive reasoning are combined, such as in proofs for the existence of God or the permissibility of actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fixed conditional premise
In all compounds 11-16, the conditional premise (المتصلة) is universal affirmative (كلية موجبة) unless stated otherwise. For example, the first premise in the 11th is 'Every B is either H or A' when D is R. Recognize that this is the constant element.
The conditional premise may be expressed with variables like ج, ب etc. Focus on its logical form: 'If D R, then B is either H or A'.
2
Identify the disjunctive premise and its quantifier-quality
The disjunctive premise (المنفصلة) varies. For each compound, note whether it is universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. For example, the 11th compound uses a particular affirmative disjunctive: 'Every J may be either H or A'.

Use the source's terminology: جزئية موجبة (particular affirmative), جزئية سالبة (particular negative), كلية موجبة (universal affirmative), كلية سالبة (universal negative).
The disjunctive is always about a different term (ج) than the conditional's terms.
3
Determine the conclusion from the combination
The conclusion is a conditional that mirrors the form of the first conditional but replaces the consequent with the disjunctive's content, adjusted for quantifier and quality. For instance, the 11th conclusion: 'Every what is D R, then every B may be either H or A' — here the consequent is particular affirmative because the disjunctive is particular affirmative.

Write the conclusion in the same pattern: 'If D R, then [quantifier] B is [disjunctive content].'
The conclusion always has 'كل ما كان د ر' (every D R) as the antecedent.
4
Apply conversion for the 13th compound
The 13th compound involves a universal negative conditional ('It is absolutely not the case that if no B is J, then R') and a universal affirmative disjunctive. The author shows that by converting the conditional (عكس) to a particular affirmative, you can combine it with the disjunctive to produce the conclusion: 'Every D R, then some B is always either A or H'.

Practice the conversion: from 'If no B is J then not R' (universal negative) to 'Every D R then some B is J' (particular affirmative).
This step illustrates how conversion rules apply in compound contexts.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of compound syllogisms (اقترانات) that combine a conditional premise (متصل) with a disjunctive premise (منفصل). The author continues from previous compounds, now listing the 11th through 16th. Each compound is defined by fixing the conditional and varying the disjunctive in quality and quantity. The author gives a formula for each: e.g., the 11th has a universal conditional and a particular affirmative disjunctive, yielding a conclusion that is a conditional with a particular affirmative consequent. The 13th involves a conversion step: the universal negative conditional is converted to a particular affirmative, which then combines with the disjunctive. After listing six, the author notes that if the conditional is particular affirmative, there are eight more compounds parallel to the first eight, left to the reader. This is a technical exposition typical of logical works, assuming familiarity with the earlier compounds and conversion rules.

Conditions for Productive Second-Figure Compound Syllogisms
Author states conditions for productive compound syllogisms in the second figure and gives an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to verify the conditions for productive second-figure compound syllogisms and apply them to an example from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Second-figure compound syllogisms require precise combinations of quantity and quality in their premises.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq presents the conditions for compound syllogisms when the arrangement resembles the second figure. Unlike categorical second figure, here the predicate of the conditional antecedent appears in the disjunction parts. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing valid compound arguments in Islamic logic, particularly in theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the second figure arrangement
Identify that the predicate of the conditional's antecedent (محمول المقدم) is also the predicate in each part of the disjunction (أجزاء الانفصال).
2
Check the conditional premise
The conditional must not be a particular negative. If it is affirmative, the disjunction must be universal; if negative, the disjunction can be particular.
3
Check the disjunctive premise
The disjunction must be either true exclusive (موجبة حقيقية) or inclusive (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع). Its parts must be uniform in quality (all affirmative or all negative) and their quantity depends on the antecedent's quality.
4
Apply specific quantity-quality rules
For each combination of conditional quality and antecedent quality, the parts of the disjunction have specific requirements as listed in the passage (e.g., if conditional is affirmative and antecedent is affirmative, parts must be universal negative or particular affirmative).
5
Determine the conclusion form
The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional, with antecedent = consequent of original conditional, consequent = result of combining original antecedent with disjunction. It is particular if the conditional is affirmative, universal if negative.

And if the predicate of the antecedent is predicated in the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the second figure, and the condition for its productivity is that the conditional is not particular negative, and that the disjunctive is true affirmative or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, and that it is universal if the conditional is affirmative, and that the parts of the disjunctive are universal uniform in quality. Commentary

After stating the conditions, the author notes that there are 28 productive compounds, and describes the form of the conclusion: it is always an affirmative conditional where the antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional, and the consequent is the result of combining the original antecedent with the disjunctive. The conclusion is particular if the conditional is affirmative, universal if negative. Then the first two compounds are given as examples: first with universal negative parts, second with particular affirmative parts. The author concludes by noting that the remaining 26 compounds can be derived similarly. This is a highly technical section typical of advanced logic works.

Conditions for Second Figure Conditional-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author states conditions for second figure mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogisms and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid second-figure conditional-disjunctive syllogism as stated by the author and apply them to determine whether a given conjunction is productive.

Core Idea
Second-figure mixed syllogisms require the conditional not be particular negative and the disjunctive be true affirmative or exclusive of emptiness.

In this passage, the author details the conditions under which a conditional-disjunctive syllogism in the second figure yields a valid conclusion. These conditions are precise and must be memorized. They govern the quality and quantity of both the conditional and disjunctive premises. Mastering them is key to constructing sound mixed syllogisms in Islamic logical discourse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the form
Recognize that in the second figure, the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional is also a predicate in both parts of the disjunction.
This mirrors the categorical second figure where the middle term is predicate in both premises.
2
Condition 1: Conditional not particular negative
The conditional proposition cannot be a particular negative (e.g., 'Some A is not B if...').
If it were, the syllogism would not be productive.
3
Condition 2: Disjunctive must be true affirmative or exclusive of emptiness
The disjunctive must be either a true affirmative (i.e., disjunction covering all possibilities) or a man'at al-khuluw (exclusive disjunction that cannot both be false).
It cannot be man'at al-jam' (cannot both be true) alone.
4
Condition 3: If conditional is affirmative, disjunctive must be universal
When the conditional is affirmative, the disjunctive must be universal in quantity (covering all individuals).
5
Subconditions based on antecedent quality
Depending on whether the antecedent is affirmative or negative, the parts of the disjunction must have specific qualities and quantities as listed.
For example, if antecedent is affirmative, disjuncts must be universal negative or particular affirmative.

وأما إن كان محمول المقدم محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال فهو على هيئة الشكل الثاني، وشرط إنتاجه أن لا تكون المتصلة جزئية سالبة، وأن تكون المنفصلة موجبة حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون كلية إن كانت المتصلة موجبة، وأن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كلية متحدة الكيفية. وإن كانت المتصلة موجبة والمقدم موجباً، أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كلية سالبة، أو جزئية موجبة. وإن كان سالباً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليّة موجبة، أو جزئية سالبة. وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة وكان المقدم موجباً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كلية موجبة، أو جزئية سالبة. وإن كان سالباً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كلية سالبة، أو جزئية موجبة، وقرائنه المنتجة ثمانية وعشرون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل موجبة متصلة، مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران المقدم بالمنفصلة، إلا أن المتصلة إذا كانت موجبة فالنتيجة جزئية، وإن كانت سالبة فالنتيجة كلية. الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم كلي موجب، وأجزاء المنفصلة كلية سالبة كقولنا كل ما كان كل "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") و(دائماً إما لا شيء من "أ" "ج" وإما لا شيء من "هـ" "ج") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فإما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "هـ") أي لا يخلو منهما. الاقتران الثاني: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، كقولنا: ودائماً إما بعض "أ" "ج" وإما بعض "هـ" "ج") فـ ( لا شيء من "" "ج") وكان (كل ب" "ج". وعلى هذا لا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كان المقدم جزئياً موجباً، أو كلياً سالباً، أو جزئياً سالباً، وإذا كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة أو كلية سالبة، وهي ستة وعشرون اقتراناً أخرى، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
And if the predicate of the antecedent is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction, it is in the form of the second figure. Its condition for production is that the conditional is not particular negative, and the disjunctive is true affirmative or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, and that it be universal if the conditional is affirmative, and that the parts of the disjunction be universal uniform in quality. And if the conditional is affirmative and the antecedent is affirmative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal negative, or particular affirmative. And if it is negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmative, or particular negative. And if the conditional is negative and the antecedent is affirmative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmative, or particular negative. And if it is negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal negative, or particular affirmative. Its productive conjunctions are twenty-eight conjunctions. The conclusion in all is an affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the consequent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the antecedent with the disjunction, except that if the conditional is affirmative, the conclusion is particular, and if it is negative, the conclusion is universal. The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent is universal affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction are universal negative, such as our saying: 'Everything that is every B is C, so D is R' and 'Always it is either nothing of A is C, or nothing of H is C' thus 'It may be that when D is R, then either nothing of B is A, or nothing of B is H', i.e., not free from them. The second conjunction: The conditional as it is, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative, such as our saying: 'And always it is either some A is C, or some H is C' thus '[...]' and it was 'every B is C'. And on this, production is not hidden if the antecedent is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, and if the conditional is particular affirmative or universal negative, which are another twenty-six conjunctions, so consider them yourself.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogisms. It begins by stating the condition: the predicate of the antecedent of the conditional must be the predicate in the parts of the disjunction. Then it lists the detailed conditions for validity, including that the conditional cannot be a particular negative, and the disjunctive must be true affirmative or exclusive of emptiness (not both). The quality and quantity of the parts of the disjunction must match the antecedent and conditional. The author then gives examples: first, when both premise and antecedent are universal affirmative and the disjuncts are universal negative; second, when the disjuncts are particular affirmative. He notes the number of productive conjunctions (28) and the nature of the conclusion. He leaves the remaining conjunctions for the reader to derive, which is a common pedagogical technique in logical texts. The passage is technical and assumes familiarity with logical notation (Arabic letters as variables).

Key Terms
المتصلة (al-muttasila): The conditional proposition (hypothetical) in a syllogism, usually in the form 'if...then...' [Note: Stable across Islamic logic.]
المنفصلة (al-munfasila): The disjunctive proposition, either exclusive (man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw) or inclusive (man'at al-khuluw) [Note: In earlier texts, disjunctive may be broader; here specified as 'true affirmative' or 'exclusive of emptiness'.]
المقدم (al-muqaddam): The antecedent of a conditional proposition [Note: No significant shift.]
الشكل الثاني (al-shakl al-thani): The second figure of syllogism where the middle term is predicate in both premises (here adapted for conditional-disjunctive) [Note: In categorical syllogisms, second figure has middle term as predicate in both; here used analogically for mixed syllogisms.]
قرائن منتجة (qara'in muntija): Productive conjunctions (iqtiranat) that yield a valid conclusion [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Universal affirmative
C) True affirmative or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction
D) Particular affirmative

Key Takeaway
The second figure for mixed syllogisms imposes strict conditions on the conditional and disjunctive premises to guarantee a valid conclusion.

Paraworking the First Example of a Productive Conjunction
Author states conditions for second figure mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogisms and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first example of a second-figure conditional-disjunctive syllogism from the passage, identify its logical components, and verify that it satisfies the conditions.

Core Idea
The first example shows how two universal affirmatives and a universal negative disjunction produce a valid particular conditional conclusion.

After listing the conditions, the author provides an example to illustrate a productive conjunction. This example uses term variables to show the form. By breaking it down, we see each condition applied. This lesson guides you through the analysis of that example.

Guided Path
1
Locate the example
Identify the first example in the passage: 'الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين...'
2
Write the conditional premise
The conditional is: 'كل ما كان كل ب ج ف د ر' (Everything that is every B is C, so D is R). This is a universal affirmative conditional.
3
Write the disjunctive premise
The disjunctive is: 'دائماً إما لا شيء من أ ج وإما لا شيء من هـ ج' (Always either nothing of A is C or nothing of H is C). This is a universal negative disjunction exclusive of emptiness.
The author specifies it is 'man'at al-khuluw' (exclusive of emptiness).
4
Check the form
Verify that the predicate of the antecedent (C) is the predicate in both parts of the disjunction (C). This matches the second figure condition.
5
Check condition satisfaction
The conditional is not particular negative; the disjunctive is universal and true affirmative/exclusive of emptiness; the antecedent is universal affirmative; the disjuncts are universal negative, which satisfies the condition when antecedent is affirmative.

The Third Figure: Structure and Conditions
Author defines the third figure of compound syllogisms with conditions and enumerates productive conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third figure of a compound conditional-disjunctive syllogism and state the necessary conditions for its productivity.

Core Idea
The third figure links the subject of the conditional's antecedent to the parts of the disjunction.

In compound syllogisms combining a conditional and a disjunctive premise, the third figure arises when the subject of the conditional's antecedent is also the subject of the disjunctive parts. The author lays down precise conditions to guarantee a valid conclusion, which we will now unpack.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining condition of the third figure
Locate the sentence that begins 'فإن كان موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال...' This condition states that the subject of the antecedent of the conditional is the subject of the parts of the disjunction. That is what makes it the third figure.
2
List the required uniformity assumption
The author assumes that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality. This simplifies the enumeration.
3
Extract the specific conditions for productivity
Find the conditions: the conditional must not be a particular negative; the disjunction must be universal if the conditional is affirmative; the antecedent of the conditional must match its quality (affirmative if affirmative, negative if negative).
4
Understand why these conditions are necessary
These conditions ensure that the middle term (the subject) is distributed properly to yield a valid conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال فهو على هيئة الشكل الثالث وليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية - وأنت تعرف ما وراء ذلك - وشرطه في الإنتاج أن لا تكون المتصلة جزئية سالبة، وأن تكون المنفصلة كليّة إن كانت المتصلة موجبة وإن كانت المتصلة موجبة أن يكون المقدم موجباً، وإن كانت سالبة أن يكون المقدم سالباً، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة وخمسون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل موجبة متصلة، مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران المقدم بالمنفصلة إلا أن النتيجة عند كون المتصلة موجبة تكون جزئية، وعند كون المتصلة سالبة تكون كليّة، والموضوع الأجزاء الانفصال في تالي النتيجة لا يكون إلا جزئياً: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والتالي كلي موجب، وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة كقولنا كل ما كان كل "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") و(دائماً إما أن يكون كل "ب" "أ" وإما "هـ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" دائماً إما "أ" وإما "هـ"). الاقتران الثاني: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة، كقولنا: (ودائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "هـ") ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فدائماً إما ليس كل "ج" "أ" وإما ليس كل "ج" "هـ"). الاقتران الثالث: المنفصلة بحالها إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، كقولنا ودائماً إما بعض "ب" "أ" وإما بعض "ب" "هـ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" دائماً إما بعض "ج" "أ" وإما "هـ"). الاقتران الرابع المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "أ" وإما ليس كل "ب" "هـ") ونتيجته كالثاني. وإن كانت المتصلة بحالها والمنفصلة كليّة سالبة، فقد يأتلف منهما اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الأربعة، إلا أن تالي النتيجة فيها يكون بسلب الانفصال. وإن كان مقدم المتصلة جزئياً موجباً، فقد يأتلف منه ومن المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى ثمانية موازية لهذه الثمانية. وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يأتلف منها ومن المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى ستة عشر موازية للاقترانات الكائنة إذا كانت المتصلة كليّة موجبة. وإن كانت المتصلة كلية سالبة، فقد يأتلف منها ومن المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً والمتصلة فيها يجوز أن تكون جزئية موجبة وسالبة، فاعتبرها فإنها سهلة الاعتبار.
If it [the antecedent's subject] is a subject of the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the third figure, assuming that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality - and you know what is beyond that. Its condition for production is that the conditional not be a particular negative, and that the disjunction be universal if the conditional is affirmative, and that if the conditional is affirmative the antecedent be affirmative, and if it is negative the antecedent be negative. Its productive conjunctive pairs are fifty-six conjunctions. The conclusion in all is an affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the consequent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the antecedent with the disjunction, except that when the conditional is affirmative the conclusion is particular, and when the conditional is negative the conclusion is universal. The subject of the parts of the disjunction in the consequent of the conclusion is only particular. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, the consequent universal affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction universal affirmatives, as we say: 'Everything that is every B is J, so D is R' and 'Always, either every B is A or every B is H', so 'It may be that if D is R, then some J is always either A or H'. The second conjunction: the conditional as it is, except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, as we say: 'Always, either no B is A or no B is H', and its conclusion: 'It may be that if D is R, then always either not every J is A or not every J is H'. The third conjunction: the disjunction as it is, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, as we say: 'Always, either some B is A or some B is H', so 'It may be that if D is R, then always some J is either A or H'. The fourth conjunction: the conditional as it is, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, as we say: 'Always, either not every B is A or not every B is H', and its conclusion is like the second. If the conditional is as it is and the disjunction is universal negative, then from them are composed another four conjunctions parallel to these four, except that the consequent of the conclusion is with the negation of the disjunction. If the antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative, then from it and the disjunction are composed another eight conjunctions parallel to these eight. If the conditional is particular affirmative, then from it and the disjunction are composed another sixteen conjunctions parallel to the conjunctions occurring when the conditional is universal affirmative. If the conditional is universal negative, then from it and the disjunction are composed another twenty-four conjunctions. The conditional in them may be particular affirmative and negative. Consider them, for they are easy to consider.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the third figure of compound syllogisms consisting of a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) premise. The author defines the figure by the condition that the subject of the antecedent of the conditional is the subject of the parts of the disjunction. He then lists the general conditions for productivity: the conditional must not be a particular negative; the disjunction must be universal if the conditional is affirmative; the antecedent must match the conditional in quality. The number of productive conjunctions is given as 56. The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional with the consequent of the original conditional as its antecedent, and the consequent of the conclusion is derived from conjoining the original antecedent with the disjunction. The author then enumerates the first four basic conjunctions with examples using variables (B, J, D, R, A, H). He explains that if the disjunction is universal negative, four more parallel conjunctions arise; if the antecedent is particular affirmative, eight more; if the conditional is particular affirmative, sixteen more; if the conditional is universal negative, twenty-four more. The total is 4+4+8+16+24=56. This systematic enumeration is characteristic of the later logical tradition, especially in works like Daqaiq Haqaiq, which aims to exhaust all valid moods. The examples use abstract letters, but in Islamic logic these are placeholders for terms from fiqh, kalam, etc. For instance, one could substitute: If every khamr is intoxicating, then it is forbidden, and either every khamr is a liquid or every khamr is a beverage. The conclusion would be a particular conditional.

Key Terms
المتصلة (al-muttasila): A conditional proposition (qadiyya shartiyya muttasila) whose antecedent and consequent are connected by implication. [Note: This term is standard across the tradition; no significant shift.]
المنفصلة (al-munfasila): A disjunctive proposition (qadiyya shartiyya munfasila) that asserts a mutually exclusive or inclusive disjunction between parts. [Note: In some earlier texts, munfasila may refer specifically to exclusive disjunction; here it includes both inclusive and exclusive, but the examples show exhaustive parts.]
الاقتران (al-iqtiran): A conjunctive pair of premises (conditional and disjunctive) that yield a conclusion. [Note: This term is used in later compound syllogism theory; in earlier Aristotelian logic, it might refer to the conjunction of terms.]
الشكل الثالث (al-shakl al-thalith): The third figure of compound syllogisms where the antecedent's subject is subject of the disjunctive parts. [Note: This usage is specific to conditional-disjunctive syllogisms; in categorical syllogisms, the third figure has a different definition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition must the disjunction satisfy if the conditional premise is affirmative?
A) The disjunction must be particular.
B) The disjunction must be universal.
C) The disjunction must be negative.
D) The disjunction must be affirmative.

Key Takeaway
The third figure of compound syllogisms is defined by the common subject of the conditional's antecedent and the disjunctive parts, with strict conditions on the quality and quantity of premises for productivity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the statement 'If every human is mortal, then Zayd is mortal' and 'Either every human is rational or every human is an animal', determine whether this forms a valid third figure syllogism. Check all conditions: is the conditional a particular negative? Is the disjunction universal? Does the antecedent match the quality? Write your answer.

Productive Conjunctions and Conclusion Formation
Author defines the third figure of compound syllogisms with conditions and enumerates productive conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to generate the four basic productive conjunctions of the third figure and explain how the conclusion varies with the conditional's quality.

Core Idea
From four base conjunctions, 56 valid moods emerge by systematic variation.

Having established the structure and conditions, the author now enumerates the productive conjunctions. He begins with four base cases where both premises are universal, then shows how changing the quantity and quality of the disjunction or the conditional leads to 52 more. We will focus on the first four to understand the pattern of conclusion formation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first conjunction
Find the example starting with 'الاقتران الأول'. It pairs a universal affirmative conditional with a universal affirmative disjunction. Note the conclusion pattern: it is a particular affirmative conditional.
2
Understand how the conclusion is structured
The conclusion's antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional (e.g., 'D is R'), and its consequent results from conjoining the original antecedent with the disjunction (e.g., 'some J is always either A or H'). This is the general form.
3
Examine the second conjunction
The conditional remains the same, but the disjunctive parts become universal negatives. The conclusion is similar but with negated predicates: 'not every J is A' etc. This shows how the quality of the disjunction flips the conclusion's predicates.
4
Extend to the third and fourth conjunctions
The third uses particular affirmative disjunction, the fourth particular negative. Observe how the conclusion's quantity and quality are preserved from the disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان موضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال فهو على هيئة الشكل الثالث وليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية - وأنت تعرف ما وراء ذلك - وشرطه في الإنتاج أن لا تكون المتصلة جزئية سالبة، وأن تكون المنفصلة كليّة إن كانت المتصلة موجبة وإن كانت المتصلة موجبة أن يكون المقدم موجباً، وإن كانت سالبة أن يكون المقدم سالباً، وقرائنه المنتجة ستة وخمسون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل موجبة متصلة، مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران المقدم بالمنفصلة إلا أن النتيجة عند كون المتصلة موجبة تكون جزئية، وعند كون المتصلة سالبة تكون كليّة، والموضوع الأجزاء الانفصال في تالي النتيجة لا يكون إلا جزئياً: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والتالي كلي موجب، وأجزاء الانفصال كليات موجبة كقولنا كل ما كان كل "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") و(دائماً إما أن يكون كل "ب" "أ" وإما "هـ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" دائماً إما "أ" وإما "هـ"). الاقتران الثاني: المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة، كقولنا: (ودائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ب" "هـ") ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فدائماً إما ليس كل "ج" "أ" وإما ليس كل "ج" "هـ"). الاقتران الثالث: المنفصلة بحالها إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، كقولنا ودائماً إما بعض "ب" "أ" وإما بعض "ب" "هـ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" دائماً إما بعض "ج" "أ" وإما "هـ"). الاقتران الرابع المتصلة بحالها، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً إما ليس كل "ب" "أ" وإما ليس كل "ب" "هـ") ونتيجته كالثاني. وإن كانت المتصلة بحالها والمنفصلة كليّة سالبة، فقد يأتلف منهما اقترانات أخرى أربعة موازية لهذه الأربعة، إلا أن تالي النتيجة فيها يكون بسلب الانفصال. وإن كان مقدم المتصلة جزئياً موجباً، فقد يأتلف منه ومن المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى ثمانية موازية لهذه الثمانية. وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يأتلف منها ومن المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى ستة عشر موازية للاقترانات الكائنة إذا كانت المتصلة كليّة موجبة. وإن كانت المتصلة كلية سالبة، فقد يأتلف منها ومن المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة وعشرون اقتراناً والمتصلة فيها يجوز أن تكون جزئية موجبة وسالبة، فاعتبرها فإنها سهلة الاعتبار.
If it [the antecedent's subject] is a subject of the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the third figure, assuming that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality - and you know what is beyond that. Its condition for production is that the conditional not be a particular negative, and that the disjunction be universal if the conditional is affirmative, and that if the conditional is affirmative the antecedent be affirmative, and if it is negative the antecedent be negative. Its productive conjunctive pairs are fifty-six conjunctions. The conclusion in all is an affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the consequent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the antecedent with the disjunction, except that when the conditional is affirmative the conclusion is particular, and when the conditional is negative the conclusion is universal. The subject of the parts of the disjunction in the consequent of the conclusion is only particular. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, the consequent universal affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction universal affirmatives, as we say: 'Everything that is every B is J, so D is R' and 'Always, either every B is A or every B is H', so 'It may be that if D is R, then some J is always either A or H'. The second conjunction: the conditional as it is, except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, as we say: 'Always, either no B is A or no B is H', and its conclusion: 'It may be that if D is R, then always either not every J is A or not every J is H'. The third conjunction: the disjunction as it is, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, as we say: 'Always, either some B is A or some B is H', so 'It may be that if D is R, then always some J is either A or H'. The fourth conjunction: the conditional as it is, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, as we say: 'Always, either not every B is A or not every B is H', and its conclusion is like the second. If the conditional is as it is and the disjunction is universal negative, then from them are composed another four conjunctions parallel to these four, except that the consequent of the conclusion is with the negation of the disjunction. If the antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative, then from it and the disjunction are composed another eight conjunctions parallel to these eight. If the conditional is particular affirmative, then from it and the disjunction are composed another sixteen conjunctions parallel to the conjunctions occurring when the conditional is universal affirmative. If the conditional is universal negative, then from it and the disjunction are composed another twenty-four conjunctions. The conditional in them may be particular affirmative and negative. Consider them, for they are easy to consider.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the third figure of compound syllogisms consisting of a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) premise. The author defines the figure by the condition that the subject of the antecedent of the conditional is the subject of the parts of the disjunction. He then lists the general conditions for productivity: the conditional must not be a particular negative; the disjunction must be universal if the conditional is affirmative; the antecedent must match the conditional in quality. The number of productive conjunctions is given as 56. The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional with the consequent of the original conditional as its antecedent, and the consequent of the conclusion is derived from conjoining the original antecedent with the disjunction. The author then enumerates the first four basic conjunctions with examples using variables (B, J, D, R, A, H). He explains that if the disjunction is universal negative, four more parallel conjunctions arise; if the antecedent is particular affirmative, eight more; if the conditional is particular affirmative, sixteen more; if the conditional is universal negative, twenty-four more. The total is 4+4+8+16+24=56. This systematic enumeration is characteristic of the later logical tradition, especially in works like Daqaiq Haqaiq, which aims to exhaust all valid moods. The examples use abstract letters, but in Islamic logic these are placeholders for terms from fiqh, kalam, etc. For instance, one could substitute: If every khamr is intoxicating, then it is forbidden, and either every khamr is a liquid or every khamr is a beverage. The conclusion would be a particular conditional.

Key Terms
المتصلة (al-muttasila): A conditional proposition (qadiyya shartiyya muttasila) whose antecedent and consequent are connected by implication. [Note: This term is standard across the tradition; no significant shift.]
المنفصلة (al-munfasila): A disjunctive proposition (qadiyya shartiyya munfasila) that asserts a mutually exclusive or inclusive disjunction between parts. [Note: In some earlier texts, munfasila may refer specifically to exclusive disjunction; here it includes both inclusive and exclusive, but the examples show exhaustive parts.]
الاقتران (al-iqtiran): A conjunctive pair of premises (conditional and disjunctive) that yield a conclusion. [Note: This term is used in later compound syllogism theory; in earlier Aristotelian logic, it might refer to the conjunction of terms.]
الشكل الثالث (al-shakl al-thalith): The third figure of compound syllogisms where the antecedent's subject is subject of the disjunctive parts. [Note: This usage is specific to conditional-disjunctive syllogisms; in categorical syllogisms, the third figure has a different definition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Particular
C) Indefinite
D) It depends on the disjunction

Key Takeaway
The third figure yields 56 productive conjunctions; the conclusion is always an affirmative conditional, with quantity depending on the conditional's quality.
