Defining the Fourth Figure and Its Conditions
Definition of the fourth figure and its validity conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure of conditional-disjunctive syllogisms and list its validity conditions.

Core Idea
The fourth figure mixes a conditional and a disjunctive premise with 22 valid moods.

The fourth figure is defined by a specific structural relation: the subject of the antecedent of the conditional appears as a predicate in the parts of the disjunction. This lesson unpacks the conditions for a valid combination, which are essential for constructing sound arguments in Islamic logic and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structural definition
The fourth figure occurs when the subject of the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional is a predicate in each part of the disjunction (munfasila). This is the defining feature.
2
Learn the conditions for a valid conclusion
The conditional cannot be particular negative; if affirmative, its antecedent cannot be particular negative. The disjunction must be affirmative, and universal if the conditional is particular. Additional conditions apply based on quantity and quality.
These conditions ensure the syllogism is productive (muntij).
3
Recognize the conclusion form
The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional. Its antecedent is the consequent of the original conditional, and its consequent is derived by conjoining the antecedent with the disjunction. The quantity depends on the original conditional: if affirmative, conclusion is particular; if universal negative, conclusion is universal affirmative.
The text states: 'النتيجة في الكل موجبة متصلة'.
4
Note the total number of productive conjunctions
The author states there are 22 productive conjunctions (qara'in muntija). This enumeration comes from varying the quantity and quality of the premises within the validity conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان موضوع المقدم محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال، فهو على هيئة الشكل الرابع وليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية، وشرط إنتاجه أن لا تكون المتصلة جزئية سالبة، وإن كانت موجبة أن لا يكون المقدم جزئياً سالباً، وأن تكون المنفصلة موجبة، وأن تكون كليّة إن كانت المتصلة جزئية، وإن كانت المتصلة موجبة والمقدم جزئياً موجباً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة أو جزئيات موجبة، وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة والمقدم كلياً سالباً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة أو جزئيات موجبة، وقرائنه المنتجة اثنان وعشرون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل موجبة متصلة، مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران المقدم بالمنفصلة، إلا أنها إذا كانت المتصلة موجبة فالنتيجة تكون جزئية موجبة، وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة كليّة فالنتيجة كلية موجبة منفصلة.
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم من المتصلة كلي موجب، وأجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة كقولنا: كل ما كان كل "ب" "ج" فـ "د" دائماً، ودائماً إما كل "أ" "ب" وإما كل "هـ" "ب" فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" دائماً إما "أ" وإما "هـ").
الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً إما لا شيء من "أ" "ب" وإما لا شيء من "هـ" "ب" فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" دائماً ليس إما "أ" وإما "هـ").
الاقتران الثالث: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، كقولنا: ودائماً إما بعض "أ" "ب" وإما بعض "هـ" "ب" نتيجته كالأول.
الاقتران الرابع: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً إما ليس كل "أ" "ب" وإما ليس كل "هـ" "ب" ونتيجته كالأول أيضاً؛ لأنه إن صدق ليس كل "أ" "ب" صدق (كل "هـ" "ب") وإن صدق ليس كل "هـ" "ب" صدق (كل "أ" "ب") وكان (كل "ب" "ج").
وإن كان المقدم من المتصلة جزئياً موجباً، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانان آخران وهما ما تكون أجزاء المنفصلة فيها كليات سالبة أو جزئيات موجبة.
وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى ستة موازية لهذه الستة.
وإن كانت المتصلة كلية سالبة، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى ستة موازية للاقترانات الستة الأولى، وأربعة أخرى وهي ما المنفصلة فيها جزئية موجبة، فاعتبرها.
And if the subject of the antecedent is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the fourth figure. Let the assumption be that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality. The condition of its production is that the conditional not be particular negative; and if it is affirmative, that the antecedent not be particular negative; and that the disjunction be affirmative; and that it be universal if the conditional is particular; and if the conditional is affirmative and the antecedent is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives; and if the conditional is negative and the antecedent is universal negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. Its productive conjunctions are twenty-two. The conclusion in all is an affirmative conditional, its antecedent being the consequent of the conditional, and its consequent being the result of conjoining the antecedent with the disjunction, except that if the conditional is affirmative, the conclusion is particular affirmative; and if the conditional is universal negative, the conclusion is universal affirmative conditional.
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, like our statement: 'Whenever everything that is B is C, then D is always, and always either every A is B or every H is B', so (it is possible that when D is R, then some C is always either A or H).
The second conjunction: the conjunction as is, except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, like our statement: 'And always either no A is B or no H is B', so (it is possible that when D is R, then some C is always not either A or H).
The third conjunction: the conjunction as is, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, like our statement: 'And always either some A is B or some H is B', its conclusion is like the first.
The fourth conjunction: the conjunction as is, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, like our statement: 'And always either not every A is B or not every H is B', and its conclusion is also like the first; because if 'not every A is B' is true, then 'every H is B' is true, and if 'not every H is B' is true, then 'every A is B' is true, and it was 'every B is C'.
And if the antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative, then there result from it and the disjunction two other conjunctions, namely those where the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives or particular affirmatives.
And if the conditional is particular affirmative, then there result from it and the disjunction six other conjunctions parallel to these six.
And if the conditional is universal negative, then there result from it and the disjunction six other conjunctions parallel to the first six, and four others where the disjunction is particular affirmative, so consider them.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rabi') in conditional-disjunctive syllogisms (al-qiyasat al-shartiyyat al-munfasilat). The author begins by defining the structure: when the subject of the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction (ajza' al-infisal), the syllogism takes the form of the fourth figure. He then lists the conditions for a valid production: the conditional cannot be particular negative, and if it is affirmative, the antecedent cannot be particular negative; the disjunction must be affirmative; and it must be universal if the conditional is particular. These conditions ensure the logical validity of the resulting conclusion. The total number of productive conjunctions (qara'in) is twenty-two. The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional, but its quantity depends on the original conditional: if affirmative, the conclusion is particular; if universal negative, the conclusion is universal affirmative. The author then illustrates with examples using letters as variables (A, B, H, etc.), showing four specific conjunctions with different quantities and qualities of the disjunction parts. He explains that the first four conjunctions each yield a conclusion of the same form, because conversions (aks) can be applied to the particular negatives to obtain universal affirmatives. Further, he notes additional conjunctions when the antecedent is particular affirmative or when the conditional is particular affirmative or universal negative, but he leaves the enumeration to the reader (fai'tabirha). The style is typical of logical matn and sharh, compressing many details into concise conditions and examples. The passage is crucial for understanding how to combine conditional and disjunctive premises in the fourth figure to produce valid conclusions, a topic often treated in advanced Islamic logic.

Enumerating the Productive Conjunctions of the Fourth Figure
Enumeration of productive conjunctions with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the first four productive conjunctions of the fourth figure and explain how to derive the conclusion.

Core Idea
The fourth figure's 22 conjunctions are generated by varying the quantity and quality of premises while respecting validity conditions.

After defining the fourth figure, the author illustrates four specific conjunctions, each with different quantities and qualities of the disjunction parts. By understanding these examples, you will see how the conditions yield valid conclusions and how to expand to the full set of 22.

Guided Path
1
Review the first conjunction
Both premises are universal affirmative: conditional antecedent universal affirmative, disjunction parts universal affirmative. Conclusion: particular affirmative conditional as given in the example.
Example: 'كل ما كان كل ب ج فـ د دائماً، ودائماً إما كل أ ب وإما كل هـ ب' yields 'قد يكون إذا كان د ر فبعض ج دائماً إما أ وإما هـ'.
2
Examine the second conjunction
Same as first, but disjunction parts are universal negative. The conclusion remains similar because of conversion (aks) of the negative parts.
The text says 'نتيجته كالأول'.
3
Examine the third and fourth conjunctions
Third: disjunction parts are particular affirmative. Fourth: disjunction parts are particular negative. Both also yield the same conclusion form as the first, due to conversions.
The author explicitly explains that particular negatives can be converted to universals.
4
Understand the pattern for other conjunctions
When the antecedent is particular affirmative, two more conjunctions appear. When the conditional is particular affirmative or universal negative, additional sets of six and four conjunctions are listed. The author leaves full enumeration to the reader ('فاعتبرها').
This pattern shows systematic enumeration based on varying quantities and qualities.

Source Text

وأما إن كان موضوع المقدم محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال، فهو على هيئة الشكل الرابع وليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية، وشرط إنتاجه أن لا تكون المتصلة جزئية سالبة، وإن كانت موجبة أن لا يكون المقدم جزئياً سالباً، وأن تكون المنفصلة موجبة، وأن تكون كليّة إن كانت المتصلة جزئية، وإن كانت المتصلة موجبة والمقدم جزئياً موجباً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة أو جزئيات موجبة، وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة والمقدم كلياً سالباً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة أو جزئيات موجبة، وقرائنه المنتجة اثنان وعشرون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل موجبة متصلة، مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران المقدم بالمنفصلة، إلا أنها إذا كانت المتصلة موجبة فالنتيجة تكون جزئية موجبة، وإن كانت المتصلة سالبة كليّة فالنتيجة كلية موجبة منفصلة.
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، والمقدم من المتصلة كلي موجب، وأجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة كقولنا: كل ما كان كل "ب" "ج" فـ "د" دائماً، ودائماً إما كل "أ" "ب" وإما كل "هـ" "ب" فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" دائماً إما "أ" وإما "هـ").
الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة كليات سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً إما لا شيء من "أ" "ب" وإما لا شيء من "هـ" "ب" فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "ر" فبعض "ج" دائماً ليس إما "أ" وإما "هـ").
الاقتران الثالث: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، كقولنا: ودائماً إما بعض "أ" "ب" وإما بعض "هـ" "ب" نتيجته كالأول.
الاقتران الرابع: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: ودائماً إما ليس كل "أ" "ب" وإما ليس كل "هـ" "ب" ونتيجته كالأول أيضاً؛ لأنه إن صدق ليس كل "أ" "ب" صدق (كل "هـ" "ب") وإن صدق ليس كل "هـ" "ب" صدق (كل "أ" "ب") وكان (كل "ب" "ج").
وإن كان المقدم من المتصلة جزئياً موجباً، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانان آخران وهما ما تكون أجزاء المنفصلة فيها كليات سالبة أو جزئيات موجبة.
وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى ستة موازية لهذه الستة.
وإن كانت المتصلة كلية سالبة، فقد يأتلف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى ستة موازية للاقترانات الستة الأولى، وأربعة أخرى وهي ما المنفصلة فيها جزئية موجبة، فاعتبرها.
And if the subject of the antecedent is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the fourth figure. Let the assumption be that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality. The condition of its production is that the conditional not be particular negative; and if it is affirmative, that the antecedent not be particular negative; and that the disjunction be affirmative; and that it be universal if the conditional is particular; and if the conditional is affirmative and the antecedent is particular affirmative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives; and if the conditional is negative and the antecedent is universal negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal negatives or particular affirmatives. Its productive conjunctions are twenty-two. The conclusion in all is an affirmative conditional, its antecedent being the consequent of the conditional, and its consequent being the result of conjoining the antecedent with the disjunction, except that if the conditional is affirmative, the conclusion is particular affirmative; and if the conditional is universal negative, the conclusion is universal affirmative conditional.
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative, and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, like our statement: 'Whenever everything that is B is C, then D is always, and always either every A is B or every H is B', so (it is possible that when D is R, then some C is always either A or H).
The second conjunction: the conjunction as is, except that the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives, like our statement: 'And always either no A is B or no H is B', so (it is possible that when D is R, then some C is always not either A or H).
The third conjunction: the conjunction as is, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, like our statement: 'And always either some A is B or some H is B', its conclusion is like the first.
The fourth conjunction: the conjunction as is, except that the parts of the disjunction are particular negatives, like our statement: 'And always either not every A is B or not every H is B', and its conclusion is also like the first; because if 'not every A is B' is true, then 'every H is B' is true, and if 'not every H is B' is true, then 'every A is B' is true, and it was 'every B is C'.
And if the antecedent of the conditional is particular affirmative, then there result from it and the disjunction two other conjunctions, namely those where the parts of the disjunction are universal negatives or particular affirmatives.
And if the conditional is particular affirmative, then there result from it and the disjunction six other conjunctions parallel to these six.
And if the conditional is universal negative, then there result from it and the disjunction six other conjunctions parallel to the first six, and four others where the disjunction is particular affirmative, so consider them.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the fourth figure (al-shakl al-rabi') in conditional-disjunctive syllogisms (al-qiyasat al-shartiyyat al-munfasilat). The author begins by defining the structure: when the subject of the antecedent (muqaddam) of the conditional is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction (ajza' al-infisal), the syllogism takes the form of the fourth figure. He then lists the conditions for a valid production: the conditional cannot be particular negative, and if it is affirmative, the antecedent cannot be particular negative; the disjunction must be affirmative; and it must be universal if the conditional is particular. These conditions ensure the logical validity of the resulting conclusion. The total number of productive conjunctions (qara'in) is twenty-two. The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional, but its quantity depends on the original conditional: if affirmative, the conclusion is particular; if universal negative, the conclusion is universal affirmative. The author then illustrates with examples using letters as variables (A, B, H, etc.), showing four specific conjunctions with different quantities and qualities of the disjunction parts. He explains that the first four conjunctions each yield a conclusion of the same form, because conversions (aks) can be applied to the particular negatives to obtain universal affirmatives. Further, he notes additional conjunctions when the antecedent is particular affirmative or when the conditional is particular affirmative or universal negative, but he leaves the enumeration to the reader (fai'tabirha). The style is typical of logical matn and sharh, compressing many details into concise conditions and examples. The passage is crucial for understanding how to combine conditional and disjunctive premises in the fourth figure to produce valid conclusions, a topic often treated in advanced Islamic logic.

Enumeration of Mixed Syllogism Moods from Conditional and Disjunctive Premises
Author enumerates valid combinations of conditional and disjunctive premises in mixed syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the valid moods of mixed syllogisms formed from a conditional and a disjunctive premise, based on the quantity and quality of the conditional.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms between conditionals and disjunctives have many valid moods determined by quality and quantity.

This passage from Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq systematically lists the valid conjunctions (mixed syllogisms) formed from a conditional premise and a disjunctive premise. Understanding this classification reveals the richness of logical inference beyond simple categorical syllogisms, which is essential for complex arguments in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Recognize that mixed syllogisms (اقترانات) combine a conditional (متصل) premise, e.g., 'if A then B', and a disjunctive (منفصل) premise, e.g., 'either A or B'. Note their logical forms.
2
Understand the base case
The author starts with a universal negative conditional and a disjunctive. Trace the inference: if the conditional's antecedent is false, the consequent is true, and vice versa. This establishes a pattern for enumeration.
3
Track the variations
The passage specifies how the number of valid conjunctions changes with the conditional's quantity and quality: for particular affirmative conditional, two additional conjunctions; for universal negative conditional, six parallel to first six plus four more when disjunctive is particular affirmative.
The total number of moods can be derived by combining these cases.
4
Apply logical notation
The author uses Arabic letters (أ, ب, ج, هـ) as term placeholders. Map these to concrete terms in an Islamic science example to internalize the patterns.

The Fourth Iqṭirān: Compound Syllogism with Disjunctive and Conditional Premises
Author describes the fourth compound syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the fourth iqṭirān and validate whether a given conclusion follows from its premises.

Core Idea
The fourth iqṭirān replaces one disjunct with the conditional consequent that entails it.

Compound syllogisms combine different premise types. In this lesson, we examine the fourth conjunction: a universal negative disjunctive (e.g., 'never either A or B') paired with a universal affirmative conditional (e.g., 'if A then C'). The challenge is to see how the conditional's consequent inherits the disjunctive opposition. This logical move appears in Islamic legal reasoning when eliminating options.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Locate the first premise: universal negative disjunctive ('dā'iman laysa immā B J wa immā D R'). This means B J and D R are always both false. The second premise is a universal affirmative conditional ('kull mā kāna D R fa-H A') meaning if something is D R then it is H A.
The disjunctive is exclusive: no middle ground.
2
Trace the logical link
Notice that the conditional's antecedent (D R) is one of the disjuncts. The conditional says H A follows from D R. Since D R is always false (from first premise), what can we say about H A?
If D R is false, H A could still be true or false if the conditional is true. But the reasoning uses the idea of 'inād' (opposition).
3
Understand the justification
The author says there is no constant opposition (inād) between B J and D R (they are not opposed because the disjunction says both are false, not that they oppose each other). And H A is concomitant (mulāzim) with D R. Therefore, there is no opposition between B J and H A either, meaning the conclusion 'always not either B J or H A' follows.
The reasoning relies on the fact that if two things are not opposed, and one entails the other, the entailment preserves non-opposition.
4
Formalize the conclusion
Given premises: (1) ¬(B J ∨ D R) [universal negation] and (2) ∀x (D R(x) → H A(x)). The conclusion is ¬(B J ∨ H A). Check: Is this valid? Since D R implies H A, if H A were true, we cannot directly infer D R (affirming the consequent is invalid). But the intuitive reasoning here assumes that the disjunction 'either B J or H A' would be false only if both are false. Since D R is always false, and H A follows from D R, we know D R is false, so H A could be false or true. However, if H A were true, it does not force D R to be true. So the conclusion as stated may not be logically valid in standard logic; it might rely on additional assumptions about the nature of opposition (inād) in this context.
This is a caution: the iqṭirān is valid within the framework of classical Islamic logic where 'opposition' is a stronger relation than mere negation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth type of compound syllogism (iqṭirān) where the disjunctive premise is universal negative and the conditional premise is universal affirmative. The author illustrates with variables and explains the logical reasoning: since the conditional's antecedent (D R) is disjunctively opposed to B J in the disjunctive, and H A follows from D R, it follows that H A is also opposed to B J, hence the conclusion. The explanation relies on the notion of 'opposition' (inād) between the disjuncts and the conditional consequence.

The Fifth Conjunction: Disjunctive with Particular Conditional
Author presents the fifth iqṭirān with a universal negative disjunctive and a particular affirmative conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of the fifth iqṭirān and determine its conclusion given a universal negative disjunctive and a particular affirmative conditional.

Core Idea
A particular conditional changes the conclusion from universal to particular in disjunctive compound syllogisms.

Building on the previous conjunction, the fifth iqṭirān preserves the universal negative disjunctive premise but replaces the universal conditional with a particular affirmative one. This subtle change transforms the conclusion from a universal negative disjunction to a particular negative disjunction, demonstrating how quantifier shifts affect syllogistic outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
The first premise is a universal negative disjunctive, remaining unchanged from the previous iqṭirān. It asserts that at all times, it is not the case that either B is J or H is A (i.e., both are always false together).
The exact form is not given, but context implies a universal negative disjunction.
2
Identify the conditional premise
The second premise is a particular affirmative conditional: 'Sometimes if D is R then H is A' (qad yakūn idhā D R fa- H A). This asserts that there exists at least one instance where the implication holds.
This contrasts with a universal conditional used in the previous type.
3
Determine the conclusion
The conclusion is a particular negative disjunction: 'Not always either B is J or H is A' (laysa dā'iman immā B J wa immā H A). This negates a universal affirmative disjunction, so it is equivalent to 'Sometimes neither B J nor H A'.
The conclusion is weaker than a universal negative disjunction.
4
Understand the logical mechanism
The particular conditional only guarantees the implication in some cases. Therefore, the disjunction's universal claim (always either one of them) cannot be maintained; it is negated to a particular.
This shows the impact of quantifier interaction in compound syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The fifth conjunction: the disjunctive remains as before, except that the conditional is particular affirmative, like our saying: 'And it may be that if D is R then H is A' and its conclusion is 'Not always either B is J or H is A'.

Commentary
This passage describes the fifth type of compound syllogism building on the previous one. The disjunctive premise remains universal negative, but the conditional premise is particular affirmative ('qad yakūn idhā ... fa-...'). The conclusion becomes a particular negative disjunctive ('laysa dā'iman immā B J wa immā H A'), which is the denial of a universal affirmative disjunction. This shows how changing the quantity of the conditional affects the conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Conditions for First-Figure Type Compound Syllogism
Author states conditions and conclusion form for first-figure type compound syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid first-figure compound syllogism and derive the conclusion form.

Core Idea
The first-figure compound syllogism mirrors categorical syllogism conditions with a uniform disjunction.

This passage details the specific conditions under which a compound syllogism—combining a conditional and a disjunctive premise—yields a valid conclusion. The author maps its structure onto the first figure of categorical syllogisms, then enumerates the quantity/quality restrictions. Understanding these conditions is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common term's role
Note that the common term (ishtirak) is the subject of the conditional's antecedent and appears as predicate in the disjunctive parts. This determines the figure.
If the common term were the predicate of the antecedent, the figure would differ.
2
Recognize the first-figure analogy
When the common term is predicate in the disjuncts, the structure mirrors the first figure: the middle term is subject in one premise and predicate in the other.
This analogy allows borrowing conditions from categorical syllogisms.
3
State the conditions for the conditional
The conditional must not be particular negative. If affirmative, the antecedent must be universal; if negative, the antecedent must be particular.
These conditions ensure proper distribution of terms.
4
State the conditions for the disjunction
The disjunction must be affirmative real (ḥaqīqiyya) or inclusive-or (māni'at al-khuluww dūn al-jam'), and must be universal if the conditional is affirmative.
Real disjunction means exactly one true; inclusive-or allows both true but not both false.
5
Derive the conclusion form
The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional: its antecedent is the conditional's consequent, and its consequent is the result of conjoining the antecedent with the disjunction. The quantity varies: particular if conditional affirmative, universal if negative.
The thirty-two productive conjunctions arise from variations in quantity/quality of the disjuncts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاشتراك بجزء المقدم فإما أن يكون موضوعه أو محموله، فإن كان موضوع المقدم فإما أن يكون محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال أو موضوعاً فيها، فإن كان محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال فهو على هيئة الشكل الأول، فليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية وشرط إنتاجه أن لا تكون المتصلة جزئية سالبة، وإن كانت موجبة أن يكون المقدم كلياً، وإن كانت سالبة أن يكون المقدم جزئياً، وأن تكون المنفصلة موجبة حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن تكون كليّة إن كانت المتصلة موجبة وقرائنه المنتجة اثنان وثلاثون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل موجبة متصلة، مقدمها تالي المتصلة، وتاليها نتيجة اقتران المقدم بالمنفصلة، إلا أنه إذا كانت المتصلة موجبة كانت النتيجة جزئية موجبة وإن كانت سالبة كانت النتيجة كلية موجبة.
And if the commonality is in part of the antecedent, then it is either its subject or its predicate. If it is the subject of the antecedent, then it is either a predicate in the parts of the disjunction or a subject in them. If it is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the first figure. So let the hypothesis be that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality. The condition for its productivity is that the conditional must not be particular negative. If the conditional is affirmative, the antecedent must be universal; if negative, the antecedent must be particular. The disjunction must be affirmative real (ḥaqīqiyya) or exclusive of void (māni'at al-khuluww) not of conjunction (al-jam'), and it must be universal if the conditional is affirmative. Its productive conjunctions are thirty-two. The conclusion in all is an affirmative conditional, whose antecedent is the consequent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the antecedent with the disjunction. However, if the conditional is affirmative, the conclusion is particular affirmative; if negative, the conclusion is universal affirmative.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage introduces a major sub-section: compound syllogisms where the shared term is the subject of the conditional's antecedent and appears as predicate in the disjuncts. The author identifies this as having the structure of the first figure of categorical syllogisms. He then lays down conditions for productivity: the conditional must not be particular negative; quantity/quality of the antecedent must match the conditional's quality; the disjunction must be real or exclusive-of-void, and universal if the conditional is affirmative. The total number of productive conjunctions under these uniform disjuncts is thirty-two. The conclusion is always a conditional with the original conditional's consequent as antecedent and a new consequent formed by combining the antecedent with the disjunction. A significant nuance: if the conditional is affirmative, the conclusion is particular; if negative, it is universal.

Key Terms
اشتراك ishtirāk: the common term between the conditional and disjunctive premises
منفصلة موجبة حقيقية munfaṣila mūjiba ḥaqīqiyya: a real affirmative exclusive disjunction
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع māni'at al-khuluww dūn al-jam': an inclusive-or disjunction that prohibits both false but allows both true

Key Takeaway
The first-figure compound syllogism requires the conditional not to be particular negative, the disjunction to be real or exclusive-of-void, and the conclusion varies in quantity based on the conditional's quality.

First Conjunction: Two Universal Affirmative Premises
Author gives the first example of a productive conjunction under the first-figure-type with two universal affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the compound syllogism (disjunctive + conditional) and verify its conclusion using conversion ('aks).

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms combine disjunctives and conditionals to derive new truths via conversion.

In the first productive conjunction under the first-figure-type, the author integrates a universal affirmative disjunction with a universal affirmative conditional. The conclusion is not universal but particular affirmative conditional, derived through logical conversion ('aks). This lesson walks through the steps of the compound syllogism, showing how premises combine to yield a certain conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
Extract the disjunction: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J'. Extract the conditional: 'Everything that is J A is H R'. These are the inputs.
The disjunction is universal affirmative; the conditional is universal affirmative.
2
Apply conversion to the conditional
The author uses 'aks to transform the conditional: from 'Every J is A is H' we get 'If H is R then every J is A'. This conversion is essential for the deduction.
Conversion here is not the standard categorical conversion but a form of contraposition or transposition.
3
Combine disjunction and converted conditional
With the converted conditional, we can infer: 'If H is R, then always either every B is A or every D is A'. This is the conclusion.
The conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional ('qad yakūn').
4
Check the conclusion's quantity
The conclusion begins 'qad yakūn' indicating particularity, not universality. This arises because the conditional's antecedent is particular in effect.
Do not mistakenly expect a universal conclusion from universal premises in compound syllogisms.
5
Formalize the compound syllogism
Represent the argument abstractly: Disjunction (∀x (Bx ∨ Dx)) and Conditional (∀x (Jx → (Hx → Rx))) yield a particular conditional conclusion.
This formalization aids in seeing the logical structure clearly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة، والمقدم كلي موجب، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج") و(كل ما كان "ج" "أ" في "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر") فـ (دائماً إما كل "ب" "أ" وإما كل "د" "أ" لأنه عكس كل ما كان كل "ج" "أ" فـ "هـ" ",") (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فكل "ج" "أ") وإذا كان ( كل "" "" وكان دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج") فقد لزم المطلوب.
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, with the parts of the disjunction being universal affirmatives and the antecedent universal affirmative, like our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J' and 'Everything that is J A is H R' and its conclusion is 'It may be that if H is R then always either every B is A or every D is A' because it is the converse of 'Every J is A is H' and 'If H is R then every J is A' and if 'every ...' and 'always either every B is J or every D is J' then the required follows.

Commentary
This passage presents the first concrete iqṭirān under the new conditions. The disjunctive is universal affirmative ('dā'iman immā kull B J aw kull D J') and the conditional is universal affirmative ('kull mā kāna J A fa-H R'). The conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional ('qad yakūn idhā H R fa-dā'iman immā kull B A aw kull D A'). The author explains the reasoning: from the conditional we can convert or contrapose to get 'if H R then every J is A', and combining with the original disjunction gives the required conclusion. Note the use of 'aks (conversion) in the explanation.

Second Conjunction: Universal Negative Antecedent
Author describes the second iqṭirān with a universal negative conditional antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second iqtiran and construct a conclusion with both disjuncts negative given a universal negative conditional antecedent.

Core Idea
Negativity in the antecedent forces negativity into both disjuncts of the conclusion.

In compound syllogisms, the quality of the antecedent in the conditional premise determines the quality of the disjuncts in the conclusion. The second iqtiran variant shows what happens when the antecedent is universal negative. By examining this pattern, you will understand how to transform a negative conditional into a conditional with a disjunctive consequent where both disjuncts are negative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional antecedent
Locate the phrase 'كل ما كان لا شيء من ج أ' – this is a universal negative proposition serving as the antecedent. It states that no J is A.
The antecedent is negative (سالب).
2
Recognize the consequent and conclusion structure
The consequent is 'هـ ر' (H is R). The conclusion is a conditional with a disjunctive consequent: 'إذا كان هـ ر فدائماً إما لا شيء من ب أ أو لا شيء من ج أ'. Note that both disjuncts are universal negatives.
3
Trace the logical transformation
The iqtiran pairs the conditional with an implicit disjunctive premise. Because the antecedent is negative, both disjuncts in the conclusion become negative. This contrasts with the first iqtiran where one disjunct would be affirmative.
The negativity carries through the conjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المقدم من المتصلة كلي سالب كقولنا: (وكل ما كان لا شيء من "ج" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ج" "أ").
The second conjunction: the conjunction remains the same, except that the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, like our saying: 'And everything that is not any J is A is H R' so 'It may be that if H is R then always either not any B is A or not any J is A'.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage presents the second iqṭirān variant. The disjunctive remains universal positive, but the conditional's antecedent is universal negative ('lā shay' min J A'). The conclusion becomes a conditional with a disjunctive consequent where both disjuncts are universal negatives ('lā shay' min B A' and 'lā shay' min J A'). This demonstrates how changing the quality of the antecedent in the conditional forces the disjuncts to become negative.

Key Terms
مقدم كلي سالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Third and Fourth Conjunctions in Conditional Syllogisms
Author defines two conjunctions with particular conditional and universal antecedents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third and fourth iqṭirān forms and determine the conclusion when the conditional is particular affirmative.

Core Idea
When the conditional is particular, the conclusion mirrors the antecedent's quality while staying particular.

In the previous lessons, we saw the first two conjunctions with a universal conditional. Now the author turns to cases where the conditional is particular affirmative. The antecedent's quantity and quality change, and the conclusion follows a pattern. This lesson will walk you through the third and fourth iqṭirān.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional type
Notice that the conditional (muttasila) is particular affirmative (جُزْئِيَّةٌ مُوجِبَةٌ). This means it has the form 'Sometimes if X then Y'.
This sets the quantity of the conclusion: it will be particular.
2
Examine the antecedent in the third iqṭirān
The text says 'المقدم الثالث: كلي موجب'. So the minor premise is a universal affirmative categorical about the antecedent, e.g., 'Every A is X'.
Since it's affirmative, the conclusion will be affirmative.
3
Examine the antecedent in the fourth iqṭirān
The text says 'وفي الرابع كلي سالب'. So the minor premise is a universal negative categorical, e.g., 'No A is X'.
Since it's negative, the conclusion will be negative.
4
Derive the conclusion by analogy
The author states 'النتيجة في الثالث كالأول، وفي الرابع كالثالث'. This means the third yields a particular affirmative conditional (like the first), and the fourth yields a particular negative conditional (like the third but negative).
So the conclusion's quantity is particular, quality matches the minor premise.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Create a concrete syllogism using the pattern. For third: 'Sometimes if a body is created, it has a cause. Every substance is a body. Therefore, sometimes if a substance is created, it has a cause.' For fourth: 'Sometimes if a body is created, it has a cause. No accident is a body. Therefore, sometimes if an accident is created, it does not have a cause.'
These examples show how the conclusion follows the pattern.

Source Text

الاقتران الثالث والرابع الاقتران بحاله إلا أن المتصلة جزئية موجبة المقدم الثالث: كلي موجب، وفي الرابع كلي سالب والنتيجة في الثالث كالأول، وفي الرابع كالثالث.
The third and fourth conjunctions: the conjunction remains the same, except that the conditional is particular affirmative. The third: the antecedent is universal affirmative, and the fourth: universal negative. The conclusion in the third is like the first, and in the fourth like the third.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage succinctly states two more iqṭirān where the conditional is particular affirmative. The third iqṭirān has a universal affirmative antecedent, and the fourth has a universal negative antecedent. The author says the conclusion for the third is 'like the first' (which was a particular affirmative conditional), and for the fourth 'like the third' (which would be a particular negative conditional? But 'like the third' is ambiguous; it likely means like the third in form but with negativity from the second). This shows that with a particular conditional, the conclusion inherits particularity while the antecedent's quality mirrors the earlier cases.

Key Terms

مقدم كلي موجب

The Fifth Conjunction: Universal Negative Conditional with Particular Negative Antecedent
Fifth iqṭirān with universal negative conditional and particular negative antecedent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the fifth iqṭirān and verify its conclusion using contraposition.

Core Idea
The fifth iqṭirān uses contraposition to derive a universal conclusion from a particular premise.

This lesson explores a compound syllogism called the fifth conjunction, which combines a universal negative conditional with a particular negative antecedent. By applying contraposition (ʿaks), the author derives a universal affirmative disjunction. Understanding this transformation is key to mastering complex syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
The conditional is universal negative: 'It is never that if some J is not A then H is R.' Note that the antecedent is particular negative (some J is not A).
The universal negative conditional negates the entire implication.
2
Apply contraposition (ʿaks) to the conditional
From 'It is never that if some J is not A then H is R', derive its contrapositive: 'It is never that if H is R then some J is A.' This reverses the order and negates both parts.
Contraposition preserves truth for universal negative conditionals.
3
Derive the universal affirmative from the contrapositive
From 'It is never that if H is R then some J is A', it follows that every J is A. Because if some J were not A, then the conditional would be true, contradicting the universal negative.
This step assumes the logical equivalence between the contrapositive and the original.
4
Combine with the disjunctive premise
The disjunctive premise is 'Always either every B is J or every D is J'. Using the derived 'every J is A', we can replace J with A in the disjuncts, yielding 'Always either every B is A or every J is A'.
The disjunction is universal affirmative, and the substitution is valid because J is coextensive with A (every J is A, and from the original conditional, every A is J? Actually, we only derived every J is A, not the converse, but the text concludes the disjunction with 'kull J A', so it works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الخامس المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة كلية سالبة، والمقدم فيها جزئي سالب كقولنا: ليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ج" ليس "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته (فدائماً إما كل "ب" "أ" وإما كل "ج" "أ") لأنه إذا صدق (ليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ج" ليس "أ" فـ "هـ" ",") فعكسه (ليس البتة إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فبعض "ج" "أ") ويلزم من ذلك أن يكون كل "ج" "أ") وكان دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج") ويلزم منه المطلوب.
The fifth conjunction: the disjunctive remains as before, except that the conditional is universal negative, and its antecedent is particular negative, like our saying: 'It is never that if some J is not A then H is R' and its conclusion 'Always either every B is A or every J is A' because if 'It is never that if some J is not A then H is R' is true, then its converse is 'It is never that if H is R then some J is A' and it follows from that that every J is A, and it was always either every B is J or every D is J, so the required follows.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage shows a more complex iqṭirān involving a universal negative conditional with a particular negative antecedent ('laysa al-battata idhā ba'd J laysa A fa-H R'). The conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction ('dā'iman immā kull B A aw kull J A'). The author explains the reasoning using conversion ('aks'): from the original conditional, we derive its contrapositive, which forces that if H R then some J is A, and then from the original disjunction we get the required universal affirmative disjunction. This is a good example of how logical transformations are used within compound syllogisms.

Key Terms
عكس
اقتران
متصلة كلية سالبة
مقدم جزئي سالب

The Sixth Conjunction: Particular Affirmative Antecedent
Author describes the sixth iqṭirān with a particular affirmative antecedent in a universal negative conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the conclusion of the sixth iqṭirān and identify its distinguishing features.

Core Idea
A particular affirmative antecedent in a universal negative conditional forces a universal conditional conclusion.

The sixth iqṭirān extends the pattern of conditional conjunctions by introducing a particular antecedent. Understanding how a negative conditional with a particular antecedent yields a universal conclusion is crucial for mastering compound syllogisms in Islamic logic. This lesson walks you through the form and its derivation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional type
The conditional is universal negative: 'It is never that if some J is A then H is R'. Note the negation applies to the whole conditional.
2
Recognize the antecedent
The antecedent is particular affirmative: 'Some J is A' (juz'ī mūjib). This is the only difference from previous conjunctions.
3
Understand the conclusion structure
The conclusion is a universal conditional: 'Everything that is H R is always either every B is A or every D is A'. This is a compound conditional conclusion.
4
Trace the derivation
If H R is true, the original conditional implies that no J is A (by modus tollens). Combining with the original disjunction (not fully shown here) forces the universal conclusion.
This step involves an implicit compound syllogism.
5
Apply to a real example
Substitute Islamic terms: Let J = 'intoxicant', A = 'forbidden', H = 'date beverage', R = 'intoxicating'. Then the universal negative conditional says: 'It is never that if some intoxicants are forbidden then date beverages are intoxicating.' The conclusion: 'Every date beverage that is intoxicating is such that either every intoxicant is forbidden or every date beverage is intoxicating.' This shows the pattern.
This instantiation uses Kalam/Fiqh categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران السادس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المقدم جزئي موجب، كقولنا: (وليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ج" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر" ونتيجته (كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما كل "ب" "أ" وإما كل "د" "أ") لأنه إذا صدق ("هـ" "ر") صدق (لا شيء من "ج" "أ") ، والمطلوب إذ ذاك لازم.
The sixth conjunction: the conjunction remains the same, except that the antecedent is particular affirmative, like our saying: 'And it is never that if some J is A then H is R' and its conclusion 'Everything that is H R is always either every B is A or every D is A' because if 'H is R' is true, then 'Nothing of J is A' is true, and the required follows.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth iqṭirān. The conditional is universal negative with a particular affirmative antecedent ('ba'd J A'). The conclusion is a universal conditional: 'kull mā kāna H R fa-dā'iman immā kull B A aw kull D A'. The reasoning: if H R is true, then from the conditional we get that no J is A (since the conditional denies that any J is A implies H R), and combining with the original disjunction yields the conclusion. This shows a strong inference from a negative conditional with a positive antecedent.

Key Terms
جزئي موجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Particular affirmative conditional
B) Universal affirmative conditional
C) Universal negative conditional
D) Particular negative conditional

Key Takeaway
The sixth iqṭirān shows that a universal negative conditional with a particular affirmative antecedent yields a universal conditional conclusion.

Third Figure Compound Syllogisms and Generalization
Author generalizes the pattern to sixteen more conjunctions and introduces the third figure shape.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for productivity of a third-figure compound syllogism and extend the pattern to sixteen additional conjunction types.

Core Idea
The third figure of categorical logic applies analogically to conditional-disjunctive syllogisms.

In our previous discussions, the middle term served as a predicate in one premise and subject in the other. Now, when the subject of the conditional antecedent is also a subject in the disjuncts, we enter the territory of the third figure. The author lists the conditions for such syllogisms to yield a conclusion and challenges you to consider sixteen further combinations based on variations in quantity and quality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components
The compound syllogism consists of a conditional (muttaṣil) and a disjunctive (munfaṣil). In the third figure, the subject of the conditional’s antecedent is the subject in each part of the disjunction.
This matches the categorical third figure where the middle term is subject in both premises.
2
List the conditions for productivity
The author specifies: the conditional must not be particular negative; the disjunction must be affirmative; if the conditional is affirmative, the disjunction must be universal; and the disjunction must be real (ḥaqīqiyya) or exclusive-of-void without conjunction.
These ensure the syllogism yields a valid conclusion.
3
Consider the sixteen additional conjunctions
Beyond uniform universal affirmatives, the disjunct parts can be particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative. Each combination yields a distinct conjunction – sixteen in total. Derive them using the conditions above.
The author does not list them; you are expected to work them out.
4
Compare with categorical third figure
In categorical logic, the third figure has the middle term as subject in both premises. Here, the role is analogous: the common term (subject of antecedent) is subject in each disjunct.
This analogy helps transfer your knowledge from categorical to compound syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج عندما إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة كلية، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، وهي ستة عشر اقتراناً أخرى، فعليك باعتبارها. وأما إن كان الموضوع من المقدم موضوعاً في أجزاء الانفصال فهو هيئة الشكل الثالث، وليكن الفرض أيضاً إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية - وأنت تعرف ما وراء ذلك - وشرطه في الإنتاج أن لا تكون المتصلة جزئية سالبة، وأن تكون المنفصلة موجبة، وأن تكون كليّة إن كانت المتصلة موجبة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، أو
And on this basis, the productivity is not hidden when the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative universal, or universal negative, or particular negative; and they are sixteen additional conjunctions, so you should consider them. And if the subject of the antecedent is a subject in the parts of the disjunction, then it is the form of the third figure. Let the hypothesis also be that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality - and you know what lies beyond that - and its condition for productivity is that the conditional must not be particular negative, the disjunction must be affirmative, and must be universal if the conditional is affirmative, and must be real (ḥaqīqiyya) or exclusive of void without conjunction, and if the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative, or...

Commentary
This concluding passage generalizes the previous discussion. The author states that there are sixteen more iqṭirān when the disjuncts are not uniformly universal affirmative but include particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative. He then transitions to a new scenario: when the common term (subject of the antecedent) is a subject (not predicate) in the disjuncts, this corresponds to the third figure of categorical syllogisms. He outlines conditions similar to before but emphasizes that the disjunction must be universal if the conditional is affirmative, etc. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating the discussion continues on the next page. This provides a framework for the student to derive all possible conjunctions.

Patterns of Compound Conditional Syllogisms (Iqtirans 2-6)
Author enumerates four specific iqtirans and generalizes to sixteen more.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and validate the four specific compound syllogisms (iqtirans 2-6) and extend the pattern to generate sixteen more valid forms.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms combine conditional and disjunctive premises; only specific quality/quantity combinations yield valid conclusions.

This passage extends our understanding of compound conditional syllogisms (iqtirans) by detailing four specific variations. By mastering these patterns, you will be able to construct and validate complex arguments that combine conditional and disjunctive premises, a skill essential for rigorous theological and juristic reasoning. The author uses letters as placeholders, but we will apply these forms to Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Each iqtiran consists of one conditional (mutasila) premise and one disjunctive (munfasila) premise. Note the quantity and quality of the conditional's antecedent and the disjunctive's parts.
The disjunctive may be 'exclusive' or 'inclusive' based on context.
2
Extract the specific forms
For iqtiran 2: conditional antecedent = universal negative; disjunctive = two universal negatives. For 3 & 4: conditional is particular affirmative with universal affirmative/negative antecedent. For 5 & 6: conditional is universal negative with particular negative/affirmative antecedent.
Write each in formal notation: e.g., (∀x)(¬Jx → ¬Ax ?) but the text uses "if...then" and "either...or".
3
Derive the conclusion
Follow the author's steps or your own: check if the conclusion follows logically. For instance, in iqtiran 5, the conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction, derived via conversion and composition.
The derivation may use immediate inferences like conversion.
4
Generalize the pattern
The author states that by varying the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts (from universal affirmative to particular negative), there are sixteen more valid iqtirans. Try to list some.
This pattern suggests a combinatorial approach to syllogistic validity.

تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية. فقد لزم المطلوب. الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المقدم من المتصلة كلي سالب كقولنا: (وكل ما كان لا شيء من "ج" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "ج" "أ"). الاقتران الثالث والرابع الاقتران بحاله إلا أن المتصلة جزئية موجبة المقدم الثالث: كلي موجب، وفي الرابع كلي سالب والنتيجة في الثالث كالأول، وفي الرابع كالثالث. الاقتران الخامس المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة كلية سالبة، والمقدم فيها جزئي سالب كقولنا: ليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ج" ليس "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته (فدائماً إما كل "ب" "أ" وإما كل "ج" "أ") لأنه إذا صدق (ليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ج" ليس "أ" فـ "هـ" ",") فعكسه (ليس البتة إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فبعض "ج" "أ") ويلزم من ذلك أن يكون كل "ج" "أ") وكان دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج") ويلزم منه المطلوب. الاقتران السادس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المقدم جزئي موجب، كقولنا: (وليس البتة إذا كان بعض "ج" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته (كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما كل "ب" "أ" وإما كل "د" "أ") لأنه إذا صدق ("هـ" "ر") صدق (لا شيء من "ج" "أ")، والمطلوب إذ ذاك لازم. وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج عندما إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة كلية، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، وهي ستة عشر اقتراناً أخرى، فعليك باعتبارها.
Continuation of the first part: on defining the forms of conditional compound syllogisms. So the required conclusion follows. The second conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, as we say: (And everything that is such that nothing of 'J' is 'A', then 'H' is 'R') so (It may be that when 'H' is 'R', then always either nothing of 'B' is 'A' or nothing of 'J' is 'A'). The third and fourth conjunctions: the conjunction in its state, except that the conditional is particular affirmative; the antecedent in the third is universal affirmative, and in the fourth universal negative, and the conclusion in the third is like the first, and in the fourth like the second. The fifth conjunction: the disjunctive in its state, except that the conditional is universal negative, and the antecedent in it is particular negative, as we say: (Not at all if some 'J' is not 'A', then 'H' is 'R') and its conclusion (then always either every 'B' is 'A' or every 'J' is 'A') because if (Not at all if some 'J' is not 'A', then 'H' is 'R') is true, its converse (Not at all if 'H' is 'R', then some 'J' is 'A') and it follows from that that every 'J' is 'A', and it was always either every 'B' is 'J' or every 'D' is 'J', and from that the required conclusion follows. The sixth conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the antecedent is particular affirmative, as we say: (Not at all if some 'J' is 'A', then 'H' is 'R') and its conclusion (Everything that is 'H' is 'R', then always either every 'B' is 'A' or every 'D' is 'A') because if ('H' is 'R') is true, (Nothing of 'J' is 'A') is true, and the required conclusion is then necessary. And on this basis, production is not hidden when the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative, universal, or universal negative, or particular negative, and they are another sixteen conjunctions, so you must consider them.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of four specific types of compound syllogisms (iqtirans) that involve conditional (mutasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) premises, followed by a generalization to sixteen others. The author follows a pattern: each iqtiran specifies a variation of the premises and then provides a formula for the conclusion. The second iqtiran takes the conditional antecedent as universal negative; its conclusion is a disjunction of two universal negatives. The third and fourth iqtirans involve a particular affirmative conditional with either universal affirmative or universal negative antecedent, with conclusions mirroring those of the first and second iqtirans respectively. The fifth iqtiran has a universal negative conditional with a particular negative antecedent; the conclusion is a universal affirmative disjunction, derived through conversion and composition. The sixth iqtiran has a particular affirmative antecedent leading to a conclusion that forces the negation condition. Finally, the author notes that by varying the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts, one can generate sixteen more valid conjunctions, which the reader is to work out. Throughout, the examples use letters (A, B, J, H, R) as placeholders for terms, as is standard in Arabic logic. The passage is technical and assumes familiarity with the first figure and basic rules of conversion.

Key Takeaway
The second to sixth iqtirans show specific valid combinations of conditional and disjunctive premises, and the pattern extends to sixteen more by varying the quantity and quality of disjunctive parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Then derive the conclusion that it is not ribawi.' Check if this follows the pattern.

Then conclude 'always either the witness is reliable or the testimony is accepted.' Validate or invalidate using the rules in the passage.

Third Figure Compound Syllogisms: Conditions and 48 Conjunctions
Defines conditions and enumerates 48 valid conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid third-figure compound syllogism and determine whether a given conjunction conforms to the productive patterns enumerated by the author.

Core Idea
The third figure's 48 productive conjunctions arise from systematic variation in quantity and quality.

This passage from al-Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq presents a detailed classification of valid compound syllogisms in the third figure. The author lays out precise conditions for productivity and then enumerates the patterns, demonstrating the exhaustive nature of the logical system. We will walk through the conditions and see how the 48 conjunctions emerge through variations in the quantity and quality of the premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining condition for the third figure
The third figure occurs when the subject of the antecedent (muqaddam) in the conditional premise is also a subject in the parts of the disjunctive premise. Locate this condition at the start of the passage: 'إذا كان الموضوع من المقدم موضوعاً في أجزاء الانفصال'.
This distinguishes the third figure from the first two.
2
Extract the four main conditions for productivity
The author gives conditions: (1) the conditional must not be particular negative; (2) the disjunctive must be affirmative; (3) if the conditional is affirmative, the disjunctive must be universal; (4) the disjunctive must be 'real' (haqiqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'). Find these in the Arabic: 'وشرطه في الإنتاج أن لا تكون المتصلة جزئية سالبة، وأن تكون المنفصلة موجبة، وأن تكون كليّة إن كانت المتصلة موجبة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع'.
These ensure the syllogism is productive (intaj).
3
Recognize the pattern leading to 48 conjunctions
The conditions further vary depending on the quantity/quality of the disjunctive parts and the conditional antecedent. The author systematically goes through combinations: first with universal affirmative disjunctive parts and universal affirmative/negative antecedent, then particular equivalents, etc. The total is 48. Follow the enumeration: 'وقرائنه المنتجة ثمانية وأربعون اقتراناً'.
The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional, as stated: 'والنتيجة في الكل موجبة متصلة'.
4
Examine the four sample conjunctions
The author provides four explicit examples with Arabic variables. The first: both premises universal affirmative, conclusion is particular affirmative. The second: antecedent universal negative, conclusion particular negative. The third and fourth mirror the first two but with particular antecedent/consequent. Read them carefully: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين... etc.
These illustrate the pattern for the first set of four.
5
Generalize to the remaining 36 conjunctions
The author then says: if the disjunctive remains the same and the conditional becomes particular affirmative, we get four parallel conjunctions; if the conditional becomes universal negative, another four. Further variations of the disjunctive parts yield 36 more. So the total is 4 + 4 + 4 + 36 = 48. The phrase 'فقد يأتلف منها أربعة اقترانات أخرى' and 'سته وثلاثون اقتراناً أخرى' indicate this.
The pattern is based on multiplying the four base cases by different quantity/quality options.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الموضوع من المقدم موضوعاً في أجزاء الانفصال فهو هيئة الشكل الثالث، وليكن الفرض أيضاً إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية - وأنت تعرف ما وراء ذلك - وشرطه في الإنتاج أن لا تكون المتصلة جزئية سالبة، وأن تكون المنفصلة موجبة، وأن تكون كليّة إن كانت المتصلة موجبة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وإن كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، أو كليات سالبة أن يكون المقدم من المتصلة الموجبة كلياً، ومن السالبة جزئياً، وإن كان المقدم من الموجبة المتصلة جزئياً ومن السالبة كلياً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة أو جزئيات سالبة، وقرائنه المنتجة ثمانية وأربعون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل موجبة متصلة على ما تقدم في الأول، وعلى ذلك التفصيل: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين أجزاء المنفصلة كلية موجبة، والمقدم كلي موجب، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وأما كل "ب" "د") و(كل ما كان كل "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما بعض "ج" "أ" وأما بعض "د" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المقدم كلي سالب كقولنا: (كل كان لا شيء من "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فإما بعض "ج" ليس وإما بعض "د" ليس "أ"). الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن التالي جزئي موجب كقولنا وكل ما كان بعض "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته كالأول. الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المقدم جزئي سالب كقولنا: (وكل ما كان ليس كل "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته كالثاني. وإن كانت المنفصلة بحالها، والمتصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يأتلف منها أربعة اقترانات أخرى، موازية لهذه الأربعة. وإن كانت المنفصلة بحالها، والمتصلة كلية سالبة، فقد يأتلف عنها أيضاً أربعة اقترانات . وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، أو كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، والمتصلة كلية سالبة، وهي ستة وثلاثون اقتراناً أخرى، فاعتبرها.
And if the subject of the antecedent is a subject in the parts of the disjunction, then it is the form of the third figure, and let the assumption also be that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality - and you know what is beyond that - and its condition for productivity is that the conditional should not be particular negative, and the disjunctive should be affirmative, and it should be universal if the conditional is affirmative, and it should be real or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction, and if the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmatives, or universal negatives, then the antecedent from the affirmative conditional should be universal, and from the negative conditional particular, and if the antecedent from the affirmative conditional is particular and from the negative universal, then the parts of the disjunction should be universal affirmatives or particular negatives, and its productive conjunctions are forty-eight, and the conclusion in all is an affirmative conditional according to what preceded in the first, and according to that detail: the first conjunction from two universal affirmatives: the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmative, and the antecedent is universal affirmative, as we say: (Always either every 'B' is 'J' or every 'B' is 'D') and (Everything that is such that every 'B' is 'A', then 'H' is 'R') and its conclusion is (It may be that when 'H' is 'R', then always either some 'J' is 'A' or some 'D' is 'A'). The second conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the antecedent is universal negative, as we say: (Everything that is such that nothing of 'B' is 'A', then 'H' is 'R') so (It may be that when 'H' is 'R', then either some 'J' is not or some 'D' is not 'A'). The third conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the consequent is particular affirmative, as we say: (And everything that is such that some 'B' is 'A', then 'H' is 'R') and its conclusion is like the first. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the antecedent is particular negative, as we say: (And everything that is such that not every 'B' is 'A', then 'H' is 'R') and its conclusion is like the second. And if the disjunctive is in its state, and the conditional is particular affirmative, then four other conjunctions can be composed from them, parallel to these four. And if the disjunctive is in its state, and the conditional is universal negative, then also four conjunctions can be composed from them. And on this basis, production is not hidden if the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, or if the disjunctive is particular affirmative and the conditional is universal negative, and they are thirty-six other conjunctions, so consider them.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for valid compound syllogisms in the third figure, where the subject of the antecedent (muqaddam) is also a subject in the parts of the disjunction. The author lists several conditions: the conditional must not be particular negative; the disjunctive must be affirmative; if the conditional is affirmative, the disjunctive must be universal; the disjunctive must be 'real' (haqiqiyya) or exclusive of emptiness without conjunction (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'). Further conditions relate to the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts and the antecedent. The author then enumerates the productive conjunctions as 48 in total, with the conclusion always being an affirmative conditional. He provides four specific examples: the first conjunction (both premises universal affirmative) yields a conclusion with particular affirmatives; the second (universal negative antecedent) yields a conclusion with particular negatives; the third (particular affirmative consequent) mirrors the first; the fourth (particular negative antecedent) mirrors the second. Then, varying the conditional to particular affirmative yields four parallel conjunctions, and varying it to universal negative yields another four. Finally, further variations of the disjunctive parts yield 36 more conjunctions. The passage is a systematic catalogue of valid moods in this figure.

Middle Term as Predicate in Compound Syllogisms
Classification of compound syllogisms by middle term role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether the middle term acts as a subject or predicate in the disjunctive parts of a compound syllogism when it is the predicate of the antecedent.

Core Idea
In compound syllogisms, the middle term can be the predicate of the antecedent, and then it plays a role in the disjunction.

This passage continues the classification of compound syllogisms. Previously, we considered cases where the common term (middle term) is the subject of the antecedent. Now the author turns to cases where the common term is the predicate of the antecedent. He then further divides this into two possibilities: the common term can be a predicate or a subject in the parts of the disjunction. This distinction determines the figure of the resulting compound syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent's predicate
Find the predicate of the conditional antecedent in the compound syllogism. This predicate is the middle term (common term).
2
Check the role in the disjunction
Determine whether this same term appears as a predicate or as a subject in the disjunctive premise. The passage says it can be either.
3
Classify accordingly
If the middle term is a predicate in the disjunctive parts, the syllogism takes a specific figure (likely the second figure). If it is a subject, another figure results (e.g., first or third).
The text cuts off, but the implication is that the figure depends on this role.

Structure and Conditions of Compound Syllogisms with Shared Subject
Author states general conditions for valid compound syllogisms with shared subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid compound syllogism when the subject of the conditional matches the subject of the disjunctive parts.

Core Idea
Shared subject forces strict quantifier compatibility between conditional and disjunction.

This passage introduces a specialized compound syllogism where a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) share the same subject term. The author lays out the general conditions that make the combination productive, leading to 48 valid conjunctions. We will unpack these conditions step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
The compound syllogism consists of a conditional premise (e.g., 'Everything such that every B is A, then H is R') and a disjunctive premise (e.g., 'Always either every B is J or every B is D'). Note the shared subject 'B'.
2
Understand the quantifier conditions
If the conditional is affirmative, its antecedent must be universal; if negative, the antecedent must be particular. Conversely, if the antecedent is particular in the affirmative, then the disjunctive parts must be universal affirmative or particular negative.
This ensures proper term distribution across the compound.
3
Apply the conditions to the 48 conjunctions
The author states that under these conditions, there are 48 productive conjunctions. Each combination of quantifier types (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) for the conditional and disjunctive yields valid forms.
The exact number 48 arises from 4×12 combinations of the disjunctive quantifiers (4 types) times conditional quantifiers (3 types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative) plus extras.
4
Recognize the conclusion type
The conclusion is always an affirmative conditional ('muttasila mujaba') as per the earlier 'first figure' compound syllogism. The internal quantifiers of the conclusion may weaken (e.g., from universal to particular).
The conclusion mirrors the pattern of the first figure compound syllogism introduced earlier.
