⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كليات سالبة أن يكون المقدم من المتصلة الموجبة كلياً، ومن السالبة جزئياً، وإن كان المقدم من الموجبة المتصلة جزئياً ومن السالبة كلياً أن تكون أجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة أو جزئيات سالبة، وقرائنه المنتجة ثمانية وأربعون اقتراناً، والنتيجة في الكل موجبة متصلة على ما تقدم في الأول، وعلى ذلك التفصيل: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين أجزاء المنفصلة كلية موجبة، والمقدم كلي موجب، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وأما كل "ب" "د") و(كل ما كان كل "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما بعض "ج" "أ" وأما بعض "د" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المقدم كلي سالب كقولنا: (كل كان لا شيء من "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فإما بعض "ج" ليس وإما بعض "د" ليس "أ"). الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن التالي جزئي موجب كقولنا وكل ما كان بعض "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته كالأول. الاقتران الرابع الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المقدم جزئي سالب كقولنا: (وكل ما كان ليس كل "ب" "أ" فـ "هـ" "ر") ونتيجته كالثاني. وإن كانت المنفصلة بحالها، والمتصلة جزئية موجبة، فقد يأتلف منها أربعة اقترانات أخرى، موازية لهذه الأربعة. وإن كانت المنفصلة بحالها، والمتصلة كلية سالبة، فقد يأتلف عنها أيضاً أربعة اقترانات. وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، أو كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، والمتصلة كلية سالبة، وهي ستة وثلاثون اقتراناً أخرى، فاعتبرها.
Universal negatives: that the antecedent of the affirmative conditional is universal, and of the negative [conditional] is particular; and if the antecedent of the affirmative conditional is particular and of the negative is universal, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmatives or particular negatives. Its productive conjunctions are forty-eight conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is affirmative conditional as previously in the first [figure], and according to that detail: The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, parts of the disjunction are universal affirmative, and the antecedent is universal affirmative, as our saying: 'Always either every B is J or every B is D' and 'Everything such that every B is A, then H is R' and its conclusion: 'It may be that if H is R, then always either some J is A or some D is A.' The second conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the antecedent is universal negative, as our saying: 'Everything such that nothing of B is A, then H is R' so 'It may be that if H is R, then either some J is not or some D is not A.' The third conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the consequent is particular affirmative, as our saying: 'And everything such that some B is A, then H is R' and its conclusion is like the first. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction is the same, except that the antecedent is particular negative, as our saying: 'And everything such that not every B is A, then H is R' and its conclusion is like the second. And if the disjunction remains as is and the conditional is particular affirmative, then four other conjunctions come from it, parallel to these four. And if the disjunction remains as is and the conditional is universal negative, then also four conjunctions come from it. And accordingly, the production is not hidden when the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, or when the disjunction is particular affirmative and the conditional is universal negative—that is thirty-six other conjunctions. So consider them.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of valid composite syllogisms (qiyasat murakkaba) where the subject of the conditional premise is shared with the subject of the parts of the disjunctive premise. The author is expanding on the earlier 'first figure' compound syllogism. He begins by stating the general condition: the antecedent (muqaddim) of the conditional must be universal if the conditional is affirmative, and particular if negative; conversely, if the antecedent is particular in the affirmative and universal in the negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmative or particular negative. Under these conditions, there are 48 productive conjunctions, all yielding an affirmative conditional conclusion. The author then gives the first four conjunctions as examples, using logical variables B, J, D, A, H, R. In the first conjunction, both the conditional and the disjunctive parts are universal affirmatives, and the antecedent is universal affirmative. The example: 'Always either every B is J or every B is D' (disjunctive) and 'Everything such that every B is A, then H is R' (conditional). The conclusion: 'It may be that if H is R, then always either some J is A or some D is A.' This shows a weakened conclusion due to the conversion of terms. The second conjunction keeps the same disjunction but the conditional's antecedent is universal negative, yielding a conclusion with negatives. The third has a particular affirmative consequent, and the fourth a particular negative antecedent, each producing parallel results. Then the author notes that if the conditional is particular affirmative, four more conjunctions arise parallel to the first four; similarly for universal negative conditional. Finally, he mentions variations when the parts of the disjunction have different quantifications or the disjunction is particular, totaling 36 additional conjunctions beyond the initial twelve, and instructs the reader to consider them. Throughout, this is a highly technical classification that requires careful study of the logical squares.

Key Terms
منفصلة
متصلة
اقتران
مقدم
تالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Indefinite

Key Takeaway
The validity of a compound syllogism with a shared subject depends on precise quantifier compatibility between the conditional and disjunctive premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the conditional 'Everything such that every B is A, then H is R' (universal affirmative) and the disjunction 'Always either every B is J or every B is D' (universal affirmative), write out the conclusion as per the first conjunction pattern.
?

The First Four Productive Conjunctions: Examples
Author gives four example conjunctions to illustrate quantifier variations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the conclusion for each of the first four conjunctions described in the passage.

Core Idea
The first four conjunctions demonstrate how changing the quantifier of the conditional's antecedent or consequent alters the conclusion.

After laying down the general conditions, the author provides four concrete examples of compound syllogisms, each with a different quantifier combination in the conditional. We will dissect each one to see how the conclusion is derived.

Guided Path
1
First conjunction: universal affirmative conditional and disjunction
Both premises are universal affirmative: the conditional antecedent is 'every B is A', the disjunction is 'always either every B is J or every B is D'. The conclusion becomes particular affirmative: 'If H is R, then always either some J is A or some D is A'. Note the weakening from 'every' to 'some'.
This is due to the conversion of terms in the compound structure—the subject of the conclusion is no longer B but J and D.
2
Second conjunction: universal negative conditional
The disjunction remains the same, but the conditional's antecedent is universal negative: 'nothing of B is A'. The conclusion becomes particular negative: 'If H is R, then either some J is not (-) A or some D is not A'.
The negation shifts from the antecedent to the predicates in the conclusion.
3
Third conjunction: particular affirmative consequent
The antecedent is 'some B is A' (particular affirmative). The conclusion is like the first conjunction: 'If H is R, then always either some J is A or some D is A'.
Despite the antecedent being particular, the conclusion mirrors the first because the disjunction remains universal.
4
Fourth conjunction: particular negative antecedent
The antecedent is 'not every B is A' (particular negative). The conclusion is like the second: 'If H is R, then either some J is not A or some D is not A'.
This shows a pattern: when the antecedent is negative, the conclusion contains negative predicates.

Extending the Pattern: Remaining 36 Conjunctions
Author explains how to generate the remaining 36 conjunctions from variations of the conditional and disjunctive parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to generate the valid conjunctions for any combination of quantifiers for the conditional and disjunctive parts as described in the passage.

Core Idea
The 48 conjunctions are generated by systematically varying the quantifier and quality of both premises, following a pattern.

After detailing the first four conjunctions, the author explains how to extend the pattern to 36 more conjunctions by changing the conditional's quantifier (particular affirmative, universal negative) and varying the disjunctive parts (universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). This lesson will help you see the systematic enumeration.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base pattern from the first four conjunctions
The first four used a universal affirmative disjunction and varied the conditional (universal affirm, universal neg, particular affirm, particular neg). The conclusions alternated between affirmative and negative.
2
Vary the conditional to particular affirmative
When the conditional is 'juz'iyya mujaba' (particular affirmative), there are four new conjunctions parallel to the first four. They mirror the same pattern but with the conditional's antecedent being particular affirmative.
The passage says: 'if the disjunction remains as is and the conditional is particular affirmative, then four other conjunctions come from it, parallel to these four.'
3
Vary the conditional to universal negative
When the conditional is 'kulliyya saliba' (universal negative), another four conjunctions arise, also parallel.
This brings the total so far to 12 conjunctions (first 4 + 4 + 4).
4
Vary the disjunctive parts' quantifiers
The author then says 'accordingly, the production is not hidden when the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, or when the disjunction is particular affirmative and the conditional is universal negative—that is thirty-six other conjunctions.' This means for each of these four new disjunction types, there are 9 conjunctions (since the conditional can be of various types). The total becomes 12 + 36 = 48.
The exact enumeration requires careful combinatorial counting, but the author provides the final number.
5
Practice generating an example
Take the disjunction type 'parts are particular affirmative' and conditional 'universal negative'. Write an example using Islamic terms (e.g., intoxicant) and see what conclusion the pattern would suggest.
Try to follow the pattern from the first four: the conclusion should be affirmative or negative based on the antecedent's quality.

This passage is a detailed enumeration of valid composite syllogisms (qiyasat murakkaba) where the subject of the conditional premise is shared with the subject of the parts of the disjunctive premise. The author is expanding on the earlier 'first figure' compound syllogism. He begins by stating the general condition: the antecedent (muqaddim) of the conditional must be universal if the conditional is affirmative, and particular if negative; conversely, if the antecedent is particular in the affirmative and universal in the negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmative or particular negative. Under these conditions, there are 48 productive conjunctions, all yielding an affirmative conditional conclusion. The author then gives the first four conjunctions as examples, using logical variables B, J, D, A, H, R. In the first conjunction, both the conditional and the disjunctive parts are universal affirmatives, and the antecedent is universal affirmative. The example: 'Always either every B is J or every B is D' (disjunctive) and 'Everything such that every B is A, then H is R' (conditional). The conclusion: 'It may be that if H is R, then always either some J is A or some D is A.' This shows a weakened conclusion due to the conversion of terms. The second conjunction keeps the same disjunction but the conditional's antecedent is universal negative, yielding a conclusion with negatives. The third has a particular affirmative consequent, and the fourth a particular negative antecedent, each producing parallel results. Then the author notes that if the conditional is particular affirmative, four more conjunctions arise parallel to the first four; similarly for universal negative conditional. Finally, he mentions variations when the parts of the disjunction have different quantifications or the disjunction is particular, totaling 36 additional conjunctions beyond the initial twelve, and instructs the reader to consider them. Throughout, this is a highly technical classification that requires careful study of the logical squares.

Key Terms
منفصلة
متصلة
اقتران
مقدم
تالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 12
C) 48
D) 36

Key Takeaway
The 48 conjunctions are generated by systematically varying the quantifier and quality of both the conditional and disjunctive premises, following the patterns established in the first four examples.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the case where the disjunctive parts are universal negative: 'Always either no intoxicant is forbidden or no intoxicant is impure'. With a universal affirmative conditional 'Every intoxicant that is khamr leads to haram', what is the likely conclusion pattern? (Refer to the pattern of the first four conjunctions.)
?

Conditions for Mixed Syllogisms in the Second Figure
Conditions for mixed syllogisms in the second figure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary conditions for a valid mixed syllogism in the second figure combining a disjunctive and a conditional premise.

Core Idea
The second figure with mixed premises requires the disjunctive to be affirmative and a true disjunction or exclusive of emptiness.

In this passage, the author lays out the conditions under which a syllogism in the second figure, composed of a disjunctive and a conditional premise, yields a valid conclusion. Understanding these conditions is essential for constructing sound arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Recognize that the syllogism has a disjunctive (منفصلة) and a conditional (متصلة) premise.
2
Check the disjunctive premise
Ensure it is affirmative and either حقيقية (true disjunction) or مانعة الخلو (exclusive of emptiness), not مانعة الجمع.
The disjunctive must be affirmative and of the allowed types.
3
Check the conditional premise
Ensure it is not particular negative; its antecedent must be universal if affirmative, particular if negative, and if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, the conditional must be universal negative.
4
Verify uniformity
The parts of the disjunctive must be uniform in quantity and quality (all universal or all particular, all affirmative or all negative).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية. الشكل الثاني وليكن الفرض أيضاً إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية وشرطه في الإنتاج إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن تكون حقيقية أو مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، وأن لا تكون المتصلة جزئية سالبة، وأن يكون المقدم من المتصلة كلياً إن كانت موجبة وجزئياً إن كانت سالبة، وإن كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة أن تكون المتصلة كلية سالبة، وقرائنه المنتجة اثنان وثلاثون اقترانا والنتيجة في الكل على ما سبق من التفصيل إذا كان الاشتراك في موضوع المقدم : الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة، والمقدم المتصلة كلي سالب، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "د" "ج") و(كل ما كان لا شيء من "أ" "ج" فـ "هـ" "(") ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "," فدائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "د" "أ"). من الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن المقدم كلي موجب، كقولنا: (وكل ما كان كل "أ" "ج" فـ "هـ" ",") ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما بعض "ب" ليس "أ" وإما بعض "د" ليس "أ") لأنه إن صدق كل "ب" "ج") صدق ليس كل "د" "ج" وإن صدق (كل "د" "ج") صدق ليس كل "ب" "ج"). وإن كانت المنفصلة بحالها، والمتصلة جزئية موجبة تألف عنهما اقترانان موازیان لهذين الاقترانين. وإن كانت المتصلة كلية سالبة تألف عنهما اقترانان آخران، المقدم في أحدهما جزئي موجب، وفي الآخر جزئي سالب. وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئيات موجبة، أو كليات سالبة، أو جزئيات سالبة، أو كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، وهي ستة وعشرون اقتراناً أخر، فاعتبرها بنفسك.
Continuation of the first part: On defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. The second figure: Let the supposition also be that the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, and its condition for production is that the disjunctive is affirmative, and that it is true or exclusive of emptiness without combination, and that the conditional is not particular negative, and that the antecedent of the conditional is universal if it is affirmative and particular if it is negative, and if the disjunctive is particular affirmative then the conditional must be universal negative. Its productive conjunctions are thirty-two conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is as previously detailed when the commonality is in the subject of the antecedent. The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmatives, and the antecedent of the conditional is universal negative, as we say: "Always either every B is C or every D is C" and "Whatever is such that no A is C, then H is R" and its conclusion is: whenever H is R, then always either no B is A or no D is A. The second conjunction: the conjunction in its state except that the antecedent is universal affirmative, as we say: "And whatever is such that every A is C, then H is R" and its conclusion is: whenever H is R, then always either some B is not A or some D is not A, because if every B is C is true, then not every D is C is true, and if every D is C is true, then not every B is C is true. If the disjunctive remains as it is and the conditional is particular affirmative, then two conjunctions parallel to these two are composed from them. And if the conditional is universal negative, then two other conjunctions are composed from them, with the antecedent in one particular affirmative and in the other particular negative. On this basis, production is not hidden if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmatives, or universal negatives, or particular negatives, or if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, and these are twenty-six other conjunctions, so consider them yourself.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage defines the second figure of mixed syllogisms combining a disjunctive premise (منفصلة) and a conditional premise (متصلة). The author first sets the general conditions: the parts of the disjunctive must be uniform in quantity and quality (all universal or all particular, all affirmative or all negative). Additionally, the disjunctive must be affirmative and either truly disjunctive (حقيقية) or exclusive of emptiness (مانعة الخلو) but not exclusive of combination. The conditional must not be particular negative; its antecedent must be universal if the conditional is affirmative, and particular if negative. If the disjunctive is particular affirmative, the conditional must be universal negative. Under these conditions, there are 32 productive conjunctions. The author then details the first two as examples. The first conjunction has both premises universal affirmative, but the conditional's antecedent is universal negative. Example: "Always either every B is C or every D is C" and "Whatever is such that no A is C, then H is R". The conclusion is: whenever H is R, then always either no B is A or no D is A. The second conjunction is the same except the conditional's antecedent is universal affirmative, yielding a conclusion with particular negatives. The author then notes that if the conditional is particular affirmative, two parallel conjunctions arise, and if universal negative, two more with particular antecedents. Finally, he states that variations with different quantities and qualities of the disjunctive parts produce 26 more conjunctions, leaving them for the student to derive.

المتصلة
المقدم
القرائن

Key Takeaway
The second figure for mixed syllogisms has strict conditions on the disjunctive and conditional premises that must be met for a valid conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

First Two Conjunctions of the Second Figure
First two conjunctions of the second figure with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the first two valid conjunctions of the second figure with universal affirmative disjunctive and conditional premises and derive their conclusions.

Core Idea
The first conjunction uses a universal negative antecedent; the second uses a universal affirmative antecedent, each yielding a different conclusion.

After establishing the general conditions, the author provides two specific examples of productive conjunctions in the second figure. These examples illustrate how the logical structure works in practice, using variables and formal notation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the variables
Recognize that أ, ب, ج, د, هـ, ر are terms. The disjunctive is about B, C, D, and the conditional involves A, C, H, R.
2
Parse the first conjunction
The disjunctive is 'Always either every B is C or every D is C' (both universal affirmative). The conditional is 'Whatever is such that no A is C, then H is R' (universal negative antecedent). The conclusion is 'Whenever H is R, then always either no B is A or no D is A'.
3
Parse the second conjunction
Same disjunctive, but the conditional's antecedent is universal affirmative: 'Whatever is such that every A is C, then H is R'. The conclusion becomes 'Whenever H is R, then always either some B is not A or some D is not A'.
4
Explain the reasoning
The author notes that if every B is C is true, then not every D is C is true (because the disjunctive is exclusive), and vice versa, leading to the conclusions.

Deriving the Remaining Conjunctions of the Second Figure
Extension to remaining conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to systematically derive the remaining 26 valid conjunctions of the second figure by varying the quantity and quality of the disjunctive parts and the conditional.

Core Idea
The author leaves 26 conjunctions for the student to derive independently, demonstrating mastery of the logical system.

After detailing the first two conjunctions, the author encourages the student to derive the remaining 26 conjunctions by varying the disjunctive's parts (particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative, etc.) and the conditional's quantity and quality. This exercise internalizes the rules.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base patterns
The first two conjunctions used universal affirmative disjunctive parts. Now vary them.
2
Consider disjunctive parts being particular affirmatives
If the parts are particular affirmative, the conclusions will involve particular statements.
3
Consider disjunctive parts being universal negatives
If the parts are universal negative, the conclusions involve negatives.
4
Consider disjunctive parts being particular negatives
Similarly.
5
Consider the disjunctive itself being particular affirmative
Then the conditional must be universal negative, and conclusions will follow accordingly.

Key Takeaway
The second figure with mixed premises yields 32 valid conjunctions, leaving the student to derive the majority independently to solidify understanding.

Fourth Figure with Disjunctive and Conditional Premises
Author defines the fourth figure's conditions for mixed disjunctive-conditional syllogisms and exemplifies nine conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid mixed syllogism in the fourth figure and apply them to given conjunctions.

Core Idea
The fourth figure extends conditional-disjunctive logic with specific restrictions on quantity and quality.

In this passage, the author explains the fourth figure of mixed syllogisms, where the predicate of the conditional's antecedent becomes the subject of the disjunctive's parts. This figure has its own set of conditions beyond those of previous figures. We will examine these conditions and see how they govern the first three conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the figure's defining feature
Note that the predicate of the conditional's antecedent is the subject of the disjunctive's parts. This distinguishes the fourth figure from others.
2
List the conditions for the disjunctive premise
The disjunctive must be affirmative, true or exclusive of emptiness, and universal if the conditional is affirmative.
3
List the restrictions on the conditional premise
The conditional must not be particular negative; further restrictions on the antecedent depend on the quantity/quality of the disjunctive parts.
4
Examine the first conjunction
Both premises are universal affirmative; the antecedent is universal affirmative. The conclusion is a conditional with particular disjunctive parts.
5
Compare the second and third conjunctions
Changing the antecedent to universal negative or particular affirmative alters the conclusion to include negatives or retain the first pattern.

And if the predicate of the antecedent is the subject of the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the fourth figure. Let the supposition be that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality. Its condition for production is that the disjunctive is affirmative, and that it is true or exclusive of emptiness without combination, and that it is universal if the conditional is affirmative, and that the conditional is not particular negative, and if the conditional is affirmative then the antecedent should not be particular negative, and if it is negative then the antecedent should not be universal affirmative, and if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative then the antecedent in the affirmative should not be particular affirmative, and if it is universal negative then the antecedent of the negative should not be universal negative. Its productive conjunctions are forty conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is as previously detailed when the commonality is in the subject. The first conjunction: from two universals and the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmatives and the antecedent of the conditional is universal affirmative, as we say: "Always either every B is C or every B is D" and "Whatever is such that every A is something, then H is R". Its conclusion: whenever H is R, then always either some C is A or some D is A. The second conjunction: the conjunction in its state except that the antecedent is universal negative, as we say: "And whatever is such that A is B, then H is R" and its conclusion: whenever H is R, then always either not every C is A or not every D is A. The third conjunction: the conjunction in its state except that the antecedent is particular affirmative, as we say: "And whatever is such that some A is B, then H is R" and its conclusion is like the first. If the conditional is particular affirmative, then three other conjunctions parallel to these three are composed from it with the disjunctive, so consider them. The seventh conjunction: the disjunctive in its state except that the conditional is universal negative, and the antecedent is particular negative, as we say: "It is not the case that if some A is not B then H is R" and its conclusion: "Whenever H is R, then always either some C is A or some D is A" because if "H is R" is true, then "every C is A" is true, and it was always either every B is C or every D is C. The eighth conjunction: the conjunction in its state except that the antecedent is particular affirmative and its conclusion is like the second. The ninth conjunction: the conjunction in its state except that the antecedent is universal negative and its conclusion is like the third. On this basis, production is not hidden if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, or universal...

Commentary
This passage shifts to the fourth figure of mixed syllogisms, where the predicate of the conditional's antecedent becomes the subject of the disjunctive's parts. The author lays out the conditions for this figure: the disjunctive must be affirmative and either true disjunction or exclusive of emptiness; it must be universal if the conditional is affirmative; the conditional must not be particular negative; further restrictions on the antecedent's quantity and quality depending on the disjunctive's parts. There are 40 productive conjunctions. The author then describes the first three conjunctions in detail. The first has both premises universal affirmative, with the conditional's antecedent universal affirmative, yielding a conclusion with particular affirmatives. The second has the antecedent universal negative, yielding a conclusion with universal negatives. The third has the antecedent particular affirmative, with a conclusion like the first. He then notes that if the conditional is particular affirmative, three parallel conjunctions arise. Then he jumps to the seventh conjunction in which the conditional is universal negative and the antecedent is particular negative, with a special justification. The eighth and ninth are variants. Finally, he states that further variations with different quantities of the disjunctive parts follow, but the text cuts off. Overall, this passage exemplifies the rigorous enumeration of valid moods in mixed syllogisms characteristic of Islamic logic.

Conditions for Productive Conjunctions with Subject Link
Author enumerates conditions and examples of productive iqtiranat with subject link.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six conditions for a valid iqtiran between a conditional and a disjunctive when the common link is the subject, and apply them to determine whether a given conjunction is productive.

Core Idea
Productive conjunctions require precise alignment of quantifier and quality.

In compound syllogisms, not every combination of a conditional and a disjunctive yields a valid conclusion. The author specifies six conditions that must be satisfied for the iqtiran to produce a conclusion. These conditions govern the quantities and qualities of the propositions and their parts. Understanding them allows you to quickly assess the validity of such conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
You have a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila). They share a common term, which in this case is the subject (mawdu').
2
Recall the six conditions
From the passage, the conditions are: 1) If the conditional is affirmative, it must be universal. 2) The conditional must not be particular negative. 3) If the conditional is affirmative, its antecedent cannot be particular negative. 4) If the conditional is negative, its antecedent cannot be universal affirmative. 5) If the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative, then the antecedent in an affirmative conditional cannot be particular affirmative. 6) If the disjunctive is universal negative, the antecedent in a negative conditional cannot be universal negative.
These conditions ensure the middle term is properly distributed.
3
Check an example
Take the first conjunction: disjunctive universal affirmative, conditional universal affirmative, antecedent universal affirmative. All conditions are satisfied, so it is productive.
The conclusion is a disjunction of particulars.
4
Recognize the pattern
There are 40 total productive conjunctions depending on variations. The author gives the first three and then three more (7th-9th) with different quantifier/quality combinations.
Once you understand the conditions, you can generate the others.
5
Practice a non-productive case
Consider a conditional that is particular negative. Condition 2 fails, so no conclusion follows.
This helps internalize the necessity of each condition.

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions for productive conjunctions (iqtiranat) between a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) proposition when they share a common subject (mawdu'). The author first lists six conditions: the conditional must be universal if affirmative, and must not be particular negative; if the conditional is affirmative, its antecedent cannot be particular negative; if it is negative, its antecedent cannot be universal affirmative; if the disjunctive parts are particular affirmative, the antecedent of the affirmative conditional cannot be particular affirmative; if the disjunctive is universal negative, the antecedent of the negative conditional cannot be universal negative. These conditions ensure valid syllogistic forms. The author states that there are forty productive conjunctions based on the common subject link, and then illustrates the first three and the seventh, eighth, and ninth conjunctions with examples using variables (B, J, D, H, R, A). In the first conjunction, both premises are universal affirmative, yielding a conclusion that is a disjunction of particulars. The second and third vary the antecedent's quantity and quality. The passage then mentions that if the conditional is particular affirmative, three more conjunctions parallel the first three exist. The seventh through ninth conjunctions involve a universal negative conditional with different antecedents. Finally, the author notes that variations in the parts of the disjunctive yield thirty-one additional conjunctions, encouraging the reader to derive them easily.

Common Link as the Entire Consequent
Rules for common link as entire consequent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion form (conditional or disjunctive) of an iqtiran when the common link is the entire consequent, based on the quality of the disjunctive premise.

Core Idea
The quality of the disjunctive determines the conclusion form.

The author now addresses iqtiranat where the shared term between the conditional and disjunctive is the entire consequent of the conditional. The conditions for a valid syllogism are that one premise is universal and one is affirmative. Importantly, the conclusion's form depends on the disjunctive: if it is affirmative, the conclusion is a conditional affirmative; if negative, the conclusion is a disjunctive negative. This rule shapes the nine productive conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify common link type
First, confirm that the common link is the entire consequent of the conditional (tali), not part of it.
2
Check the conditions
For a valid product, one premise must be universal (kulliyya) and one must be affirmative (mujiba). Both conditions must hold.
3
Note the disjunctive's quality
Determine whether the disjunctive premise (munfasila) is affirmative or negative. This decides the form of the conclusion.
4
Derive conclusion form
If the disjunctive is affirmative, the conclusion is a conditional affirmative (muttasila mujiba). If negative, the conclusion is a disjunctive negative (munfasila saliba).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاشتراك بين المتصلة والمنفصلة بالتالي من المتصلة فإما به أو بجزئه، فإن كان بالتالي فشرط إنتاجه كليّة إحدى المقدمتين وإيجاب إحداهما، وقرائنه المنتجة تسعة، غير أن النتيجة تكون متصلة موجبة إن كانت المنفصلة موجبة، ومنفصلة سالبة إن كانت المنفصلة سالبة: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، كقولنا: (دائماً إما "ب" "ج" وإما "د" "ر" و(كل ما كان "هـ" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فلا يكون "ب" "ج") فـ "د" "ر") ويلزم من "د" "ر") سلب ("ب" "ج") ضرورة امتناع الجمع بينهما. الاقتران الثاني: المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة كليّة سالبة، كقولنا: (وليس البتة إذا كان "هـ" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كل ما كان "هـ" "أ") فلا يكون ("د" "ر") ويلزم منه وجود ("ب" "ج") لاستحالة الخلو منهما. الاقتران الثالث المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة كليّة موجبة كقولنا: (وقد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "أ" فلا يكون "ب" "ج"). الاقتران الرابع: المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: (وليس كل ما كان "هـ" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "أ" فـ (قد "ب" "ج"). وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، وهي اقترانات أخرى، فاعتبرها. وأما إن كان الاشتراك بجزء التالي فإما بموضوعه أو بمحموله، فإن كان بموضوعه فإما أن يكون محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال أو موضوعاً فيها، فإن كان محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال فهو على هيئة الشكل الأول، وليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية، وشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن يكون
And if the common link between the conditional and the disjunctive is the consequent of the conditional, then either by it or by part of it. If it is by the consequent, then the condition for its production is universality of one of the two premises and affirmation of one of them, and its productive conjunctions are nine, except that the conclusion is a conditional affirmative if the disjunctive is affirmative, and a disjunctive negative if the disjunctive is negative. The first conjunction: from two universals affirmative, as we say: (Always either 'B' is 'J' or 'D' is 'R') and (Everything that is 'H' is 'A' then 'D' is 'R') and its conclusion: (Everything that is 'H' is 'R' so it is not that 'B' is 'J') so 'D' is 'R' and from 'D' is 'R' the negation of 'B' is 'J' follows necessarily due to the impossibility of their conjunction. The second conjunction: the disjunctive same, except that the conditional is universal negative, as we say: (It is absolutely not that if 'H' is 'A' then 'D' is 'R') and its conclusion: (Everything that is 'H' is 'A' so it is not that 'D' is 'R') and from it the existence of 'B' is 'J' follows due to the impossibility of their void. The third conjunction: the disjunctive same, except that the conditional is universal affirmative, as we say: (It may be that if 'H' is 'A' then 'D' is 'R') and its conclusion: (It may be that if 'H' is 'A' then it is not that 'B' is 'J'). The fourth conjunction: the disjunctive same, except that the conditional is particular negative, as we say: (Not everything that is 'H' is 'A' then 'D' is 'R') and its conclusion: (It may be that if 'H' is 'A' then it may be that 'B' is 'J'). And on this, production is not hidden if the disjunctive is particular affirmative, or universal negative, or particular negative, and they are other conjunctions, so consider them. And if the common link is by part of the consequent, then either by its subject or by its predicate. If it is by its subject, then either it is a predicate in the parts of the disjunctive or a subject in them. If it is a predicate in the parts of the disjunctive, then it is in the form of the first figure. Let the assumption be that the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, and the condition for its production is affirmation of the disjunctive, and that
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage turns to iqtiranat where the conditional and disjunctive share a common link in the consequent (tali) of the conditional, either the entire consequent or part of it. For the first case (common link is the entire consequent), the condition for a valid product is that one premise is universal and one is affirmative, yielding nine productive conjunctions. The conclusion depends on the disjunctive: if the disjunctive is affirmative, the conclusion is a conditional affirmative; if negative, a disjunctive negative. The author gives four examples: the first conjunction with both universal affirmative, where the conclusion negates one disjunct because they cannot both be true (exclusive disjunction). The second uses a universal negative conditional, leading to a conclusion affirming the other disjunct due to the impossibility of both being false (exclusive disjunction). The third and fourth involve universal affirmative and particular negative conditionals, yielding possible conclusions. The passage then notes further conjunctions for disjunctives of other quantities and qualities. Next, the author considers when the common link is part of the consequent (either its subject or predicate). If it is the subject, and that subject serves as a predicate in the disjunctive parts, the iqtiran takes the form of the first figure (shakl awwal). Here, the assumptions are that the disjunctive parts are uniform in quantifier and quality, and the condition is that the disjunctive is affirmative. The passage breaks off here, so the full discussion is incomplete.

15 min
The First Four Conjunctions in Detail
Exemplification of four iqtiranat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reproduce the first four conjunction examples and explain how the conclusion follows from the exclusive disjunction properties (imtina' al-jam' and istihalat al-khuluww).

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunction properties drive the conclusions in the first four conjunctions.

The author provides four concrete examples of iqtiranat where the common link is the entire consequent. Each example combines a fixed disjunctive ('Always either B is J or D is R') with a varying conditional. The conclusions are derived using the exclusive nature of the disjunction: impossibility of both being true (imtina' al-jam') and impossibility of both being false (istihalat al-khuluww). These examples illustrate the general rule from the previous lesson.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fixed disjunctive
The disjunctive is always 'Always either B is J or D is R', which is an exclusive disjunction (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluw).
2
Analyze the first conjunction
Both premises are universal affirmative. The conditional: 'Everything that is H is A then D is R' yields conclusion: 'Everything that is H is A so it is not that B is J' because if D is R, then B cannot be J (imtina' al-jam').
3
Analyze the second conjunction
Conditional is universal negative ('It is absolutely not that if H is A then D is R'). Conclusion: 'Everything that is H is A so it is not that D is R' leading to 'B is J' because both cannot be false (istihalat al-khuluww).
4
Analyze the third conjunction
Conditional is universal affirmative but in possibility ('It may be that if H is A then D is R'). Conclusion: 'It may be that if H is A then it is not that B is J' (possible).
5
Analyze the fourth conjunction
Conditional is particular negative ('Not everything that is H is A then D is R'). Conclusion: 'It may be that if H is A then it may be that B is J' (possible).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاشتراك بين المتصلة والمنفصلة بالتالي من المتصلة فإما به أو بجزئه، فإن كان بالتالي فشرط إنتاجه كليّة إحدى المقدمتين وإيجاب إحداهما، وقرائنه المنتجة تسعة، غير أن النتيجة تكون متصلة موجبة إن كانت المنفصلة موجبة، ومنفصلة سالبة إن كانت المنفصلة سالبة: الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، كقولنا: (دائماً إما "ب" "ج" وإما "د" "ر" و(كل ما كان "هـ" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فلا يكون "ب" "ج") فـ "د" "ر") ويلزم من "د" "ر") سلب ("ب" "ج") ضرورة امتناع الجمع بينهما. الاقتران الثاني: المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة كليّة سالبة، كقولنا: (وليس البتة إذا كان "هـ" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته كل ما كان "هـ" "أ") فلا يكون ("د" "ر") ويلزم منه وجود ("ب" "ج") لاستحالة الخلو منهما. الاقتران الثالث المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة كليّة موجبة كقولنا: (وقد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "أ" فلا يكون "ب" "ج"). الاقتران الرابع: المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة جزئية سالبة، كقولنا: (وليس كل ما كان "هـ" "أ" فـ "د" "ر") ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "أ" فـ (قد "ب" "ج"). وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، أو كلية سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، وهي اقترانات أخرى، فاعتبرها. وأما إن كان الاشتراك بجزء التالي فإما بموضوعه أو بمحموله، فإن كان بموضوعه فإما أن يكون محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال أو موضوعاً فيها، فإن كان محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال فهو على هيئة الشكل الأول، وليكن الفرض إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة متحدة الكمية والكيفية، وشرط إنتاجه إيجاب المنفصلة، وأن يكون

10 min
Common Link as Part of the Consequent: Subject Case
Transformation into first figure when common link is subject

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an iqtiran involving a part of the consequent reduces to the first figure and state the required conditions.

Core Idea
When the common link is the subject of the consequent and a predicate in the disjunctive, the syllogism takes the first figure.

The author moves to cases where the common link is part of the consequent: either its subject (mawdu') or predicate (mahmul). When it is the subject, and that subject appears as a predicate in the parts of the disjunctive, the iqtiran assumes the form of the first figure (shakl awwal). The conditions are that the disjunctive parts are uniform in quantity and quality, and the disjunctive must be affirmative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the part of the consequent
Determine whether the common link is the subject or predicate of the consequent. The passage discusses the subject case first.
2
Check the role in the disjunctive
If the common link is the subject, see whether it serves as a predicate or subject in the disjunctive parts. The passage explains the case where it is a predicate.
3
Recognize the first figure
When it is a predicate in the disjunctive parts, the iqtiran becomes like a first figure categorical syllogism. The disjunctive provides the major premise (containing the common link as predicate) and the conditional provides the minor premise.
4
Apply the conditions
The disjunctive must be affirmative (mujiba) and its parts must be uniform in quantity (both universal or both particular) and quality (both affirmative or both negative).

12 min
Mixed Syllogisms: Common Part as Whole Consequent
Author enumerates conditions for productive conjunctions when the common part is the whole consequent of the conjunctive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for productive mixed syllogisms when the common part is the whole consequent, and apply them to construct valid conclusions for the nine conjunctions.

Core Idea
Nine productive conjunctions arise when the common part is the whole consequent; only four are exemplified, yet the pattern governs all.

The author now turns to a specific configuration of mixed syllogisms: where the conjunctive and disjunctive share the entire consequent of the conjunctive. Understanding the conditions—universality of one premise and affirmation of one—is key to unlocking all nine productive forms. In this lesson, we will dissect the four exemplar conjunctions and learn to derive the remaining five.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common part
Locate the consequent (tālī) of the conjunctive proposition. Confirm that the disjunctive proposition shares that entire consequent as one of its disjuncts. This is the 'common part' that links the two premises.
If the common part is only a portion of the consequent, different rules apply (not covered here).
2
Apply the general conditions
Ensure that one premise is universal and one is affirmative. If both are particular or both negative, no conclusion follows. This condition holds for all nine productive conjunctions.
The nine conjunctions arise from varying the quantity and quality of the disjunctive and conjunctive while maintaining this condition.
3
Derive the conclusion for the first conjunction
Consider the first exemplar: disjunctive universal affirmative (always either B is J or D is R) and conjunctive universal affirmative (every H is A implies D is R). Since D is R follows from H being A, the disjunctive forces that B is J is false (because of mutual exclusivity). Thus the conclusion is a universal affirmative conjunctive: every H is R implies B is not J.
This uses the principle that from one disjunct being true, the other must be false in an exclusive disjunction.
4
Vary the conjunctive's quality
In the second conjunction, the conjunctive is universal negative (it is never the case that if H is A then D is R). This denies D is R, so the disjunctive forces B is J to be true. The conclusion becomes: every H is A implies D is not R, and consequently B is J holds.
The conclusion is still a conjunctive, but with a negative antecedent or consequent depending on the form.
5
Handle particular premises
In the third and fourth conjunctions, the conjunctive is particular (affirmative or negative). The conclusion is also particular, mirroring the conjunctive's quantity. Generalise: by systematically varying the disjunctive's quantity and quality, you obtain all nine conjunctions.
The author leaves the remaining five as an exercise: 'so consider them'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And as for if the commonality between the conjunctive and the disjunctive is through the consequent of the conjunctive, then either through it or through a part of it. If through the consequent, then the condition for its production is universality of one of the two premises and affirmation of one of them, and its productive pairs are nine, except that the conclusion will be a conjunctive affirmative if the disjunctive is affirmative, and a disjunctive negative if the disjunctive is negative: The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives, like our saying: 'Always either B is J or D is R' and 'Every H is A so D is R' and its conclusion is 'Every H is R so B is not J' and from D is R necessarily follows the negation of B is J due to the impossibility of their combination. The second conjunction: the disjunctive as it is, except that the conjunctive is universal negative, like our saying: 'It is never the case that if H is A then D is R' and its conclusion is 'Every H is A so D is not R' and from that follows the existence of B is J due to the impossibility of being empty of both. The third conjunction: the disjunctive as it is, except that the conjunctive is universal affirmative, like our saying: 'It may be that if H is A then D is R' and its conclusion is 'It may be that if H is A then B is not J'. The fourth conjunction: the disjunctive as it is, except that the conjunctive is particular negative, like our saying: 'Not every H is A so D is R' and its conclusion is 'It may be that if H is A then B is J'. And on this, the production is not hidden when the disjunctive is particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, and they are other conjunctions, so consider them.
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage explains the rules for mixed syllogisms where the common part between a conjunctive (conditional) and a disjunctive proposition is the whole consequent of the conjunctive. The author states that for a conclusion to be produced, one premise must be universal and one affirmative, yielding nine productive forms. The conclusion takes the form of a conjunctive if the disjunctive is affirmative, and a disjunctive negative if the disjunctive is negative. Four examples are given: (1) both premises universal affirmative, (2) disjunctive unchanged but conjunctive universal negative, (3) disjunctive unchanged but conjunctive universal affirmative, (4) disjunctive unchanged but conjunctive particular negative. Each example shows the resulting conclusion. The author notes that analogous reasoning applies when the disjunctive is particular affirmative, universal negative, or particular negative, encouraging the student to work them out.

Key Terms
متصلة (muttasila): A conjunctive conditional proposition (e.g., 'If A then B') used in combination with a disjunctive. [Note: No significant shift; standard usage in logic.]
منفصلة (munfasila): A disjunctive proposition (e.g., 'Either A or B') used in combination with a conjunctive. [Note: No significant shift; standard usage.]
التالي (tālī): The consequent of a conditional proposition (the part after 'then'). [Note: No significant shift.]
اقتران (iqtirān): A conjunction or combination of propositions that yields a syllogism; here, a specific pairing of premises. [Note: In this text, iqtirān refers to a 'mood' of a mixed syllogism.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In mixed syllogisms where the common part is the whole consequent, the nine productive conjunctions are governed by the conditions of universality and affirmation, with the conclusion mirroring the disjunctive's quality.

The Syllogism

Mixed Syllogisms: Common Part within the Consequent
Explains subcases when common part is part of consequent and lists seven exemplary conjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subcases and conditions for productive mixed syllogisms where the common part is part of the consequent, and construct the conclusion for any of the seven exemplary conjunctions.

Core Idea
The mixed syllogism with a shared part in the consequent reduces to first figure under uniform disjuncts.

This lesson dives into a specific type of mixed syllogism: when the shared term is not the entire consequent but only a part of it (its subject or predicate). The author explains the conditions that make such syllogisms productive and illustrates seven exemplars. You will learn to navigate the many conjunctions systematically.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two subcases
The common part (mawḍūʿ or maḥmūl) can be either the subject or the predicate of the consequent. The author first separates these two branches and then focuses on one: when it is a predicate in the disjunction.
2
Recognize the first figure form
When the common part is a predicate in the disjunctive parts, the resulting syllogism follows the shape of the first figure (shakl awwal). Note the conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative, and the consequent of the conjunctive must be universal if the conjunctive is affirmative, particular if negative.
One of the premises must be universal overall.
3
Understand the 48 productive conjunctions
There are 48 possible productive conjunctions (iqtiranāt). The author gives seven as exemplars for the seven possible combinations of the conjunctive's quantity/quality and the consequent's quantity/quality. Master these exemplars to extrapolate the rest.
4
Analyze the first exemplar
Exemplar #1: Always either every B is D or every D is J (disjunctive universal affirmative) and Every H is R so every J is A (conjunctive universal affirmative). The conclusion is: Every H is R so always either every B is A or every D is A.
Observe how the consequent's predicate 'A' is combined with each disjunct.
5
Compare with the other exemplars
The other six exemplars vary the quantity/quality of the conjunctive and the consequent. For example, exemplar #5 uses a universal negative conjunctive with a particular negative consequent, and the conclusion follows the first exemplar's pattern because the negative essentially inverts the quantifiers.
Work through each to see how the conclusion's quantity and quality track the conjunctive.

And as for if the commonality is through a part of the consequent, then either through its subject or its predicate. If through its subject, then either it is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction or a subject in them. If it is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction, then it is in the form of the first figure. And let the supposition be that the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, and the condition for its production is affirmation of the disjunctive, and that the consequent of the conjunctive be universal if the conjunctive is affirmative, and particular if it is negative, and that one of the two premises be universal, and its productive pairs are forty-eight conjunctions, and the conclusion in all is a conjunctive affirmative, its antecedent is the antecedent of the conjunctive, and its consequent is the result of combining the disjunctive with the consequent, and the conclusion follows the conjunctive in quantity and quality: The first conjunction from two universal affirmatives and the parts of the disjunctive are universal affirmatives, and the consequent is universal affirmative, like our saying: 'Always either every B is D or every D is J' and 'Every H is R so every J is A' so 'Every H is R so always either every B is A or every D is A'. The second conjunction: the conjunction as it is, except that the consequent is universal negative, like our saying: 'And every H is R so no J is A' so 'Every H is R so always either no B is A or no D is A'. The third conjunction: the disjunctive as it is, except that the conjunctive is particular affirmative and the consequent is universal affirmative, like our saying: 'It may be that if H is R then every J is A' and its conclusion is particular conjunctive: 'It may be that if H is R then either every B is A or every D is A'. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction as it is except that the consequent is universal negative, like our saying: 'It may be that if H is R then no J is A' so 'It may be that if H is R then either no B is A or no D is A'. The fifth conjunction: the disjunctive as it is, except that the conjunctive is universal negative and the consequent is particular negative, like our saying: 'It is never the case that if H is R then some J is not A' and its conclusion like the first; because if H is R, then 'Every J is A' is true. The sixth conjunction: the conjunction as it is, except that the consequent is particular affirmative, like our saying: 'It is never the case that if H is R then some J is A' and its conclusion like the second; because if H is R, then no J is A. The seventh conjunction: the disjunctive as it is, except that the conjunctive is particular negative and the consequent is particular negative, like our saying: 'Not every H is R so some J is not A' and its conclusion is 'It may be that if H is R then some B is not A or some D is not A'.

Commentary
This passage details the rules for mixed syllogisms when the common part is not the whole consequent but a part of it (either its subject or predicate). The author first distinguishes two subcases: the common part may be the subject or the predicate of the consequent. He then focuses on the subcase where the common part is the predicate in the parts of the disjunction, which follows the form of the first figure. Conditions are stated: the disjunctive must be affirmative, the consequent of the conjunctive must be universal if the conjunctive is affirmative and particular if it is negative, and one of the premises must be universal. There are 48 productive conjunctions, and the conclusion is always a conjunctive affirmative that matches the conjunctive in quantity and quality. Seven exemplary conjunctions are then given, each with its example of terms (B, D, J, H, R, A). The first two have universal disjunctives and universal conjunctive (affirmative and negative). The third and fourth have particular conjunctive with universal consequent. The fifth and sixth have universal negative conjunctive with particular consequent. The seventh has particular negative conjunctive with particular consequent. The author shows how the conclusion is derived in each case.

Eight Productive Conjunctions of the First Figure for Mixed Disjunctive-Conditional Syllogisms
Author enumerates eight productive conjunctions of the first figure for mixed disjunctive-conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the eight productive conjunctions for mixed disjunctive-conditional syllogisms in the first figure.

Core Idea
Eight conjunctions cover all valid mixes of a disjunctive and a conditional premise in the first figure.

This passage systematically enumerates the eight valid conjunctions (iqtiranat) for a compound syllogism that combines a disjunctive premise with a conditional premise in the first figure. Understanding these forms equips you to construct proofs in Islamic theology where such arguments arise, for example when reasoning about exclusive divine attributes. The author uses Arabic variables to illustrate each case, and then notes that forty more conjunctions can be derived by varying the parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of the compound syllogism
Each conjunction consists of a disjunctive premise (e.g., 'always either every B is D or every D is C') and a conditional premise (e.g., 'if H is R then every C is A'). The conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the conditional premise and a consequent that is a disjunction built from the original disjunctive parts and the original consequent.
2
Recognize the first figure condition
The first figure here means that the subject of the conditional's antecedent (represented by 'H is R') appears as the subject of the disjuncts in the conclusion. This ensures the premises share a common subject for proper inference.
3
List the eight conjunctions by varying quality and quantity
The eight cases differ by whether the conditional and disjunctive are universal or particular, affirmative or negative. For each, the author gives an example and the resulting conclusion. For instance, the first conjunction has two universal affirmatives and yields a universal affirmative conclusion; the second has a universal affirmative disjunctive and a universal negative conditional, yielding a universal negative conclusion.
Memorize the pattern: universal conditional gives universal conclusion; particular conditional gives particular conclusion.
4
Check productivity conditions
The author implicitly states that one premise must be universal for productivity. Also, if the conditional is affirmative, the conclusion is universal; if negative, the conclusion is particular. These conditions ensure the syllogism yields a valid conclusion.
After the eight, the author mentions forty more conjunctions if the parts of the disjunction are varied (e.g., particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative). These follow the same principles.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين وأجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة، والتالي كلي موجب، كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "د" وإما كل "د" "ج") و(كل ما كان فكل "ج" "أ") فـ (كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما كل "ب" وإما كل "د" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن التالي كلي سالب كقولنا: (وكل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فلا شيء من "ج" "أ") فـ (كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما لا شيء من "ب" وإما لا شيء من "د" ). الاقتران الثالث: المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة جزئية موجبة والتالي كلي موجب كقولنا : وقد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فكل "ج" "أ") ونتيجته جزئية متصلة، (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فإما كل "ب" "أ" وإما كل وهي "د" "أ"). الاقتران الرابع: الاقتران بحاله إلا أن التالي كلي سالب، كقولنا: (وقد يكون إذا "ر" فلا شيء من "ج" "أ") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فإما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "د" "أ"). الاقتران الخامس: المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن الكلية سالبة، والتالي فيها سالب كقولنا وليس البتة إذا كان ر" فبعض "ج" ليس "أ") ونتيجته كالأول؛ لأنه إذا كان "هـ" "ر") صدق (كل "ج" "أ"). الاقتران السادس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن التالي جزئي موجب كقولنا وليس البتة إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فبعض "ج" "أ") ونتيجته كالثاني؛ لأنه إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فلا شيء من "ج" "أ". الاقتران السابع : المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة جزئية سالبة، والتالي جزئي سالب كقولنا: (وليس كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فبعض "ج" ليس "أ") ونتيجته (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فإما كل "ب" "أ" وإما كل "د" "أ"). الاقتران الثامن الاقتران بحاله إلا أن التالي جزئي موجب، كقولنا: (وليس دائماً إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فبعض "ج" "أ") ونتيجته قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فإما لا شيء من "ب" "أ" وإما لا شيء من "د" "أ"). وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة، وكلية سالبة، وجزئية سالبة، وكذا إذا كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة، وهي أربعون اقتراناً أخرى، فاعتبرها إذ هي سهلة الاعتبار.
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, and the consequent is universal affirmative, as we say: (Always either every B is D or every D is C) and (Everything that is such that every C is A) so (Everything that is H is R always either every B is A or every D is A). The second conjunction: the conjunction remains the same except that the consequent is universal negative, as we say: (And everything that is H is R so that nothing of C is A) so (Everything that is H is R always either nothing of B is A or nothing of D is A). The third conjunction: the disjunctive remains the same except that the conditional is particular affirmative and the consequent is universal affirmative, as we say: (And it may be that if H is R then every C is A) and its conclusion is a particular conditional, (It may be that if H is R then either every B is A or every D is A). The fourth conjunction: the conjunction remains the same except that the consequent is universal negative, as we say: (And it may be that if R then nothing of C is A) so (It may be that if H is R then either nothing of B is A or nothing of D is A). The fifth conjunction: the disjunctive remains the same except that the universal is negative, and the consequent in it is negative, as we say: (It is not at all that if R then some C is not A) and its conclusion is like the first; because if H is R, every C is A holds true. The sixth conjunction: the conjunction remains the same except that the consequent is particular affirmative, as we say: (It is not at all that if H is R then some C is A) and its conclusion is like the second; because if H is R then nothing of C is A. The seventh conjunction: the disjunctive remains the same except that the conditional is particular negative, and the consequent is particular negative, as we say: (It is not that everything that is H is R then some C is not A) and its conclusion is (It may be that if H is R then either every B is A or every D is A). The eighth conjunction: the conjunction remains the same except that the consequent is particular affirmative, as we say: (It is not always that if H is R then some C is A) and its conclusion is (It may be that if H is R then either nothing of B is A or nothing of D is A). And based on this, the production is not hidden if the parts of the disjunction are particular affirmative, universal negative, and particular negative, and also if the conditional is particular affirmative, and they are forty other conjunctions, so consider them, for they are easy to consider.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage enumerates eight productive conjunctions (iqtiranat) of the first figure for a mixed syllogism composed of a disjunctive (munfasila) and a conditional (muttasila). The first figure here means that the subject of the conditional (the 'subject' of 'if H is R') is the same as the subject of the parts of the disjunction (the variables appear as subjects in both). The author first states general conditions: the conditional must be affirmative for universal conclusions and particular for negative ones; one of the two premises must be universal; and the conclusion is a conditional with the same antecedent as the conditional premise and a consequent that is the result of combining the disjunctive with the original consequent. Then he lists eight specific cases, each varying the quantity and quality of the conditional, the disjunctive, or the consequent. For each, he gives an example using logical variables (ب, ج, د, أ) and the placeholder 'هـ' and 'ر' for the subject and predicate of the conditional's antecedent. The examples show how to derive the conclusion. After the eight, he notes that by similarly varying the parts of the disjunctive (e.g., particular affirmative, universal negative, particular negative) and the conditional when it is particular affirmative, there are forty more productive conjunctions, which he leaves for the reader to deduce. This is typical of the detailed enumeration in Arabic logic manuals.

Structure of Compound Syllogisms from Conditional and Disjunctive Premises
Author enumerates eight compound syllogisms from conditional and disjunctive premises with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a compound syllogism combining a conditional and a disjunctive premise, and construct the conclusion pattern for the first conjunction.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms combine conditional and disjunctive premises into a single conditional conclusion.

In this passage, the author describes how to form compound syllogisms by combining a conditional (if-then) proposition with a disjunctive (either-or) proposition, where the consequent of the conditional matches the subject terms of the disjunction. This lesson introduces the basic structure and the first of eight specific patterns, using the example of the first conjunction from both universal affirmative premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Find the conditional premise (متصلة) and the disjunctive premise (منفصلة). In the first conjunction, the disjunctive is 'دائماً إما كل ب ج أو كل ب د' and the conditional is 'كل ما كان هـ ر فكل ب أ'.
The conditional's consequent is 'كل ب أ', which matches the subject term 'ب' in the disjunction.
2
Determine the conclusion structure
The conclusion is a new conditional with the same antecedent ('كل ما كان هـ ر') and a consequent that is a disjunction derived from the original disjunction's parts and the conditional's consequent.
In the first conjunction, the conclusion is 'كل ما كان هـ ر فدائماً إما بعض ج أ وإما بعض د أ'.
3
Note the change in quantity
The original disjunction parts were universal affirmative ('كل ب ج', 'كل ب د'), but in the conclusion, they become particular affirmative ('بعض ج أ', 'بعض د أ'). This is because the conditional's consequent 'كل ب أ' distributes 'ب' to all 'أ', so the parts are subalternated.
This pattern holds when both premises are universal affirmative.

Continuation of the first art: On defining the forms of categorical syllogisms. If 'H' is 'R', then either every 'B' is 'A' or every 'D' is 'A'). The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, and the consequent is universal affirmative, as we say: Always either every 'B' is 'J' or every 'B' is 'D') and (Everything that is 'H' is 'R' so every 'B' is 'A') and its conclusion: Everything that is 'H' is 'R' so always either some 'J' is 'A' or some 'D' is 'A'). The second conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the consequent is universal negative, as we say: And everything that is something from 'B' is 'A') so (Everything that is 'H' is 'R' so always either not every 'D' is 'A' or not every 'J' is 'A'). The third conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the consequent is particular affirmative, as we say: And everything that is 'H' is 'R' so some 'B' is 'A' and its conclusion is like the first. The fourth conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the consequent is particular negative, as we say: (And everything that is 'H' is 'R' so not every 'B' is 'A') and its conclusion is like the second. The fifth conjunction: the disjunction in its state, except that the conditional is universal negative, and the consequent is particular negative, as we say: (And not at all if 'H' is 'R' then some 'B' is not 'A') and its conclusion is like the first; because if 'H' is 'R' then (every 'B' is 'A'). The sixth conjunction: the conjunction in its state except that the consequent is particular affirmative, as we say: ((And not at all if 'H' is 'R' then some 'B' is 'A') and its conclusion is like the second; because if 'H' is 'R' then (nothing from 'B' is 'A'). The seventh conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the consequent is universal negative, as we say: (And not at all if 'H' is 'R') then ( nothing from 'B' is 'A') and its conclusion is like the third; because if ('H' is 'R') then (some 'B' is 'A'). The eighth conjunction: the conjunction in its state, except that the consequent is universal affirmative, as we say: (And not at all if ( 'H' is 'R') then (every 'B' is 'A') and its conclusion is like the fourth; because if ('D' is 'R') then (some 'B' is not 'A').
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of compound syllogisms that combine a conditional premise (متصل) and a disjunctive premise (منفصل) where the consequent of the conditional is the subject of the disjunction's parts. The author presents eight specific conjunctions (اقترانات) with varying quantity and quality of the premises and parts. Each conjunction is defined by the type of conditional (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) and the type of disjunction parts (universal/particular, affirmative/negative). The conclusion is always a conditional whose antecedent is the original conditional's antecedent and whose consequent is a disjunction derived from the original disjunction's parts and the conditional's consequent. The patterns show how altering the quantity or quality changes the conclusion. For example, in the first conjunction, both premises are universal affirmative, and the conclusion is a universal conditional with a particular disjunction. In the second, the consequent is universal negative, leading to a conclusion with negated parts. The author also notes that when the conditional is universal negative (الخامس to الثامن), the conclusion is derived by converting the conditional's consequent. This section is foundational for understanding composite syllogisms in Islamic logic.

The Eight Conjunctions: Varying Quantity and Quality
Author enumerates eight compound syllogisms from conditional and disjunctive premises with conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the conclusion for each of the eight conjunctions by tracing changes in quantity and quality of premises.

Core Idea
Eight patterns emerge from altering the quantity and quality of the conditional's consequent and the disjunction's parts.

The author expands the first pattern into eight distinct conjunctions by varying whether the conditional's consequent and the disjunction's parts are universal or particular, affirmative or negative. This lesson systematically examines how each variation leads to a different conclusion, showing the logical possibilities of compound syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Recall the base pattern
The first conjunction uses universal affirmative premises. The conclusion particularizes the disjunction parts. Keep this as reference.
2
Examine conjunctions 2-4: change only the consequent
The second conjunction changes the consequent to universal negative; the conclusion negates both parts. The third uses particular affirmative consequent, conclusion remains particular affirmative. The fourth uses particular negative consequent, conclusion negates parts.
The conclusion form mirrors the quantity/quality of the original consequent in a specific way.
3
Examine conjunctions 5-8: conditional is universal negative
When the conditional premise is universal negative (e.g., 'Not at all if H is R then ...'), the conclusion is derived by negating the consequent of the original conditional. The author explains 'because if H is R then ...' and gives a reversed consequent.
For example, fifth conjunction: the conditional is 'لا إذا هـ ر فبعض ب ليس أ', leading to conclusion like first but with the consequent reversed to 'كل ب أ'.
4
Identify the pattern of reversal
When the conditional is universal negative, the conclusion's consequent is the opposite (affirmative/negative, universal/particular) of the original consequent. This is because the negation of the conditional yields the complementary affirmative.
The author explicitly states 'لأنه إذا كان هـ ر فـ (كل ب أ)' for the fifth conjunction.

The Five Conjugations of Universal Negative Conditional with Disjunctive
Enumerates five productive conjugations of conditional-disjunctive syllogisms and extends to particular conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five productive conjugations formed by a universal negative conditional with a universal affirmative disjunctive, and determine the corresponding categorical syllogism figure for each.

Core Idea
Conditional-disjunctive syllogisms yield five distinct valid conclusions, each matching a categorical figure.

After establishing the basic conditional syllogisms, the author now explores combinations of a universal negative conditional with a universal affirmative disjunctive. By varying the quantity and quality of the conditional's consequent, five productive conjugations emerge, each leading to a conclusion analogous to one of the four categorical figures. Understanding this pattern is key to mastering compound syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the invariant premise
Note that the disjunctive remains universal affirmative with identical parts, and the conditional is universal negative. This is the fixed structure for the five conjugations (fourth to eighth).
The disjunctive is assumed from earlier lessons; here it is 'بحالها' (unchanged).
2
Examine the consequent variations
In each conjugation, the consequent of the conditional changes: in the fourth it is particular negative, fifth particular negative again but with different result, sixth particular affirmative, seventh universal negative, eighth universal affirmative.
The fifth and fourth both have particular negative consequents but differ in the conditional type? Actually the text says the fifth has conditional universal negative and consequent particular negative, so it's the same as fourth? Possibly a typo; but the pattern is systematic.
3
Derive the conclusion
For each conjugation, the author states the conclusion is 'like the first/second/third/fourth figure'. Understand the reasoning: the conclusion's form is determined by the consequent's scalar (universal/particular) and modal (affirmative/negative) properties.
For example, in the fourth conjugation, the conclusion is like the second figure (كالثاني).
4
Recognize the pattern for extension
The author then extends to particular conditionals: if the conditional is particular affirmative, four parallel conjugations arise with particular affirmative conclusions; if particular negative, four with particular negative conclusions. The same pattern applies when the disjunctive's parts are particular.
This exhaustive enumeration is typical in Islamic logic to ensure completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الاقتران الخامس: المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة كلية سالبة، والتالي جزئي سالب كقولنا: ) وليس البتة إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فبعض "ب" ليس "أ") ونتيجته كالأول؛ لأنه إذا كان "هـ" "ر") فـ (كل "ب" "أ").

الاقتران السادس الاقتران بحاله إلا أن التالي جزئي موجب، كقولنا: ((وليس البتة إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فبعض "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كالثاني؛ لأنه إذا كان "هـ" "ر") فـ (لا شيء من "ب" "أ").
الاقتران السابع: الاقتران ،بحاله، إلا أن التالي كلي سالب كقولنا: (وليس البتة إذا كان "هـ" "ر") فـ ( لا شيء من "ب" "أ") ونتيجته كالثالث؛ لأنه إذا كان ("هـ" "ر") فـ (بعض "ب" "أ").
