وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية موجبة تألف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة، موازية للاقترانات الأربعة الأولى، إلا أن النتيجة فيها جزئية موجبة. وإن كانت المتصلة جزئية سالبة تألف منها مع المنفصلة اقترانات أخرى أربعة، موازية للأربعة الثانية، والنتيجة فيها أيضاً جزئية.
وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الإنتاج إذا كانت أجزاء المنفصلة جزئية موجبة سالبة، أو جزئية سالبة، أو كانت المنفصلة جزئية موجبة وهي ستة وخمسون اقتراناً أخرى، فاعتبرها فإنها سهلة الاعتبار.
The fourth conjugation: The conjugation remains the same, except that the consequent is particular negative, as in our statement: (And everything that is R, then not every B is A) and its result is like the second. The fifth conjugation: The disjunctive remains the same, except that the conditional is universal negative, and the consequent is particular negative, as in our statement: (It is not at all that if H is R, then some B is not A) and its result is like the first; because if H is R, then every B is A. The sixth conjugation: The conjugation remains the same except that the consequent is particular affirmative, as in our statement: ((It is not at all that if H is R, then some B is A) and its result is like the second; because if H is R, then no B is A). The seventh conjugation: The conjugation remains the same, except that the consequent is universal negative, as in our statement: (It is not at all that if H is R, then no B is A) and its result is like the third; because if H is R, then some B is A). The eighth conjugation: The conjugation remains the same, except that the consequent is universal affirmative, as in our statement: (It is not at all that if H is R, then every B is A) and its result is like the fourth; because if D is R, then some B is not A). And if the conditional is particular affirmative, there are formed from it with the disjunctive another four conjugations, parallel to the first four, except that the result in them is particular affirmative. And if the conditional is particular negative, there are formed from it with the disjunctive another four conjugations, parallel to the second four, and the result in them is also particular. And on this basis, the production is not hidden if the parts of the disjunctive are particular affirmative negative, or particular negative, or if the disjunctive is particular affirmative. These are another fifty-six conjugations, so consider them; they are easy to consider.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of productive conjugations (اقترانات) in conditional-disjunctive syllogisms. The author first lists five conjugations (fourth to eighth) under the assumption that the conditional premise is universal negative and the disjunctive is universal affirmative with identical parts. Each conjugation varies the quantity and quality of the consequent of the conditional, and the conclusion is derived similarly to a previously mentioned simple categorical syllogism (kathani, awwal, thalith, rabi'). The author then extends the discussion to cases where the conditional is particular affirmative or particular negative, each yielding four parallel conjugations. Finally, a general remark is made about fifty-six additional conjugations when the disjunctive itself has particular parts. The text uses symbolic variables (أ, ب, ج, د, هـ, ر) as placeholders. For example, in the fourth conjugation, the conjunctive premise 'كل ما كان ر فليس كل ب أ' (Everything that is R, not every B is A) is a universal affirmative conjunctive with a particular negative consequent, and its conclusion is like the second figure's conclusion. The author's aim is to systematically cover all productive combinations, emphasizing that once the pattern is understood, the rest are easy to derive. This mirrors the method in Islamic logic of exhaustive classification of syllogistic forms.

Key Terms
اقتران
متصلة
منفصلة
تالي

Key Takeaway
The five conjugations show that universal negative conditionals with universal affirmative disjunctives produce conclusions mirroring categorical syllogisms, with the figure determined by the consequent's quantity and quality.

Compound Syllogisms with Shared Predicate of Consequent
Lists four conjugations for compound syllogisms with shared predicate of consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a productive compound syllogism when the shared term is the predicate of the consequent, and to reconstruct the first four conjugations.

Core Idea
When the shared term is the predicate of the consequent, the syllogism resembles the second figure.

We have already studied compound syllogisms where the shared term is the subject of both premises. Now we turn to a new case: the shared term is the predicate of the conditional's consequent. This changes the form and the conditions for productivity. The author explains that this structure is analogous to the second figure of categorical syllogisms, and lists conditions such as the disjunctive must be affirmative, its parts uniform in quality, and one premise universal. There are forty-eight productive conjugations, of which the first four are detailed. Understanding this pattern is essential for analyzing mixed conditional-disjunctive arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared term's position
In this passage, the common term (ishtirāk) is the predicate of the consequent (maḥmūl al-tālī) of the conditional. Recognize that this term appears in the disjunctive parts, either as predicate or subject. The passage says: 'if it is a predicate in them, then it is in the form of the second figure'.
The second figure in categorical syllogisms has the middle term as predicate in both premises.
2
List the conditions for productivity
The author gives four conditions: the disjunctive must be affirmative, its parts must be uniform in quality (both affirmative or both negative), one of the two premises must be universal, and the consequent of the conditional must be universal if affirmative, particular if negative.
These conditions ensure the syllogism yields a valid conclusion.
3
Analyse the structure of the conclusion
The result (natīja) is an affirmative conditional proposition whose antecedent (muqaddam) is the antecedent of the original conditional, and whose consequent (tālī) is the result of combining the original consequent with the disjunctive. The quantity of the conclusion follows the original conditional.
For example, if the original conditional is universal, the conclusion is universal affirmative conditional.
4
Examine the first two conjugations
In the first conjugation, both premises are universal affirmative, and the consequent is universal negative. The resulting conditional has a disjunctive consequent with two universal negatives. In the second conjugation, the consequent becomes universal affirmative, changing the disjunctive consequent to two particular negatives.
Work through the provided symbolic example: 'Always either every B is J or every D is J' with 'Everything that is H is R from A is J'.
5
Extend to the third and fourth conjugations
The third conjugation uses a universal negative conditional with a particular affirmative consequent, yielding a result similar to the first conjugation. The fourth uses a universal negative conditional with a particular negative consequent, similar to the second. Note how the negation of the conditional changes the relationship.
The author then mentions that if the conditional is particular (affirmative or negative), only two parallel conjugations exist, with a particular affirmative conditional as result.

And if the sharing is by the predicate of the consequent, then either it is a predicate in the parts of the disjunction or a subject in them. If it is a predicate in them, then it is in the form of the second figure, and let the assumption also be that the parts of the disjunctive are uniform in quantity and quality, and the condition of its production is that the disjunctive is affirmative, that its parts are uniform in quality, that one of the two premises is universal, and that the consequent of the conditional is universal if it is affirmative, and particular if it is negative. Its productive conjugations are forty-eight conjugations, and the result in all is an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of the conjunction of the consequent with the disjunctive, and it follows the conditional in quantity. The first conjugation: from two universal affirmatives, and the parts of the disjunction are universal affirmatives, and the consequent is universal negative, as in our statement: (Always either every B is J or every D is J) and (Everything that is H is R from A is J) and its result: Everything that is H is R, so always either nothing of B is A or nothing of D is A. The second conjugation: The conjugation remains the same, except that the consequent is universal affirmative, as in our statement: And everything that is H is R, so always either some B is not A or some B is not D, because if 'every B is J' is true, then 'some D is not J' is true, and if 'every D is J' is true, then 'some B is not J' is true, and all A is J). The third conjugation: The disjunctive remains the same, except that the conditional is universal negative, and its consequent is particular affirmative, as in our statement: (It is not at all that if H is R, then some A is J) and its result is like the first; because if H is R, then 'nothing of A is J' is true. The fourth conjugation: The conjugation remains the same, except that the consequent is particular negative, as in our statement: (It is not at all that if H is R, then some A is not J) and its result is like the second; because if H is R, then 'all A is J' is true, and either every B is J or every D is J. If 'every B is J' is true, then 'not every D is J' is true, and if 'every D is J' is true, then 'not every B is J' is true. And if the conditional is particular affirmative, then there may be formed from it with the disjunctive another two conjugations parallel to the first two. And if the conditional is particular negative, then there may be formed from it with the disjunctive another two conjugations parallel to the other two, except that the result in them is a particular affirmative conditional.

Commentary
This passage introduces a new type of compound syllogism (اقتران) where the common term between the conditional and disjunctive is the predicate of the conditional's consequent. The author explains that if the common term is a predicate in the disjunctive parts, the syllogism resembles the second figure of categorical syllogisms. Conditions for productivity are stated: the disjunctive must be affirmative, its parts uniform in quality, one premise universal, and the consequent's quantity depends on the conditional's quality. There are forty-eight such productive conjugations. The author then enumerates the first four conjugations in this category, each varying the quantity and quality of the consequent. For example, the first conjugation has a universal negative consequent and yields a conclusion that is an affirmative conditional with a disjunctive consequent. The second conjugation has a universal affirmative consequent, and the reasoning is explained through sub-syllogisms. The third and fourth conjugations involve a conditional that is universal negative. Finally, the author notes that if the conditional is particular, only two conjugations are formed instead of four, and the result is a particular affirmative conditional. This passage deepens the exhaustive treatment of mixed syllogisms, showing the pattern for the case where the shared term is the predicate of the consequent. The examples use symbolic letters, which are typical in Arabic logic texts.

Conditions for Productive Disjunctive-Conditional Conjunctions
Author enumerates general conditions and four examples of productive conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four conditions for a productive conjunction of a disjunctive and a conditional premise, and apply them to validate example conjunctions.

Core Idea
Four conditions govern whether a disjunctive-conditional pair yields a valid conclusion.

In conjunctive syllogisms (القياسات الاقترانية), when a disjunctive premise is paired with a conditional premise, not every combination yields a conclusion. The author states precise conditions for productivity and illustrates with four examples. This lesson will guide you through parsing these conditions and recognising how they operate in specific conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
Locate the disjunctive (المنفصلة) in the examples. In the first conjunction, it is 'دائماً إما كل ب ج وإما كل د ج' — an affirmative disjunction with universal affirmative parts. Note that the text requires the parts to be uniform in quality.
All four examples use the same disjunctive.
2
Identify the conditional premise
Find the conditional (المتصلة) in each example. Its quality and the quantity of its consequent vary. For instance, the first conjunction uses 'كل ما كان هـ ر فكل أ ج' (universal affirmative conditional with universal affirmative consequent).
The consequent's quantity must match the conditional's quality as per condition (d).
3
Extract the four general conditions
From the author's opening statement, list: (a) the disjunctive must be affirmative, (b) its parts uniform in quality, (c) one of the two premises must be universal, (d) the consequent of the conditional must be universal if the conditional is affirmative, and particular if negative.
These conditions ensure the conjunction produces a valid conclusion.
4
Apply conditions to the examples
Check each of the four conjunctions against the conditions. For example, the first conjunction satisfies all: disjunctive affirmative and uniform, both premises universal, conditional affirmative so consequent universal. Verify the given conclusion matches the pattern.
The conclusion always has the same antecedent as the conditional; the consequent is a disjunction derived from the original disjunctive using the conditional's consequent.

Continuation of the first art: on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. Its productivity: the disjunctive must be affirmative, its parts must be uniform in quality, one of the two premises must be universal, and the consequent of the conditional must be universal if it is affirmative, and particular if it is negative. Its productive conjunctions are forty-eight. The conclusion in all of them is an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the disjunctive, and it follows the conditional in quantity.
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, the parts of the disjunction being universal affirmatives, and the consequent being universal negative, like our statement: "Always either every B is C or every D is C" and "Every H is R has every A as C" and its conclusion: "Every H is R has always either no B is A or no D is A".
The second conjunction: the conjunction as such, except that the consequent is universal affirmative, like our statement: "And every H is R has always either some B is not A or some D is not A" because if "Every B is C" is true, then "Some D is not C" is true; and if "Every D is C" is true, then "Some B is not C" is true, and "Every A is C".
The third conjunction: the disjunctive as such, except that the conditional is universal negative, and its consequent is particular affirmative, like our statement: "It is absolutely not the case that if H is R then some A is C" and its conclusion like the first; because if H is R, then "No A is C" is true.
The fourth conjunction: the conjunction as such, except that the consequent is particular negative, like our statement: "It is absolutely not the case that if H is R then some A is not C" and its conclusion like the second; because if H is R, then "Every A is C" is true, and either every B is C or every D is C; if every B is C is true, then not every D is C; and if every D is C, then not every B is C.
And if the conditional is particular affirmative, then there result from it and the disjunctive two other conjunctions parallel to the first two. And if the conditional is particular negative, then there result from it and the disjunctive two other conjunctions parallel to the other two, except that the conclusion in them is a particular affirmative conditional.

Commentary
This passage is a technical enumeration of productive conjunctions (اقترانات) formed from a disjunctive premise (منفصلة) and a conditional premise (متصلة) in conjunctive syllogisms. The author begins by stating the general conditions for productivity: the disjunctive must be affirmative, its parts must be uniform in quality (i.e., all affirmative or all negative), one of the two premises must be universal, and the consequent of the conditional must be universal if the conditional is affirmative and particular if it is negative. There are 48 such productive conjunctions. The conclusion in all cases is an affirmative conditional where the antecedent is the antecedent of the given conditional, and the consequent is the result of conjoining that antecedent with the disjunctive; the quantity (universal or particular) of the conclusion follows the quantity of the conditional.

Then the author illustrates with four examples using logical variables. In each example, the disjunctive is 'Always either every B is C or every D is C' (affirmative and universal). The conditional varies. The first conjunction has the conditional universal affirmative with a universal negative consequent: 'Every H is R has every A as C' (though the consequent 'every A is C' is positive, the text says 'كلي سالب' perhaps meaning the conditional's consequent is universal negative? Actually it says 'والتالي كلي سالب' meaning the consequent of the conditional is universal negative. But the example shows 'كل أ ج' which is affirmative. There might be a typo or it means the consequent in the conclusion is universal negative. Actually re-reading: the example conditional is 'كل ما كان هـ ر فكل أ ج' which is universal affirmative. Then the conclusion has 'لا شيء من ب أ' and 'لا شيء من د أ', which are universal negatives. So the consequent in the conditional is affirmative, but the conclusion's disjuncts are negative. The text says 'والتالي كلي سالب' referring to the consequent of the conditional? It says 'والتالي كلي سالب' meaning the consequent of the conditional is universal negative? But that contradicts the example. Possibly the text means the consequent of the conditional in the example is actually negative, but the example given is ambiguous. Alternatively, the condition 'والتالي من المتصلة كلياً إن كانت موجبة' means the consequent should be universal if the conditional is affirmative, and particular if negative. The examples seem to vary the conditional's quality. The commentary should note this confusion. For simplicity, we follow the text as given.

The second conjunction has the same disjunctive but a universal affirmative conditional with a universal affirmative consequent, and the conclusion has particular negatives.

The third conjunction has a universal negative conditional ('ليس البتة إذا كان هـ ر فبعض أ ج') with a particular affirmative consequent, and the conclusion is like the first (universal negatives).

The fourth conjunction has a universal negative conditional with a particular negative consequent, and the conclusion like the second (particular negatives).

Finally, the author notes that if the conditional is particular affirmative, two more conjunctions parallel to the first two arise; if it is particular negative, two more parallel to the last two arise, but the conclusion becomes particular affirmative conditional. This completes the enumeration of the first set of 48 conjunctions.

Parsing the Fourth Figure in Mixed Syllogisms
Author presents conditions and examples when the consequent of the conditional is the subject of the disjunctive parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid mixed conditional-disjunctive syllogism when the consequent's predicate is the subject of the disjunction.

Core Idea
The fourth figure applies when the consequent's predicate becomes the subject of the disjunction.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in compound syllogisms where the predicate of a conditional's consequent serves as the subject for the parts of a disjunction. The author likens this to the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms and lays out strict conditions for the syllogism to be productive. By parsing these conditions through examples, you will learn to recognize the structural pattern and test its validity.

Guided Path
1
Locate the consequent's predicate
Identify the term that is the predicate of the consequent in the conditional premise. This term becomes the subject of the disjunctive parts.
For example, in 'كل ما كان هـ ر فكل أ ب', the predicate of the consequent is 'ب'.
2
Check the quantity and quality of the premises
Ensure that the disjunction is affirmative, one premise is universal, and the consequent of an affirmative conditional is not particular negative.
The text states: 'وأن لا يكون التالي من الموجبة المتصلة جزئياً سالباً'.
3
Apply the alternative rule if needed
If the consequent from the affirmative conditional is particular affirmative and from the negative conditional is universal negative, then the disjunctive parts must be universal affirmative or particular negative.
This is an alternative condition mentioned after the semicolon.
4
Examine the conclusion structure
The conclusion is an affirmative conditional with the same antecedent as the original conditional. Its consequent is derived from combining the original consequent with the disjunctive parts.
For example, from the first conjunction: 'كل ما كان هـ ر فدائماً إما بعض ج أ وإما بعض د أ'.
5
Test a conjunction using variables
Take one of the author's examples and verify that the premises satisfy the conditions and yield the claimed conclusion.
E.g., the first conjunction: universal affirmative disjunction and universal affirmative conditional yield a conclusion with particular affirmative disjunctive parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الاقتران الأول من كليتين موجبتين، وأجزاء المنفصلة كليات موجبة، والتالي كلي موجب كقولنا: دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما كل "ب" "د") و(كل ما كان "هـ" فكل "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما بعض "ج" "أ" وإما بعض "د" "أ").
الاقتران الثاني: الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن التالي كلي سالب، كقولنا: (وكل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فلا شيء من "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فدائماً إما ليس كل "ج" "أ" وإما ليس كل "د" "أ").
الاقتران الثالث الاقتران بحاله إلا أن التالي جزئي موجب، كقولنا وكل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فبعض "أ" "ب") ونتيجته كالأول.
الاقتران الرابع: المنفصلة بحالها، إلا أن المتصلة كلية سالبة، والتالي جزئي موجب كقولنا وليس البتة إذا كانت "هـ" "ر" فبعض "أ" ليس "ب") ونتيجته كالأول؛ لأنه إذا كان "هـ" "ر") فـ (كل "أ" "ب").
الاقتران الخامس الاقتران بحاله، إلا أن التالي جزئي موجب كقولنا: (وليس
And as for if the subject of the disjunctive parts is the predicate of the consequent, then it is in the form of the fourth figure. Let the assumption be that the parts of the disjunction are uniform in quantity and quality, and the condition for its productivity is that the disjunctive be affirmative, one of the two premises be universal, and that the consequent of the affirmative conditional not be particular negative, nor of the negative conditional universal affirmative. And if the consequent of the affirmative is particular affirmative and of the negative universal negative, then the parts of the disjunction must be universal affirmative or particular negative. Its productive conjunctions are fifty-four. The conclusion in all is an affirmative conditional whose antecedent is the antecedent of the conditional, and whose consequent is the result of conjoining the consequent with the disjunctive, and it follows the conditional in quantity:
The first conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, the parts of the disjunction being universal affirmatives, and the consequent being universal affirmative, like our statement: "Always either every B is C or every B is D" and "Every H is R has every A as B" and its conclusion: "Every H is R has always either some C is A or some D is A".
The second conjunction: the conjunction as such, except that the consequent is universal negative, like our statement: "And every H is R has no A as B" and its conclusion: "Every H is R has always either not every C is A or not every D is A".
The third conjunction: the conjunction as such, except that the consequent is particular affirmative, like our statement: "And every H is R has some A as B" and its conclusion like the first.
The fourth conjunction: the disjunctive as such, except that the conditional is universal negative, and the consequent is particular affirmative, like our statement: "It is absolutely not the case that if H is R then some A is not B" and its conclusion like the first; because if H is R, then "Every A is B".
The fifth conjunction: the conjunction as such, except that the consequent is particular affirmative, like our statement: "And it is not"
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents a second scenario where the consequent (تالي) of the conditional serves as the subject for the parts of the disjunction, which the author likens to the fourth figure of categorical syllogisms. The conditions for productivity are modified accordingly: the disjunctive must be affirmative, one premise universal, and restrictions apply to the consequent: it cannot be particular negative from an affirmative conditional nor universal affirmative from a negative conditional. Alternatively, if the consequent is particular affirmative from an affirmative and universal negative from a negative, then the disjunctive parts must be universal affirmative or particular negative. There are 54 productive conjunctions in this scenario. The conclusion structure remains the same: an affirmative conditional with the same antecedent and a consequent derived from conjoining.

The author then provides five examples of conjunctions, though the last is cut off. In the first example, both premises are universal affirmative: the disjunctive 'Always either every B is C or every B is D' and the conditional 'Every H is R has every A as B' yields a conclusion with particular affirmatives ('some C is A or some D is A'). The second example changes the consequent to universal negative, yielding a conclusion with universal negatives ('not every C is A or not every D is A'). The third example uses a particular affirmative consequent, giving the same conclusion as the first. The fourth example uses a universal negative conditional with a particular affirmative consequent, again yielding the same conclusion as the first because the conditional implies 'Every A is B'. The fifth example begins similarly but is incomplete; it likely returns to a variant. These examples illustrate how the roles of subject and predicate in the disjunctive affect the resulting conclusion.

Mastering Conditional-Disjunctive Syllogisms: The Sixth Combination and Beyond
Author enumerates remaining forms of conjunctive syllogisms with conditional and disjunctive premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the form of a conditional-disjunctive syllogism and determine its conclusion pattern based on the quantifiers and quality of the premises.

Core Idea
The conclusion of a conditional-disjunctive syllogism is determined by systematically varying the quantifiers of both premises.

In this passage, the author completes the systematic enumeration of valid forms of conjunctive syllogisms that combine a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) premise. By varying the quantity and quality of the conditional and the parts of the disjunctive, an exhaustive set of possible combinations emerges. Understanding this classification is crucial for recognizing valid argument patterns in Islamic logical discourse, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh where compound premises are common.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two premise types
Identify that the first premise is a conditional (متصلة) and the second is a disjunctive (منفصلة). The text uses variables: conditional relates هـ and ر; disjunctive relates أ and ب.
The 'sixth combination' assumes a specific disjunctive premise from earlier lessons.
2
Analyze the sixth combination's conditional
The conditional in the sixth combination has a universal negative consequent: 'if H is R, then no A is B'. The author states its conclusion is like the third figure of categorical syllogisms, yielding 'some A is B'.
The conclusion is particular affirmative because the antecedent H is affirmative and the consequent is universal negative.
3
Generalize to particular conditionals
If the conditional is particular affirmative, three new combinations arise parallel to the first three basic ones. If particular negative, three more parallel to the second three. This shows the pattern depends on the conditional's quantity and quality.
The 'first three' and 'second three' basic combinations are not detailed here; they are from earlier lessons.
4
Extend to disjunctive variations
The author notes that when the disjunctive's parts are particular affirmative, particular negative, or universal negative, or the disjunctive itself is particular affirmative, forty-two additional combinations exist. The pattern is systematic and the student is to work them out.
This demonstrates the exhaustive nature of the classification.
5
Recognize the pedagogical goal
The author intentionally leaves the forty-two combinations as an exercise ('فاعتبرها بنفسك فإنها سهلة الاعتبار') to reinforce the student's grasp of the underlying systematic method.
This encourages independent logical reasoning within the Islamic logical tradition.

The Syllogism

Structure of the Exceptive Syllogism with Conjunctive Conditional
Definition and structure of the exceptive syllogism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two premises of a conjunctive conditional syllogism and determine whether the exception is of the antecedent or consequent.

Core Idea
An exceptive syllogism requires a conditional premise and an exceptive premise affirming or denying one part.

This lesson introduces the basic structure of the exceptive syllogism (qiyas istithnā'ī). Unlike the conjunctive syllogism, the conclusion is explicitly mentioned in one premise. We focus here on the conjunctive conditional type, explaining the two possibilities for exception: of the antecedent or of the consequent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional premise
Find the conditional statement in the argument. It should be a conjunctive conditional (if...then...).
2
Identify the exceptive premise
Look for the premise that affirms or denies one part of the conditional, either the antecedent or the consequent (or their contradictories).
3
Determine the target of exception
Check whether the exceptive premise relates to the antecedent or the consequent. The passage states that if the exception is of the antecedent, it can be the very same or its contradictory.
If the excepted part is the very antecedent, then the conclusion affirms the consequent (modus ponens).
4
Recognize the structure
Note that the exceptive syllogism has exactly two premises: a conditional and an exceptive. The conclusion is neither of these but follows from them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في بيان المنتج والعقيم. والاستثنائي هو بالمطلوب أو نقيضه مذكور فيه بالفعل، كما ستعرفه عن قريب وكما أنه لا يتم القياس الاقتراني دون مقدمتين، فكذلك الاستثنائي، لكن إحدى مقدمتيه لا بد وأن تكون شرطية والأخرى استثنائية لأحد جزئي الشرطية أما عينه أو نقيضه والشرطية إما أن تكون متصلة أو مفصلة، فإن كانت متصلة فالاستثناء منها أما بالمقدم أو التالي فإن كان بالمقدم فإما أن يكون المستثنى عينه أو نقيضه، فإن كان عينه لزم عنه وعن تسليم الشرطية لزوماً بيناً تسليم عين التالي، لكن شرط أن تكون المتصلة كليّة أو أن يكون الوقت للاتصال معيناً، وأن يكون ما استثني هو عين ما وضع مقدماً، وأن يكون الاتصال لزومياً حقيقياً، فلا إنتاج عما المتصلة فيه جزئية أو مهملة، كما إذا قلنا قد يكون إذا كان زيد موجوداً فهو كاتب أو يأكل فإنه ليس يلزم من قولنا: إنه موجود أنه كاتب أو يأكل إذ لم يكن ذلك لازماً لوجوده مهما كان، وكذا لا إنتاج عما المقدم فيه مهمل أو جزئي؛ لجواز أن يكون ما استثني غير ما وضع مقدماً، وكذا لا إنتاج إن كان الاتصال وفاقياً لا لزومياً، كما إذا قيل (إن) كانت النار حارة فالماء بارد إذ البرودة للماء معلومة وإن قطع النظر عن حرارة النار، فليس يلزم من استثناء عين الحرارة للنار لذاتها أن يكون الماء بارداً، إذ الاتصال بينهما غير حقيقي، بل لو قدر مع الاستحالة أن الماء غير بارد باعتبار طبعه مع فرض وجود حرارة النار، لما كان ذلك محالاً؛ لفرض المقدم بل لمعنى من الخارج، ولو استثني نقيض التالي لم يلزم منه نقيض المقدم، كما لو قلنا لكن الماء ليس ببارد) لم يلزم عنه النار) ليست (حارة فإذن لا بد أن تكون حقيقية، أي أن لزوم التالي عن وضع المقدم عند الوضع، وأن يكون الاستثناء لعين ما وضع مقدماً، وأن يكون وقت فرض وجود المقدم في الاستثناء داخلاً تحت ما حكم فيه بالاتصال في الشرطية، ومثال ذلك عند كون المتصلة موجبة، قولنا : (كل) ما كان ب ج
The first chapter on explaining the productive and sterile. The exceptive [syllogism] is that in which the desired [conclusion] or its contradictory is actually mentioned in it, as you will soon know. Just as the conjunctive syllogism is not completed without two premises, so too the exceptive [syllogism] [requires two premises], but one of its two premises must be conditional and the other exceptive of one of the two parts of the conditional, either its very self or its contradictory. The conditional is either conjunctive or disjunctive. If it is conjunctive, then the exception from it is either of the antecedent or the consequent. If it is of the antecedent, then the excepted [part] is either its very self or its contradictory. If it is its very self, then from it and from the admission of the conditional follows, by an obvious implication, the admission of the very consequent, but on condition that the conjunctive [conditional] is universal or that the time for the connection is specific, and that what has been excepted is exactly what was posited as antecedent, and that the connection is real and essential. So there is no production from that conjunctive [conditional] which is particular or indefinite, as when we say 'Sometimes when Zayd exists, he is a writer or he eats', for it does not follow from our saying that he exists that he is a writer or eats, since that is not necessary for his existence whenever [he exists]. Similarly, there is no production from that whose antecedent is indefinite or particular, because it is possible that what has been excepted is other than what was posited as antecedent. Also, there is no production if the connection is coincidental, not essential, as when it is said 'If fire is hot, then water is cold', for the coldness of water is known even if one disregards the heat of fire. So it does not follow from the exception of the very heat of fire for its own essence that water is cold, because the connection between them is not real. Rather, even if one were to suppose, with impossibility, that water is not cold by virtue of its nature, while assuming the existence of the heat of fire, that would not be impossible due to assuming the antecedent, but due to some external meaning. And if the contradictory of the consequent is excepted, then the contradictory of the antecedent does not follow from it, as if we said 'But water is not cold' does not entail that fire is not hot. Therefore, it is necessary that it be real, meaning that the implication of the consequent from positing the antecedent occurs at the time of positing, and that the exception is of exactly what was posited as antecedent, and that the time of assuming the existence of the antecedent in the exception is included under what was judged concerning the connection in the conditional. An example of that when the conjunctive is affirmative is our saying: 'Everything that is B is J'.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the conditions under which a conditional syllogism (قياس استثنائي) is productive, i.e., yields a certain conclusion. The author begins by defining the exceptive syllogism as one in which the conclusion (مطلوب) or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in one of the premises, unlike the conjunctive syllogism (اقتراني) which requires two premises but neither contains the conclusion directly. The exceptive syllogism also requires two premises: a conditional premise (شرطية) and an exceptive premise (استثنائية) that affirms or denies one of the two parts of the conditional (either the antecedent or the consequent, or their contradictories). The conditional can be conjunctive (متصلة) or disjunctive (مفصلة). For the conjunctive type, the exception can be of the antecedent or the consequent. When the exception is of the antecedent, the excepted part can be the very same antecedent (عين المقدم) or its contradictory. If it is the very same antecedent, then from affirming the antecedent and the conditional, we affirm the consequent. However, this only works under strict conditions: (1) The conjunctive conditional must be universal (كلية) or the time of the connection must be specified. (2) What is excepted must be exactly what was posited as the antecedent. (3) The connection (اتصال) must be real and essential (لزومي حقيقي), not coincidental. The author then gives counterexamples: If the conditional is particular or indefinite (جزئية أو مهملة), e.g., 'Sometimes when Zayd exists, he is a writer or eats' – here existence does not entail writing or eating. If the antecedent is indefinite or particular, it might be that the excepted antecedent is not the same as what was posited. If the connection is coincidental, like 'If fire is hot, water is cold' – heat is not the real cause of water's coldness, so affirming the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent. The author also notes that excepting the contradictory of the consequent does not yield the contradictory of the antecedent (denying the consequent does not deny the antecedent in this context). Finally, the example given is a universal affirmative conjunctive: 'Everything that is B is J' (i.e., whenever B is the case, J is the case). This illustrates the required conditions for a productive conditional syllogism.

Key Terms
الاستثنائي
الشرطية المتصلة
المقدم
التالي

Conditions for Productive Modus Ponens
Conditions for productive modus ponens in conjunctive conditional

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three conditions for a valid modus ponens in a conjunctive conditional syllogism: universality, exact antecedent match, and real essential connection.

Core Idea
Modus ponens is productive only when the conditional is universal, the exception matches exactly, and the implication is real.

This lesson delves into the three conditions that the author explicitly states for a productive modus ponens (affirming the antecedent). Without these conditions, the argument is sterile. We will examine each condition and see why they are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Check universality of the conditional
The conditional must be universal (كليّة) in scope, meaning it applies to all cases. Alternatively, the time of the connection must be specified. If the conditional is particular or indefinite, the inference fails.
Example: 'Sometimes when Zayd exists, he is a writer' is not universal.
2
Verify exact match of the antecedent
The excepted part (the affirmed antecedent) must be exactly the same as the antecedent in the conditional. If the antecedent in the exception is different (e.g., indefinite or mis-specified), the conclusion does not follow.
The potential for mismatch arises if the antecedent is not clearly defined.
3
Confirm the connection is real and essential
The implication (اتصال) must be real (لزومي حقيقي), meaning the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent. If the connection is coincidental (وفاقي), like 'If fire is hot, water is cold', affirming the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent.
The author gives the fire/water example as a case of coincidental connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول في بيان المنتج والعقيم. والاستثنائي هو بالمطلوب أو نقيضه مذكور فيه بالفعل، كما ستعرفه عن قريب وكما أنه لا يتم القياس الاقتراني دون مقدمتين، فكذلك الاستثنائي، لكن إحدى مقدمتيه لا بد وأن تكون شرطية والأخرى استثنائية لأحد جزئي الشرطية أما عينه أو نقيضه والشرطية إما أن تكون متصلة أو مفصلة، فإن كانت متصلة فالاستثناء منها أما بالمقدم أو التالي فإن كان بالمقدم فإما أن يكون المستثنى عينه أو نقيضه، فإن كان عينه لزم عنه وعن تسليم الشرطية لزوماً بيناً تسليم عين التالي، لكن شرط أن تكون المتصلة كليّة أو أن يكون الوقت للاتصال معيناً، وأن يكون ما استثني هو عين ما وضع مقدماً، وأن يكون الاتصال لزومياً حقيقياً، فلا إنتاج عما المتصلة فيه جزئية أو مهملة، كما إذا قلنا قد يكون إذا كان زيد موجوداً فهو كاتب أو يأكل فإنه ليس يلزم من قولنا: إنه موجود أنه كاتب أو يأكل إذ لم يكن ذلك لازماً لوجوده مهما كان، وكذا لا إنتاج عما المقدم فيه مهمل أو جزئي؛ لجواز أن يكون ما استثني غير ما وضع مقدماً، وكذا لا إنتاج إن كان الاتصال وفاقياً لا لزومياً، كما إذا قيل (إن) كانت النار حارة فالماء بارد إذ البرودة للماء معلومة وإن قطع النظر عن حرارة النار، فليس يلزم من استثناء عين الحرارة للنار لذاتها أن يكون الماء بارداً، إذ الاتصال بينهما غير حقيقي، بل لو قدر مع الاستحالة أن الماء غير بارد باعتبار طبعه مع فرض وجود حرارة النار، لما كان ذلك محالاً؛ لفرض المقدم بل لمعنى من الخارج، ولو استثني نقيض التالي لم يلزم منه نقيض المقدم، كما لو قلنا لكن الماء ليس ببارد) لم يلزم عنه النار) ليست (حارة فإذن لا بد أن تكون حقيقية، أي أن لزوم التالي عن وضع المقدم عند الوضع، وأن يكون الاستثناء لعين ما وضع مقدماً، وأن يكون وقت فرض وجود المقدم في الاستثناء داخلاً تحت ما حكم فيه بالاتصال في الشرطية، ومثال ذلك عند كون المتصلة موجبة، قولنا : (كل) ما كان ب ج
The first chapter on explaining the productive and sterile. The exceptive [syllogism] is that in which the desired [conclusion] or its contradictory is actually mentioned in it, as you will soon know. Just as the conjunctive syllogism is not completed without two premises, so too the exceptive [syllogism] [requires two premises], but one of its two premises must be conditional and the other exceptive of one of the two parts of the conditional, either its very self or its contradictory. The conditional is either conjunctive or disjunctive. If it is conjunctive, then the exception from it is either of the antecedent or the consequent. If it is of the antecedent, then the excepted [part] is either its very self or its contradictory. If it is its very self, then from it and from the admission of the conditional follows, by an obvious implication, the admission of the very consequent, but on condition that the conjunctive [conditional] is universal or that the time for the connection is specific, and that what has been excepted is exactly what was posited as antecedent, and that the connection is real and essential. So there is no production from that conjunctive [conditional] which is particular or indefinite, as when we say 'Sometimes when Zayd exists, he is a writer or he eats', for it does not follow from our saying that he exists that he is a writer or eats, since that is not necessary for his existence whenever [he exists]. Similarly, there is no production from that whose antecedent is indefinite or particular, because it is possible that what has been excepted is other than what was posited as antecedent. Also, there is no production if the connection is coincidental, not essential, as when it is said 'If fire is hot, then water is cold', for the coldness of water is known even if one disregards the heat of fire. So it does not follow from the exception of the very heat of fire for its own essence that water is cold, because the connection between them is not real. Rather, even if one were to suppose, with impossibility, that water is not cold by virtue of its nature, while assuming the existence of the heat of fire, that would not be impossible due to assuming the antecedent, but due to some external meaning. And if the contradictory of the consequent is excepted, then the contradictory of the antecedent does not follow from it, as if we said 'But water is not cold' does not entail that fire is not hot. Therefore, it is necessary that it be real, meaning that the implication of the consequent from positing the antecedent occurs at the time of positing, and that the exception is of exactly what was posited as antecedent, and that the time of assuming the existence of the antecedent in the exception is included under what was judged concerning the connection in the conditional. An example of that when the conjunctive is affirmative is our saying: 'Everything that is B is J'.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the conditions under which a conditional syllogism (قياس استثنائي) is productive, i.e., yields a certain conclusion. The author begins by defining the exceptive syllogism as one in which the conclusion (مطلوب) or its contradictory is explicitly mentioned in one of the premises, unlike the conjunctive syllogism (اقتراني) which requires two premises but neither contains the conclusion directly. The exceptive syllogism also requires two premises: a conditional premise (شرطية) and an exceptive premise (استثنائية) that affirms or denies one of the two parts of the conditional (either the antecedent or the consequent, or their contradictories). The conditional can be conjunctive (متصلة) or disjunctive (مفصلة). For the conjunctive type, the exception can be of the antecedent or the consequent. When the exception is of the antecedent, the excepted part can be the very same antecedent (عين المقدم) or its contradictory. If it is the very same antecedent, then from affirming the antecedent and the conditional, we affirm the consequent. However, this only works under strict conditions: (1) The conjunctive conditional must be universal (كلية) or the time of the connection must be specified. (2) What is excepted must be exactly what was posited as the antecedent. (3) The connection (اتصال) must be real and essential (لزومي حقيقي), not coincidental. The author then gives counterexamples: If the conditional is particular or indefinite (جزئية أو مهملة), e.g., 'Sometimes when Zayd exists, he is a writer or eats' – here existence does not entail writing or eating. If the antecedent is indefinite or particular, it might be that the excepted antecedent is not the same as what was posited. If the connection is coincidental, like 'If fire is hot, water is cold' – heat is not the real cause of water's coldness, so affirming the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent. The author also notes that excepting the contradictory of the consequent does not yield the contradictory of the antecedent (denying the consequent does not deny the antecedent in this context). Finally, the example given is a universal affirmative conjunctive: 'Everything that is B is J' (i.e., whenever B is the case, J is the case). This illustrates the required conditions for a productive conditional syllogism.

Key Terms
كلية
وقت الاتصال
لزومي حقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one of the three conditions for a productive modus ponens in a conjunctive conditional syllogism?
A) The conditional must be particular.
B) The connection must be coincidental.
C) The excepted antecedent must be exactly the same as the one in the conditional.
D) The time of the connection must be indefinite.

Key Takeaway
A productive modus ponens requires a universal conditional, exact antecedent match, and a real essential connection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the following argument: 'If a person is a believer, then they perform salah. Ahmad is a believer. Therefore, Ahmad performs salah.' Does this satisfy the three conditions? Which condition might be violated? (Hint: Is the connection real? Many believers may not perform salah due to excuses.)

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Sterility Conditions: Particular, Indefinite, and Coincidental
Examples of sterile conjunctive conditional syllogisms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three scenarios where a conjunctive conditional syllogism is sterile: particular/indefinite conditional, mismatched antecedent, and coincidental connection.

Core Idea
A conditional syllogism fails if the conditional is not universal, the antecedent is not exactly the same, or the implication is coincidental.

This lesson covers the specific cases where a conjunctive conditional syllogism fails to produce a certain conclusion, as illustrated by the author. We examine examples of particular conditionals, indefinite antecedents, and coincidental connections, and learn why they are sterile.

Guided Path
1
Identify a particular or indefinite conditional
If the conditional uses words like 'sometimes' or 'some', it is particular or indefinite. Example: 'Sometimes when Zayd exists, he is a writer.' Affirming the antecedent does not guarantee the consequent.
The author says there is no production (لا إنتاج) from such conditionals.
2
Check for a mismatched antecedent
If the antecedent in the exceptive premise is not exactly the same as in the conditional (e.g., it is indefinite or particular), the syllogism is sterile. It is possible that the excepted antecedent is not the one intended.
This occurs when the antecedent is not clearly specified.
3
Detect a coincidental connection
If the connection between antecedent and consequent is not real (لزومي) but coincidental (وفاقي), affirming the antecedent does not necessitate the consequent. Example: 'If fire is hot, water is cold' – the heat of fire is not the genuine cause of water's coldness.
The author explains that even if one could imagine water not being cold while fire is hot, that impossibility is not due to the antecedent but to an external factor.
4
Understand why denying the consequent does not work
The passage also notes that excepting the contradictory of the consequent (i.e., denying the consequent) does not yield the contradictory of the antecedent. This is a specific characteristic of the coincidental connection example.
In a valid modus tollens, denying the consequent should deny the antecedent, but here it fails because the connection is not real.

Source Text

الفصل الأول في بيان المنتج والعقيم. والاستثنائي هو بالمطلوب أو نقيضه مذكور فيه بالفعل، كما ستعرفه عن قريب وكما أنه لا يتم القياس الاقتراني دون مقدمتين، فكذلك الاستثنائي، لكن إحدى مقدمتيه لا بد وأن تكون شرطية والأخرى استثنائية لأحد جزئي الشرطية أما عينه أو نقيضه والشرطية إما أن تكون متصلة أو مفصلة، فإن كانت متصلة فالاستثناء منها أما بالمقدم أو التالي فإن كان بالمقدم فإما أن يكون المستثنى عينه أو نقيضه، فإن كان عينه لزم عنه وعن تسليم الشرطية لزوماً بيناً تسليم عين التالي، لكن شرط أن تكون المتصلة كليّة أو أن يكون الوقت للاتصال معيناً، وأن يكون ما استثني هو عين ما وضع مقدماً، وأن يكون الاتصال لزومياً حقيقياً، فلا إنتاج عما المتصلة فيه جزئية أو مهملة، كما إذا قلنا قد يكون إذا كان زيد موجوداً فهو كاتب أو يأكل فإنه ليس يلزم من قولنا: إنه موجود أنه كاتب أو يأكل إذ لم يكن ذلك لازماً لوجوده مهما كان، وكذا لا إنتاج عما المقدم فيه مهمل أو جزئي؛ لجواز أن يكون ما استثني غير ما وضع مقدماً، وكذا لا إنتاج إن كان الاتصال وفاقياً لا لزومياً، كما إذا قيل (إن) كانت النار حارة فالماء بارد إذ البرودة للماء معلومة وإن قطع النظر عن حرارة النار، فليس يلزم من استثناء عين الحرارة للنار لذاتها أن يكون الماء بارداً، إذ الاتصال بينهما غير حقيقي، بل لو قدر مع الاستحالة أن الماء غير بارد باعتبار طبعه مع فرض وجود حرارة النار، لما كان ذلك محالاً؛ لفرض المقدم بل لمعنى من الخارج، ولو استثني نقيض التالي لم يلزم منه نقيض المقدم، كما لو قلنا لكن الماء ليس ببارد) لم يلزم عنه النار) ليست (حارة فإذن لا بد أن تكون حقيقية، أي أن لزوم التالي عن وضع المقدم عند الوضع، وأن يكون الاستثناء لعين ما وضع مقدماً، وأن يكون وقت فرض وجود المقدم في الاستثناء داخلاً تحت ما حكم فيه بالاتصال في الشرطية، ومثال ذلك عند كون المتصلة موجبة، قولنا : (كل) ما كان ب ج

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a coincidental connection (وفاقي) as described in the passage?
A) If Zayd is a human, then he is mortal.
B) If it rains, then the ground is wet.
C) If fire is hot, then water is cold.
D) If a person performs wudu, then their prayer is valid.

Key Takeaway
A conjunctive conditional syllogism is sterile if the conditional is particular/indefinite, the antecedent is mismatched, or the connection is coincidental.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the argument: 'Sometimes if a student studies, they pass the exam. Ahmad studied. Therefore, Ahmad passed the exam.' Identify which sterility condition applies and explain why the conclusion does not follow.
